# 儒门崇理折衷堪舆完孝录

**The *Rumen Chongli Zhezhong Kanyu Wanxiao Lu*:**

**Scripture Title:** *Rumen Chongli Zhezhong Kanyu Wanxiao Lu* (*The Confucian School’s Veneration of Principle: A Balanced Record of Geomancy and Filial Piety*).
**Author:** Unknown.
**Eight fascicles.**
**Source text:** *Seal-character section* of the *Wanli Continuation of the Daoist Canon*.

**Preface to the *Wanxiao Lu*:**

1. On geomantic landforms (*luan tou*), works such as the *Xuexin Fu* and the *Burial Classic* have already expounded in full. On stellar *qi*, texts like the *Cuiguan Pian* and the *Yuchi Jing* have likewise covered the subject exhaustively. What further need is there for repetition? The entries in this compilation address only what the ancients left unsaid or treated without sufficient detail.

2. All hackneyed doctrines are omitted. However, certain discourses—such as those of the Xiong clan of Guang□, the Li clan of Rao’an, the Ji clan of Kuocang, the Zhou clan of Taihe, and the Wu clan of Linchuan—though grounded in sound principle, have yet to circulate widely. These are thus included in their entirety.

3. On the interplay of form and *qi*, the maxims of earlier worthies may serve as reference. Yet the art of determining fate through the Seven Governors (*qi zheng*) by year and month remains rarely transmitted. For this reason, the present work treats form and *qi* only briefly while elaborating on the Seven Governors. Discussions of form and *qi* retain only contemporary texts, whereas those on the Seven Governors preserve both ancient and modern sources.

4. Heavenly tally marks and the Purple-White system (*tianfu zibai*) elucidate the mechanisms of the Five Movements and Six *Qi*, unveiling the mysteries of the *Luoshu* and *Hetu*. As their principles correspond internally and externally to the Seven Governors, and since such knowledge is seldom transmitted, they are likewise preserved here.

5. The heart of the benevolent and filial knows no ulterior motive in its actions. Yet this compilation frequently speaks of fortune and misfortune, of blessings and calamities—does this not risk misleading the world? Nevertheless, for those of utmost filial devotion who remain undaunted by worldly concerns, these accounts of verification serve to move them. Needless to say, outcomes may not always align with predictions.

6. Geomancy is fundamentally a static art. The ancients, however, often sought dynamism within stillness, hence their frequent reliance on transformation (*bian*). Yet transformation is a matter of expediency (*quan*), and expediency can only be wielded by sages. When mediocre practitioners misuse it, success becomes a rare exception. This work, therefore, confines itself to expounding the constant. As for transformations that verge on the bizarre or arcane, these are excluded entirely.

**Volume One of *The Ruist School’s Veneration of Principle: A Synthesis of Geomancy and Filial Piety***

**Chapter One: On the Orthodox School of Yin and Yang**

Since the existence of Heaven and Earth, yin and yang have been divided, the supreme principle flowing between the two, primordial qi circulating through the ages. In profound obscurity, the wondrous opening and closing of the infinite unfolds; in primal chaos, the divine transformations remain unfathomable. At times, it unfolds as the alternation of the two qi; at others, it spreads as the mutual generation and restriction of the Five Phases. In waxing and waning, the marvelous work of the mysterious is complete; in fullness and emptiness, nothing lies beyond the workings of creation.

Though the spirits of Heaven and Earth remain unknowable, the patterns of their alternation may yet be examined. The sun and moon, the stars—what shines above follows fixed degrees. Mountains and rivers, earth and stone—what congeals below assumes fixed forms. By deducing these degrees, one may discern the auspiciousness or inauspiciousness of years, months, days, and hours; by examining these forms, one may assess the fortune or misfortune of dragon veins, burial sites, surrounding hills, and flowing waters.

The Dao encompasses the Three Ultimates, and principle admits no duality. Only those who unite the Three Powers may grasp the Mandate of Heaven and aid the work of the spirits. As for those rigidly clinging to one-sided views, how could they shoulder great responsibilities? Their lofty discourse and grand strides resemble Yang and Zeng in name only; their sketched diagrams and painted charts offer false comfort, mere trifles. If asked about astronomy or geography, where is their true insight?

To speak of grand matters would be futile, for they fixate on trivial flaws, oblivious to the whole, and cling to partial views. Have they not obscured the distinctions of mountains and rivers in their orientations? They claim to determine fortune and misfortune by direction alone, yet they stray from the sagely canon of seasonal ordinances and further muddle the reckoning of days and hours with杂说. Moreover, the proliferation of spurious texts and the clamor of absurd theories defy the immutable truth: "Heaven has no two suns," an axiom from antiquity to the present.

Those who discourse on the Great Yang speak of calendrical calculations, the Crow and Hare, the Dipper’s handle, and the like, positing seven discordant positions for the sun. Yet the people have no two kings, as the sages have declared. Those who discourse on the Imperial Star speak of the August Emperor, the Celestial Sovereign, the Mouth of the Dipper, and the like, introducing thirteen disparate Imperial Stars.

Those who disrupt yin and yang devise distinctions such as "contending yin," "contending yang," "divided yin," "divided yang," "true yin," "true yang," "prior Heaven," and "posterior Heaven," unaware that the yin and yang of the Supreme Ultimate admit no duality. Those who rearrange the Five Phases invent categories like "yin mansion," "Great Plan," "Eight Trigrams," "Double Mountains," "Mysterious Void," "Tonal Resonance," "Celestial Sphere," and "Stellar Lodges," oblivious that the Five Phases of generation and completion admit no bifurcation.

Such examples are too numerous to enumerate, yet these practices pervade the world. Thus, doubt and belief intertwine, leaving people for generations unable to discern the suitability of burial sites, delaying interment for decades. Only then do they realize that false arts are unworthy of trust; only the sincerity of one’s heart is true. If the master of one’s heart is truly virtuous, people will naturally excel, and the land will naturally be efficacious. Should thoughts and intentions falter even slightly, the land will not shelter, nor will the spirits protect.

Otherwise, why would the head of the dragon vein bring strict calamities within ten days, while the Ox-Sleeping Mountain elevates one to the rank of the Three Dukes? The mechanisms of rise and fall, success and failure, are all decreed by Heaven. Thus, schemes born of greed and deceit are futile, merely exhausting the heart. Some desecrate their own ancestors’ graves, no better than carving flesh to sate hunger; others stealthily bury their dead in others’ burial grounds, no different from stealing a bell while covering one’s ears.

I exhort the world: nothing compares to upholding integrity and cultivating the heart-ground, for this is the root of attaining the land. Following Heaven’s principle is the foundation of geomancy, and loyalty to a true and enlightened master precludes falling for illusory techniques. In assessing landforms, prioritize the Five Stars and mountain contours, while trigrams and celestial patterns are of little consequence. In selecting auspicious dates, emphasize the Seven Governors’ essential principles, while the miscellaneous doctrines of a hundred schools are unworthy of reliance. These are the orthodox principles of yin and yang, through which one may fathom the mysterious workings of creation.

Assessing landforms through the Five Stars and the Four Phrases constitutes the grand essence. Chapter Two: On the Flow and Obstruction of Qi and Fortune

Dragon veins, burial sites, encircling hills, and watercourses pertain to form. Flourishing and decline, waxing and waning pertain to qi. If the form is fundamentally auspicious yet the qi has not arrived, there is no principle by which blessings may be prematurely bestowed—witness how Mount Ni produced Confucius, and Mount Song produced Shen [Shun], for were not these landscapes the same in ancient times? Yet because the qi had not yet arrived, even the workings of creation could not activate their potential ahead of time. If the form is exceedingly auspicious yet the qi has already dissipated, there is likewise no principle by which blessings may be renewed—witness how the Feng clan produced Shun, and the Zhou of Qi produced King Wen, for did the landscapes of later ages differ from those of old? Yet because the qi had already been exhausted, even the workings of creation could not reactivate their potential.

The qi of Heaven and Earth flows without stagnation: ancient Luoyang is not what it once was, while the present capital surpasses its former glory. Though the same mountains and rivers are at work, the timing and duration of blessings vary—whether early or late, near or distant—solely due to the arrival or non-arrival, exhaustion or non-exhaustion of qi. How could one rigidly adhere to inquiries about dragon veins, burial sites, hills, and watercourses?

Yet the mechanisms of flourishing and decline invariably manifest premonitions. Thus, when the Yellow River and Luo River reveal auspicious signs, they foretell the predictions of the *Gua Chou*; when qilin appear in the suburbs, they herald the advent of royal governance; when the Yellow River runs clear, one knows a sage-king will ascend the throne; when the Five Stars converge, they portend the simultaneous rise of worthy scholars. All these relate to the flourishing of the realm and the state. Thus, the quintessential qi of Heaven and Earth manifests as auspicious omens in such ways.

When the qi of fortune is about to wane, monstrosities and calamities proliferate, and inauspicious qi gathers. This qi, vast enough to encompass all, is also subtle enough to permeate the smallest spaces. Even in bustling markets or the sites of residences and tombs, one may speak of flourishing and decline. Whenever entering a region, ascending a residence or tomb, one observes: if birds and beasts thrive, grasses and trees flourish, the climate is mild and warm, valleys radiate brilliance, the earth is deep and fertile, and the scenery is clear and extraordinary—then at dawn, dusk, or midnight, one may witness auspicious lights appearing, pure qi intermingling, and faintly hear the distant sound of bells and drums. The elderly lean on canes, the strong stand tall, children are orderly and bright, and all people live in harmony, courteous and deferential. All these are signs of flourishing qi, and great blessings will surely follow.

But if, upon entering a region or ascending a residence or tomb, one sees grasses and trees withered, birds and beasts scattered, the wind mournful, the atmosphere desolate, mountains and rivers broken and barren, valleys cold and dark—then at dawn, dusk, or midnight, one finds no radiance, only stagnant qi rising, and an eerie silence as if entering an uninhabited land. Households lack elders with gray hair, the young and strong are weak, internal strife arises, and courtyards teem with discord. All these are signs of declining qi, and misfortune will swiftly follow.

There are also those whose homes emit a pale, mournful qi, which invariably foretells mourning attire. If a murky, stagnant black qi arises, it portends epidemics. If a scorching, fiery red qi appears, it foretells fires, legal troubles, or similar disasters. The growth of moss or white filaments, or hens crowing, are all caused by inauspicious qi. Some tombs collapse without cause, their trees uprooted by wind, or struck by lightning—such families will surely decline. If the soil of a tomb is parched, cracked, and exposed, this indicates excessive fire qi, which will attract insects. If only green mugwort grows on a tomb, or white grasses or trees with white leaves appear, this indicates excessive water qi, which will bring forth springs.

Mountains that are large but have small watercourses and lack dragon veins will breed white ants. Mountains that are small but have large watercourses and lack dragon veins will produce springs. Even if the incoming dragon vein is authentic, if the burial site lies in a place devoid of qi, the same misfortunes will follow. If grasses and trees grow all around a tomb yet none grow upon it, white ants will fill the coffin. If only green moss grows on the tomb while nothing grows around it, the burial site will overflow with water. If the site contains sand or stone, water will inevitably seep in. If the site is burdened by killing qi, white ants will also appear. Burying in a hollow will cause water to seep in; burying in hard, unyielding ground will attract ants. These are all inevitable principles, all signs of declining qi.

This outlines the general patterns of flourishing and declining qi. With careful observation, one may delve ever deeper into their subtleties. Today, when people see auspicious omens like lingzhi mushrooms, who does not revere them? Yet distinctions must be made. If a lingzhi’s cap barely emerges, resembling a treasure canopy’s tip peeking out, or if it seems to hum like bees making honey—faintly audible yet silent—this marks the initial flourishing of qi. If the cap is exposed, then the stem appears; if the tip is revealed, then the eaves emerge; if a sound seemed present, now it truly is. Whether red, yellow, crimson, or multicolored in intricate patterns, this is the qi of full flourishing.

When a lingzhi’s stem is exposed beneath its cap, or a treasure canopy’s handle appears beneath its eaves; when its luster fills the eyes or its hum fills the ears—this is the qi of utmost flourishing. Beyond this point, flourishing reaches its peak and begins to wane. If a lingzhi’s stem grows long, or a treasure canopy’s handle rises high; if the buzzing of mosquitoes recedes into the distance, or the rumble of distant thunder ascends from low to high; if auspicious clouds form five-colored patterns that are vibrant outside yet pale within—these are signs of qi about to decline.

When a lingzhi’s stem slants and drifts without touching the earth, or a treasure canopy’s handle rises high yet flutters without support; when the buzzing of mosquitoes recedes into the far distance, or the rumble of faint thunder passes too high; when five-colored patterns scatter and gather unpredictably—these are the qi of true decline. Should the lingzhi or treasure canopy rot and decay; should the once-lofty sound now fall silent; should the distant become remote and the once-vibrant hues suddenly fade; should they drift like fading stars about to disperse or flutter like withered leaves in the wind—these are the qi of utter decline.

For the wise, discernment lies solely in this.

**Chapter Three: On the Nobility and Baseness of Qi and Color**

Master Bu said: "Assessing land is like assessing a person." This is an apt analogy for the method of land inspection. I observe that those who practice physiognomy often judge a person’s nobility or baseness by their form and determine their prosperity or adversity by their qi and color. Only when both are thoroughly examined can the art of physiognomy reveal the truth without deception. Yet why do those who assess land today focus solely on form and neglect qi and color, failing to achieve the same accuracy as in human physiognomy?

Master Fan said: "The ancestral dragon’s high peaks and grand halls are often adorned with clouds of qi." Master Yang said: "Gazing at qi to trace the dragon is easy." Thus, the ancients in assessing land never neglected qi. Why do modern practitioners abandon it entirely?

Yet qi that appears in faint glimmers is especially difficult to discern. Only at dawn, when the clear qi has not yet dispersed; at dusk, when the proper qi is just withdrawing; during the moments when rain has just cleared from both sides; or at the dawn of the Mid-Autumn Festival—at these times, faint traces become visible, and indistinct forms can be observed. Particularly at midnight, recognition is most precise.

All green and red hues belong to yang qi. White and black belong to yin qi. Yellow that is moist and lustrous is the qi of harmonious yin and yang. By observing the height and demeanor of qi, one can divine the length of the dragon vein where it settles; by noting its breadth and scale, one can foretell the abundance of surrounding mountains and waters. If the qi rises straight and upright, the true site must lie in the main dragon vein; if it drifts and slants, the true site must be a branch.

The qi of the main vein is naturally grand and majestic, while that of branches is humble and weak. Mountains that are robust and vigorous possess flourishing qi; those that are soft and frail have faint qi. Thus it is said: "For every ten *li* a dragon travels, its qi rises a *zhang*."

Wealthy graves emit qi like stacked layers, rich in color yet murky. Noble graves emit qi like an unfurled canopy, clear and extraordinary. When it first emerges as a single thread, then forms a floral canopy—lush and verdant, like clouds, like mist, like a banner or imperial regalia draped in yellow, adorned with all five colors—if it coils like a dragon, it is called *wang qi*, the land of emperors. For emperors embody the union of yin and yang, the essence of the five qi, and earth governs rulership; its color is fundamentally yellow, and the dragon symbolizes the sovereign, hence the presence of all five colors, with yellow predominating and resembling the dragon.

When red light shines brilliantly, dense and towering like a forest, like a rainbow, like the moon, like a phoenix in dance, it is called *shu qi*, the land of empresses and consorts. For empresses and consorts are nearly equal to the sovereign in status, and fire aids earth, while the phoenix is the sovereign’s auspicious omen—thus red predominates and resembles the phoenix.

If the qi is white within and yellow without, green in front and purple behind, green like an ox’s head, red like a tiger’s tail, it is the fierce qi of generals and marquises. When it begins like mist and ends like boiling cauldrons, green within and red without, like a shooting star illuminating the earth, like a dragon soaring or a phoenix ascending, it is the *fu xi qi*, the land of good fortune and prime ministers.

A single thread of red and white piercing the sun is the loyal and upright qi of remonstrating ministers. A single thread of red, like a rainbow with faint colors, is the extraordinary literary qi of top scholars. Pure white or pure yellow indicates the land of local officials. Red, yellow, purple, and white intermingled with green and moistness belong to the finest scholars of the imperial academy. Red, yellow, black, and white intermingled with thickness and weight belong to the heroes of the imperial cavalry.

When qi first emerges as a single thread and finally forms a treasure canopy, with all five colors present, light and floating upward, patterned yet uncluttered—like treading in rhythm, like a pagoda, like a waving banner, like a standard or pennant gleaming in the sun and illuminating the stars—it is *yi qi*, the land of immortals. This qi often appears in Flying Goose Dragons and Iron Crutch Dragons.

When all five colors are present, upright and unleaning, like a great minister standing in court, it is *zheng qi*, the land of Daoist scholars. This qi often appears in Upright Dragons and Recluse Dragons.

Generally, qi that is green or red and bright belongs to civil officials; white or black and direct belongs to military officers. Qi that is green, red, white, or black with a thread of yellow and moistness belongs to those accomplished in both civil and military arts. Qi resembling birds produces scholars; qi resembling beasts produces warriors. Qi that is vast and illuminates the sky must yield ministers and generals; qi that is low and small yet luminous also produces civil officials with military readiness.

To know the rank of an official, observe the height and breadth of the qi. To know the duration of an official’s tenure, examine the size and thickness of the qi. To determine the direction of official position and the fate of those it favors, consult the celestial divisions and cardinal directions. If both the life palace and burial site lie in *zi* and the auspicious qi aligns with the *Xuanleng* constellation, official positions will often be in Song, benefiting those born in *hai*, *mao*, and *wei*. If both lie in *chou* and the auspicious qi aligns with the *Xingji* constellation, official positions will often be in Zheng, benefiting those born in *shen*, *zi*, and *chen*. If both lie in *yin* and the auspicious qi aligns with the *Ximu* constellation, official positions will often be in Chu, benefiting those born in *si*, *you*, and *chou*.

If both the life palace and burial site lie in *mao* and the auspicious qi aligns with the *Dahuo* constellation, official positions will often be in Zhou, benefiting those born in *yin*, *wu*, and *xu*. If both lie in *chen* and the auspicious qi aligns with the *Shouxing* constellation, official positions will often be in Qin, benefiting those born in *hai*, *mao*, and *wei*. If both lie in *si* and the auspicious qi aligns with the *Chunwei* constellation, official positions will often be in Jin, benefiting those born in *shen*, *zi*, and *chen*. If both lie in *wu* and the auspicious qi aligns with the *Chunhuo* constellation, official positions will often be in Zhao, benefiting those born in *si*, *you*, and *chou*. If both lie in *wei* and the auspicious qi aligns with the *Chunshou* constellation, official positions will often be in Lu, benefiting those born in *yin*, *wu*, and *xu*. If both lie in *shen* and the auspicious qi aligns with the *Shichen* constellation, official positions will often be in Wei, benefiting those born in *hai*, *mao*, and *wei*. If both lie in *you* and the auspicious qi aligns with the *Daliang* constellation, official positions will often be in Qi, benefiting those born in *shen*, *zi*, and *chen*. If both lie in *xu* and the auspicious qi aligns with the *Jianglou* constellation, official positions will often be in Wu, benefiting those born in *si*, *you*, and *chou*. If both lie in *hai* and the auspicious qi aligns with the *Juzi* constellation, official positions will often be in Yan, benefiting those born in *yin*, *wu*, and *xu*.

The rise and fall of official positions have no fixed direction, and thus the rotation of auspicious qi likewise has no fixed location. For instance, if the life palace and mountain lie in *zi* while the qi of official nobility appears in *shen*, official positions will often be in Jin. If it sometimes shifts to *chen*, official positions will then be in Zheng. If it further shifts to *wu*, official positions will move to Zhou. Generally, when qi ascends from low to high, from subtle to manifest, it signifies promotion. If it gradually declines and weakens, it foretells demotion. If it transforms into nothingness, it portends dismissal. Moreover, if qi drifts and enters the mountain of the life palace, it signifies retirement and return to one’s hometown. Such variations cannot be fully described; only those of exceptional wisdom and insight can fathom their profound subtleties.

**Chapter Four: On the Authenticity of Qi and Its Colors**

The qi of the earth may be authentic or counterfeit. The counterfeit resembles the authentic, while the authentic may appear counterfeit. In places of great fortune, the qi is extraordinary—steady as a precious canopy, lush as sacred lingzhi. From afar, it casts a visible shadow; up close, it has no tangible form. It may appear red, yellow, purple, crimson, or white with a moist sheen, or black with a luminous glow. It may blend five colors or manifest myriad images—like brocade in the sky or patterns of five hues. These are the auspicious qi of creation concentrated in the earth, their essence and radiance uniquely revealed.

In contrast, some qi appears white or blue, scattered and diffuse, its form distinct yet its essence shallow—likely formed by the interplay of sunlight and water vapor. There are also the dawn qi at sunrise, the rosy qi at sunset, the radiant qi of the moon’s rise and fall, the cloud qi after rain, the damp qi of ancient mountains, the mist qi of rivers and marshes, the luster of pearls in hidden pools, the brilliance of jade within mountains, the floating glow of old cellars, the shimmer of ancient tombs, the converging rays of Yanping that shoot toward the *Douniu* constellation, the blade-like gleam of melon fields bathed in moonlight, the glow of withered plants and aged trees, the fire-like radiance of ancient vessels. Human blood spilled in the wild for long periods emits light at dusk, while the blood of birds and beasts, when accumulated, may also produce flames. The breath of demonic serpents resembles clouds, that of demonic pheasants resembles brocade, and that of demonic tortoises resembles rosy clouds. The breath of clams often forms structures like towers and pavilions. Other phenomena—such as spirit fire, ghost fire, earth lamps, and altar lamps—are too numerous to list, all being counterfeit qi that mimics the authentic.

Generally, authentic qi is broad above and narrow below, while counterfeit qi is narrow above and broad below. Authentic qi is faint outside and dense within; counterfeit qi is dense outside and faint within. Authentic qi is dim outside and bright within; counterfeit qi is bright outside and dim within. Authentic qi possesses both qi and light; counterfeit qi has light but no qi. Authentic qi emerges from mountain peaks; counterfeit qi lingers along mountain slopes. Authentic qi often gathers in bright, open places; counterfeit qi often appears in secluded valleys. Authentic qi frequently manifests where mountains converge; counterfeit qi often arises in solitary yin or yang locations. Without precise discernment, one may mistake a magpie for a phoenix, a crow for a simurgh, or a lizard for a dragon.

As for counterfeit qi, we shall not discuss it further. Regarding authentic qi, there are distinctions between the qi of dragons, burial sites, sand, and water. The qi of ancestral dragons is invariably radiant, illuminating the heavens. The qi within burial sites must reveal a singular, concentrated brilliance. The qi of sand serves to assist the dragon and burial site. Thus, when observing the qi above sand, one must prioritize the qi of the dragon and burial site; one cannot assume that a site is auspicious merely because the qi above the sand is strong. For example, if the dragon and burial site lie in the east and the qi above the sand appears white or red, or if they lie in the south and the qi above the sand appears black or yellow, these are destructive or draining qi—encountering them portends misfortune. Only when the original qi of the dragon and burial site is exceptionally strong can harm be averted. Conversely, if the dragon and burial site lie in the east and the qi above the sand appears black and moist, or if they lie in the south and the qi above the sand appears green and moist, these are highly auspicious signs. Even if the original qi of the dragon and burial site is extremely weak, no disaster will occur.

Generally, destructive or draining qi that falls into a debilitated position brings minor misfortune, while supportive or reinforcing qi that falls into a debilitated position brings only slight fortune. For instance, fire in the north or earth in the east are examples of debilitated positions.

The qi of water shares the same characteristics as the qi of sand.

**Chapter Five: On the Generation and Restriction of the Five Qi**

The qi of spring is green; the qi of summer is red. The pure autumn is white and moist; black resides in winter. The yellow qi flows through the four seasons—all distinct in their nature.

When green qi dominates in fragrant spring, even if white qi appears, it brings no harm. When crimson radiance blazes in summer’s sun, though black qi may arise, there is no need for concern. White in its flourishing need not fear red’s approach; when black prevails, why worry over yellow? Even when earth of the summer season encounters wood, it does not invite disaster. The flourishing yellow qi, meeting green, is not overcome. Observing closely, one sees that when qi is abundant, it fears no restriction; thus, when qi is weak, it naturally cannot generate. The earth of the three springs struggles to aid metal; the white of the nine summers cannot nourish water. Winter’s fire cannot rescue the earth of the late season; autumn’s wood pays no heed to the scorching sovereign. In the four seasons, when water is weak and green-yellow prevails, how can the light, drifting green qi of the east be relied upon, for it despises the white dew? Yet if threads of crimson and purple appear, one may know that a banquet in the Jade Forest will be granted. When tender white qi drifts in the west, it dislikes the arrival of scorching heat. But if faint black qi emerges, success on the battlefield is assured. When faint red fears black and encounters the yellow matchmaker, achievement is imminent. When water is scant and dreads earth, if green and yellow appear, then career success is certain. Though yellow fears wood, white can resolve it; if earth stands alone with green, it may cause ailments of sinew and bone. Single red foretells prison calamity; heavy yellow brings pestilence; solitary white heralds death; a comet in the north, regardless of color, portends unforeseen disaster. There is no need to doubt this—all such signs arise when green, red, yellow, and white lack moisture, and black appears damp, stagnant, and dull. If the qi is balanced and harmonious, what harm can come?

When green qi scatters and loses its proper form, it often gives rise to shamans and sorcerers. When red light pierces sharply with strange anomalies, it surely produces litigious advocates. When white qi emerges chaotically and drifts in disarray, the world transmits tales of cruelty and malice. When black light slants and appears askew, generations suffer from arrow wounds. When yellow appears dim and lightless, it foretells the birth of the blind, deaf, mute, or dumb. When green is disordered, there will be many changes; when black is disordered, there will be much licentiousness; when red is disordered, literary talent fails to blossom; when white is disordered, weapons and warfare will surely arise. Moreover, when yellow qi is disordered, illness follows. Though red and yellow appearing together are generally auspicious, if chaotic red emerges in the west, it will bring blood diseases; if chaotic yellow arises in the north, it will surely cause depletion of vitality. Though green and black may seem inauspicious, if lustrous green appears in the south, its brilliance will only grow; if tender black emerges in the east, it will bring delightful vitality. For within misfortune, fortune may hide, and within fortune, misfortune may lurk—all is the natural mechanism, an immutable principle. When qi is excessively strong, it breeds violence and ferocity; when it is too weak, it leads to loneliness and hardship. Those at the peak of abundance will swiftly meet their end; those at the extreme of weakness will ultimately face extinction. Though the duration may differ, the outcome is the same.

**Chapter Six: On the Mechanism of Heaven and Earth’s Qi**

The preceding chapters discussed qi; the following will address form. In the art of fengshui, one must first recognize the mechanism of qi—without understanding the mechanism of qi, what is fengshui? Qi connects with the heavens and reaches the earth; though it communicates with heaven and penetrates the earth, the two are distinct. Contemporary masters briefly illustrate a few points to instruct our descendants.

Qi may advance and achieve; the mechanism may also advance and achieve. Advancement is auspicious; achievement is even more auspicious. Qi may retreat and decline; the mechanism may also retreat and decline. Retreat is inauspicious; decline is even more inauspicious. Treading upon the dragon, ascending the site, embracing the sand and water, and examining the mechanism of qi—if it advances or achieves, it is a noble land, ensuring honor and prosperity; if it is a rich land, it will lose its flourishing. Even in humble and lowly places, all will be well. If it retreats or declines, it is a poor dragon, foretelling exhaustion; if it is a base dragon, it foretells illness and suffering. Even in wealthy and noble lands, all will come to ruin.

Spring belongs to wood; wood’s qi is green, and wood’s mechanism is ascension. Summer belongs to fire; fire’s qi is red, and fire’s mechanism is blazing. Autumn belongs to metal; metal’s qi is white, and metal’s mechanism is melting. Winter belongs to water; water’s qi is black, and water’s mechanism is sinking. The four seasons belong to earth; earth’s qi is yellow, and earth’s mechanism is congealing.

Gently drifting, misty and pervasive, swaying like flying threads, rustling with a resonant sound—this is the qi of wood. The cry of a crow, the bark of a dog, a green-robed boy carrying a pot and flask—this is the mechanism of wood. In all that is seen or encountered, if it carries the sentiment of movement, the appearance of joy, or the posture of rising, it belongs to wood and is fitting for spring.

Green haze and red light, flying blossoms gathering before the eyes, flickering and flashing, fierce and roaring—this is the qi of fire. The barking of dogs, the crowing of roosters, the shouts of wives and children, the songs and leaps of shepherds and woodcutters—this is the mechanism of fire. In all that is seen or encountered, if it carries the sentiment of surging, the appearance of rage, or the posture of thunderous intensity, it belongs to fire and is fitting for summer.

Clear and white, the color of wind drifting, heard as if sounding yet unseen—this is the qi of metal. Singing while walking, voices responding to voices, driving oxen and herding horses, speaking slowly and moving leisurely—this is the mechanism of metal. In all that is seen or heard, if it carries the sentiment of contemplation, the appearance of weariness, or the posture of satiety, it belongs to metal and is fitting for autumn.

Tranquil and still, without dust or disturbance, unmoving and unshaken, yet not desolate—this is the qi of water. Walking shoulder to shoulder, sitting knee to knee, conversing hand in hand, birds flying without a sound—this is the mechanism of water. In all that is seen or encountered, if it carries the sentiment of sorrow, the appearance of melancholy, or the posture of restraint, it belongs to water and is fitting for winter.

Dust filling the eyes, yellow sand drifting faintly, all sounds silenced, the wind mild and cool—this is the qi of earth. Adjusting one’s clothes and tying one’s shoes, standing upright in refined conversation, shouldering burdens, birds roosting, and beasts returning—this is the mechanism of earth.

In all that is seen or encountered, if it carries an air of solemn dignity, the bearing of bearing burdens, or the posture of deep composure, it belongs to earth and is fitting for all four seasons.

In spring, the mechanism of fire qi brings advancement; wood qi brings fulfillment; water qi brings retreat; and metal qi brings decline. From one, the rest may be known. The earth of the four seasons likewise corresponds to the four times, and by its qi one may judge a man’s descendants and official rank, while by its mechanism one may determine ancestral estates and wealth.

When qi is mixed, a single household may contain several surnames. When qi advances, descendants achieve renown. When qi fulfills, sons are added and ranks promoted. When qi retreats, officials retire and sons fall ill. When qi declines, officials meet disaster and sons die.

When the mechanism is inauspicious, the yang dwelling brings licentious elopement, and the yin tomb likewise brings licentious elopement. When the mechanism advances, production increases and wealth arrives. When it fulfills, granaries are full and storehouses overflow. When it retreats, the household dwindles and empties. When it declines, fields are sold and debts incurred.

Examine the time of the qi mechanism: if it belongs to *yin*, it corresponds to the life of *yin-hai*. If it belongs to *si*, it corresponds to the life of *si-shen*. The rest may be inferred by analogy to determine fortune and predict lifespans.

In the month of *lu-yin*, judge misfortune and predict the time of life’s punishments and calamities—this method is infallible.

In spring, when climbing a mountain, if one sees a rider on horseback: a tomb with stone masonry indicates a family with disorders of the diaphragm or feet; a tomb overgrown with weeds indicates a household of complete wealth and nobility.

In summer, when entering a village and hearing swallows’ calls: if inquiring about illness, one may judge that death will come in autumn; if inquiring about official rank, one may judge that success will come in autumn.

In autumn, when climbing a mountain and seeing someone carrying wine: a tomb with a stele indicates wealth without accumulation; a tomb without a stele indicates frugality with wealth.

In winter, when climbing a mountain and hearing crows: a male tomb indicates that a son will rise to the rank of imperial censor; a female tomb indicates a poor household with filial sons.

At noon, when visiting a tomb and observing the sunlight: if it shines on the west, that direction will prosper; if on the east, that direction will decline.

At noon, when entering a hall and seeing vegetable sellers: it indicates that men and women will lack descendants and lose their spouses in middle age.

All these are judged by the qi mechanism.

---

**Chapter Seven: On Dragon Lairs, Sand, and Water**

When discussing dragons, one must speak of the "coming dragon." If the dragon’s arrival is not seen, how can one take it as the basis? When discussing lairs, one must speak of "lair sentiment." If the lair’s sentiment is not examined, what can serve as evidence?

Though the branches and trunks of dragons differ, their arrival always displays soaring, hidden leaps, or submerged movements. If even one is lacking, the dragon is not true. Though the forms of lairs vary, their settling always involves sentiment, posture, and qi veins. If even one fails to gather, the lair is not correct.

Where there is a true dragon and a correct lair, auspicious sand remains auspicious, while inauspicious sand will be subdued. It is like a well-ordered court, where even treacherous heroes become pure and good. Water that comes will come, and that which goes will also be put to use. It is like a body free from major illness, where minor ailments are of no concern.

If there is not a single true dragon, its arrival will not endure, and those buried there will be solitary, poor, and destitute. If there is no true lair, its core will be clumsy, and those buried there will suffer from poverty or even extinction. All interactions—whether generating or overcoming—pertain to sand, and all should serve me. Children, fathers, wealth, and officials should all embrace me gently. What embraces me is auspicious; what opposes me is disastrous. Coming and going alike pertain to water, and all should flow leisurely. *Gen*, *bing*, *xun*, and *xin* should all converge gently. Metal, water, and earth are excellent; wood and fire are ominous. Enclosure and pursuit are not sand; crabs and shrimp are not water. One principle determines the dragon; one principle verifies the lair.

Dragons have regular and transformed states; lairs have additions and subtractions. When metal transforms like wood, fire dissolves beside it, and the posture is like a single thread, its nobility is unmatched. When earth forms a lair, the lair desires concealment. When earth transforms like metal, wood arrays between them, and the posture is like a split melon—its wealth is beyond words.

When fire lies in submission, the lair removes its peak. Sand dislikes leaving its homeland but also cherishes leaving it. What is disliked lies in sharpness and erosion, leading to drifting and dissipation. What is cherished lies in roundness and elegance, bringing distant official posts and merchant wealth.

Water should neither be cherished for leaving nor feared for leaving. That which leaves should glance back, beckoning wealth from all directions. That which comes should leap like an arrow—nothing will be harmonious.

*(Note: "穴音纬交覆深屋也" refers to the lair’s sound resembling the intertwining of weft threads, covering a deep chamber.)*

---

**Chapter Eight: On Dragons**

Fledglings awaiting food—how can they cry while crouching? Puppies rising from obscurity—how can they howl in alleyways? The peak of emerging power—the dragon’s rising ancestor is called the "emerging general," passing through a gorge is called the "passing general," and forming a lair is called the "settling general." How can jagged cliffs be round? The throat-like pass of the lair’s formation—how can it be level?

What of the two qi’s postures? What of the five omens’ movements? Marvelous and fleeting, changing with each step and dividing paths, they advance side by side, hooves and bridles competing in rank. Descending from high to low, attendants scatter in profusion—like a flock of sheep hesitating, like startled birds flapping their wings, like earthworms crawling along furrows, like spider silk tracing eaves, like the veins of silk, like the marks of water.

Their fluttering, single leaves must rush toward one formation; their piled, clustered ranks must follow one surge. Now rising, now falling; now broken, now connected. Posture does not burden form; form does not burden the tomb.

On a refined and elegant ridge, the lair desires ingenuity. On a dense and crowded ridge, the lair desires exclusivity. Faint and subtle, its descent already weak—the lair desires uprightness. Winding and meandering, its posture already horizontal—the lair must spiral.

Where leaves are lush and branches dense, the waist must be knotted. Where leaves are sparse and branches clear, the end may be extended. Thus it is said: when many mountains turn their heads, the lair is at the plain’s head; when many mountains fly and flee, the lair is at the water’s mouth.

Where high and low intercept one another, the lair takes form at the central waist—this may serve as the essential principle for tracking the Dragon, and likewise as the great foundation for locating the tomb.

Thus the Dragon’s movement has hands and feet; the Dragon’s repose has head and face. The Dragon’s offspring are dignified and solemn, turning toward no side; the Dragon’s four faces all regard the Dragon’s spirit.

The Dragon’s progression should mutually engender and harmonize; the Dragon’s strategic passes should mutually restrain and punish. The Dragon’s rise from its ancestor must face its descendants; the Dragon’s arrival at its head must resemble its ancestral line. Ancestral weight and depth yield fine descendants; descendants’ elegance and distinction arise from fine ancestors.

Thus it is said:
When ancestor and lineage are strong, one’s position is well-established, and descendants flourish.
Though the lineage branches, the ancestral virtue remains undiminished, and descendants prosper greatly.
Though the rise to prominence may be modest, the inheritance endures, and renown spreads far.
When the ancestor has passed but the lineage thrives, a generation’s wealth follows.
When ancestor and lineage shine, prosperity spans generations, descendants advance in succession, and the ancestral line endures unbroken.
When the lineage’s descent weakens and the ancestral mountain is delicate, a generation attains modest comfort.
When the lineage’s descent weakens but the ancestral mountain regards it, the tomb brings radiant greatness.
When the ancestor is lost and the lineage forsaken, one’s house is left to others—all because the ancestor abandoned the lineage.

Dragons of the north, south, east, and west in flatlands differ not from those of high mountains. For the Five Stars of high mountains form a solid body, while the Five Stars of flatlands form an external body. Branching, knotting at the throat, opening curtains to release veins—whether moving forward or backward, whether welcoming or sending off—there is no difference.

Yet the northern Dragon’s mountains prevail over water, so it often settles by qi; the southern Dragon’s water prevails over mountains, so it often settles by water.

**Chapter Nine: On the Tomb**

Recently, those of the Zhuan Gate have taken to saying: hollow, pincer, breast, and prominence.
Hollow and pincer are one—hollow is round, while pincer is straight.
Breast and prominence are one—prominence juts upward, while breast hangs down.
Dragons of Metal and Water favor forming hollows and pincers; Dragons of Earth and Wood favor forming breasts and prominences.

A hollow is verified by a bubbling center; without a bubbling center, it is askew or empty—such hollows bring orphanhood, widowhood, and poverty.
A pincer is verified by pierced ears; without pierced ears, it is drifting or scattered—such pincers cause family dispersal and separation.
A breast is verified by following water; without following water, it is withered or exhausted—such breasts bring either poverty or extinction.
A prominence is verified by whiskers and wings; without whiskers and wings, it is solitary and isolated—such prominences bring hardship and ruin.

In high mountains, tombs within prominences favor hollows; in flatlands, tombs within hollows favor prominences. Piercing emptiness or thinness makes the tomb’s nature malign; clamping firmness or suddenness makes the tomb’s nature warm and harmonious.

Borrow from officials, not from ghosts—borrowing from ghosts brings extinction. Borrow from tigers, not from Dragons—borrowing from Dragons brings annihilation. Borrow from tables, not from supports—borrowing from supports makes the household collapse like melting snow. Borrow from mountains, not from water—borrowing from water makes wealth shatter like ice.

**Chapter Ten: On the Sand**

When the main mountain descends, the surrounding mountains must assist, guarding and following like feathers and wings.

Mountains must stand distinct—distinct, then not lost in the crowd, rising above the multitude, peerless among the throng. Yet mountains must not stand alone—alone, then surely solitary, adrift and exiled, with no kin or followers. Mountains must not be too many—too many, then without reliance, like treacherous ministers and rebellious sons forming cliques in every direction. Mountains must be numerous—yet among the many, one must be supreme, arrayed to left and right like attendants and founding meritorious ministers.

Mountains must connect—connected, then they contend. Mountains must interlock—interlocked, then they do not leak. The distinction between a leaking state and one that seals lies in their mutual need and collision. Mountains must not hang low—low, then they lose advantage. Mountains must descend—descending, then their momentum halts. The distinction between hanging and descending lies in whether killing *qi* or vital *qi* prevails.

What is favored is completeness on all four sides; what is feared is dominance on one side alone. Without convergence, the Five *Qi* scatter to the Eight Winds; pressed or submerged, the Three Lights are imprisoned by the Five Agents.

Only when three sides are dense and expansive, accommodating and receptive, with one watercourse level and clear, does it serve to defend and guard. Nobility and baseness are firmly tied to the Rear Dragon; good and ill fortune fully manifest in the frontal opposition.

Like ants in orderly lines, they bind to their target, low and coiled in winding hollows; like bees in swarms, they array themselves along the sides, high and concealed in sheltered basins. Advancing, their momentum seems to clash; retreating, their stance remains reverent. Gathered to left and right as auxiliaries, they tower at the gates to seal the wind.

Like an unfolded screen or a dead city, they harbor no intent to scatter or depart; like a suspended bell or an overturned cauldron, they hold the sentiment of turning toward their source and honoring their lord. Why seek the likeness of banners or flags? And why insist on the form of bamboo shoots or spears?

When the rear trails and the front soars, left encircles and right bows—this is the vastness of mountains restraining the Dragon. When the rear is emaciated and the front disordered, left rebels and right stretches straight—this is the exhaustion of mountains forsaking their source. When branches divide behind and streams divide before, left like a comb’s teeth and right like a fine-toothed comb’s brows—this is mountains abandoning water, their feet uneven. When all behind resemble umbrella ribs and all before like flat boards, left departing like an army on the march and right like a fleeing horde—this is mountains chasing water, their feet branching apart.

Yet to discern the excellence or fault of the *sha* method, first observe the prosperity or decline of the primary lord. If the Wood Star of the primary lord is excessive, it is fitting to see Fire and Metal; if the Wood Star is greatly weakened, it is fitting to see Wood and Water—to bolster its decline, yet also to restrain its excess. From one instance, the rest may be inferred.

**Chapter Eleven: On Water**

Water follows the mountains’ course, mountains mark where water halts;
Water turns with the mountains’ bend, mountains guard against water’s flight.
Men know mountains must not tilt nor lean—who knows water must not overflow nor stagnate?
No sword-hilt streams beneath the armpit, no lopsided chimes flowing past the face,
No breast-dividing currents like a sash, no arm-splitting flows like roof tiles,
No frog-backed trickles, no vulture-chested double cascades.
A zither laid horizontally, its essence akin to an encircling lapel;
A crescent moon, a long rainbow—these bear no likeness to a reversed crossbow.
Six streams converging from the west, the right never hollow;
Six channels flowing eastward, the left never void.
Arriving like an unrolled mat, level and dividing the turbid earth;
Departing like a spread curtain, locked yet discerning the sky’s clarity.
With connections and intersections, without clashes or arrows.
Seeking the dragon’s head, the flowing waters must separate within and without;
Following the form to locate the site, they should return to their dips and descents.
For mountains seek a resting foot—if they stretch on, they sever other affections;
Water loves to encircle the city—if it turns away, it fails to gather inner *qi*.
Thus, first gather before releasing, and cherish lingering after departure.
A *qian* flow should be anchored by a long current, not escaping before the hall;
A minor accumulation meeting a greater one should curve within the wrist.
Though lakes and ponds may gleam clear, their stillness cannot match the intimacy of a goldfish’s gill-shadow.
Level and broad, desire the river’s silence; straight and swift, shun the ditch’s abrupt turns.
Level yet not level, horizontal yet not horizontal—diverging like the character "eight";
Tilted yet not tilted, straight yet not straight—locked like the character "ding."
Where banners wave and crossbows reverse, calamity draws near;
Where lips collapse and flanks press in, disaster arrives.
Long troughs and slanted pipes—within years, the coffers are swept clean;
Lopsided spears and dry cuts—within a generation or two, ancestral rites stand solitary.
Now like a sash, now like silk—each must align with celestial patterns;
Now dark, now deep—each governs its palace’s domain.
Chaotic streams crossing palaces tread clear paths yet trample murky radiance;
Pure and unadulterated, entering the road, they congeal like a green mirror reflecting the azure abyss.
When the primary lord is weak, it is fitting to nurture and support;
When the primary lord is strong, it is fitting to restrain and drain.
Support the weak, temper the strong—all returns to harmony.
When the dragon is true and the site is correct, the water naturally conforms to the method.
*Dǒu* sounds like "gather," *shōu* like "decline," *tā* like "other."
*Líng* sounds like "spirit."

**Chapter Twelve: On the Gorge Position**

Though star peaks must undergo transformation, they must not lose their original spirit. For instance, after the Metal Star alternates, it must reappear as Earth and Metal to retain its fundamental nature.

The discussion on the primary exposure pertains to crossing the gorge. Crossing the gorge resembles the edge of restrained *qi*, yet differs in essence. Restrained *qi* seeks temporary respite to prevent excessive dispersion, while the gorge acts as the pivotal mechanism—like a cicada’s molt or a dragon’s metamorphosis.

When the Metal Dragon crosses the gorge, consider that Metal is the essence of Earth. Water’s mother is born in *sì*, entombed in *chǒu*, nurtured in *chén*, flourishes in *yǒu*, wanes in *jī*, isolates in *guǐ*, clashes in *kòu*, perishes in *yín*, receives emolument in *shēn* and *yǒu*, and attains nobility in *yín* and *wǔ*. Passing from entombment and perishing to birth and flourishing, or from isolation and waning to emolument and nobility, marks an auspicious site. Yet the form traversed must distinguish between orthodox and variant transformations, like the purity of pearl and gold.

If the orthodox form crosses, all protective stars should align with its essence, as if molten gold flows in harmony. If the variant form crosses, all obstructive constellations should adapt to its transformation, making the gorge’s passage extraordinary. Before crossing, take generation as the body; after crossing, take restraint as the function.

For example, in rearing a child: once the skeletal frame matures, one must shed infantile traits to undertake adult responsibilities—hardening sinews, tempering resolve, refining through mutual polishing, to achieve full personhood. Thus, though Fire restrains Metal, Metal is refined by Fire’s passage; though Water restrains Fire, Fire is tempered by Water’s passage; though Wood restrains Earth, Earth is aerated by Wood’s passage; though Metal restrains Wood, Wood is honed by Metal’s passage; though Earth restrains Water, Water is solidified by Earth’s passage.

Observe the gorge’s flanks to discern verdancy or barrenness; its twists and turns to gauge elevation or enclosure; the roc’s form to infer the tomb’s posture; the gorge’s momentum to ascertain the tomb’s validation; its stars to determine the tomb’s hue; its depth or shallowness to measure the tomb’s penetration; its length or brevity to assess the tomb’s lip texture.

Once the gorge is crossed, examine which star emerges as the minor ancestor. If Metal crosses and Metal appears, the dragon is exposed—shield it with Water, so the child protects the mother, preventing her from being overtly revealed, thus preserving dignity. If Metal crosses and Water appears, the dragon is drained—fortify it with Earth to sustain its primordial *qi*. If Metal crosses and Earth appears, the dragon is congested—restrain it with Wood to gradually unveil its splendor. If Metal crosses and Wood appears, the dragon is afflicted—bolster it with Metal to prevent *qi* depletion. If Metal crosses and Fire appears, the dragon is injured—restrain it with Water, drain it with Earth to preserve its essence.

After the ancestral star arises, it must undergo transformation. After one or two transformations, restrain the *qi*. After repeated restraint, raise the summit. The summit star must clearly cross the gorge—if Metal serves as the summit, the dragon’s original spirit reveals its true essence.

Wood and Earth stars may interchange as mother and child. Water and Fire, however, are unsuitable for auspicious sites. When Metal encounters Wood or Fire, wealth and destruction converge. Yet if Metal layers without transformation, Wood and Fire must appear—like establishing a household, which requires wealth for stability and office for prominence.

---

**Chapter Thirteen: On Form and Momentum**

The incoming mountain embodies momentum. Convergence forms shape; momentum is the root, shape the blossom. When the blossom flourishes, the fruit is firm; when the root runs deep, the trunk thrives.

Momentum must flow—flowing far, its traces soar. Shape must not flow—flowing dissipates, dissipating fails to congeal. Momentum must not halt—halting severs its origin. Shape must halt—like a rooster, it gathers and contains.

Shape must not be exposed—exposure scatters *qi* in fleeting winds. Momentum must be exposed—exposure gathers *qi* at lofty peaks. Momentum must not be sullied—sullied *qi* stagnates and fails to congeal. Shape must be sullied—sullied *qi* gathers and harmonizes.

For shape is the accumulation of momentum, and momentum the exaltation of shape; shape is the convergence of momentum, and momentum the follower of shape. Lateral momentum flanks the center to guard the tomb; frontal momentum faces the tomb to respond to the dragon.

External momentum should be rounded, internal momentum refined. The ancestral dragon’s form resembles a flower’s core; the mounted dragon’s form, a hall’s center. It upholds the central chamber like a calyx, residing within the gate’s defense.

The positioned dragon’s form resembles a reclining person’s *jiānjǐng* or a leaping fish’s fin—adapting to its direction while laterally responding. The tomb of the receiving dragon resembles the heart’s gaze at the sash or the sun and moon’s radiance—tracing its lineage to broadly embrace and conceal, like an aristocratic residence with towering gates, its halls unseen; or a magnanimous host’s hospitality, exuding warmth and reverence.

Like a neglected garden with crumbling fences, it becomes a thoroughfare for the uninitiated; like a venerable elder or powerful official, it must be flanked by formidable guards; like a treasure vault or granary, it must be amply stocked and fortified. Thus it is said: roots coiled and stems firm yield branches flourishing like towering trees; flanks reinforced and streams channeled shape the true dragon.

---

**Chapter Fourteen: On Determining the Tomb Site**

The horizontal pulse determines the tomb site. The horizontal pulse arises where the dragon passes, then submerges at the center—strike where the shuttle’s belly swells. It must have a frontal audience and rear support, with lateral shoulders and hat, to be a true *dǒufǔ*. The perforated pulse often receives at the waist, favoring the dragon’s spine behind the summit.

The text states: "A horizontal pulse that bears and descends—where no dragon appears, its whiskers below may yet conceal a dragon." It must never emerge outward, for emergence severs the *qi*. The vertical pulse determines the tomb site. The vertical pulse arrives straight, neither left nor right; its burial point often risks *qi* leakage, so it must be flanked by layered, clasping shoulders to block the water before interment. If the mountain’s repose is too slack, the site may prove fatal. The text states: "Straight delivery, straight advance—where *qi* gathers, settle; where it scatters, abandon." It must never be aligned head-on, for alignment invites collision and ruin.

The broken pulse determines the tomb site. The broken pulse travels with swollen ends and a constricted center, like lotus roots linked by threads or a silver ingot’s narrowed waist. Identify the site within the slender middle, and if responsive, bury it where the waist receives *qi* in a transverse stance. Without response, the site remains ambiguous—never force it. The leaking pulse determines the tomb site. Beneath great mountains, beside embryonic breath, a faint thread seeps into the plain. If it swells into a mound, this is a *qi*-leaking site; bury at the summit, facing inward.

The stolen pulse determines the tomb site. The stolen pulse lies in flatlands, a grass-snake’s ash-line or tortoise-shell ridge rising from the level ground. Examine its terminus, and bury at the withered spine, ensuring a frontal *mingtang* and rearward water flow for authenticity. The flying pulse determines the tomb site. The flying pulse moves like spider trails or horse tracks, like swallows’ silk or drifting threads, often spiraling obliquely. Choose the center where encircling mountains converge, and bury at the most upright point.

The level pulse determines the tomb site. The level pulse is a plain’s dragon: when surrounding fields stand high and distant, and the approaching land dips into low fields, which then rise again into elevated tracts, the low becomes water, the high the pulse, and the outer heights the dragon and tiger. Observe where waters merge, and bury at the palm’s center. The descending pulse determines the tomb site. The descending pulse flows from lofty heights as a single thread, like a dangling insect or a spider’s suspended web. Bury at the tiny, pearl-like mound, avoiding grand constructions that harm the dragon.

The long pulse determines the tomb site. The long pulse stretches flat and drifts far, now surfacing, now vanishing, now swelling, now shrinking. Bury only where *qi* dissipates and waters converge in the *tang*. The short pulse determines the tomb site. The short pulse halts no true dragon, but at the dragon’s journey, one or two segments descend, or a single vein settles at a gathering point. Bury centrally, avoiding bias—though such sites bring swift rise and swift decline.

The true pulse determines the tomb site. The true pulse advances centrally, winding and supple, its stars aligned and refined. Bury where the facing peaks converge at the center; if the Dark Warrior’s snout extends, it suppresses the site; if its head retracts, it shields it.

**Chapter Fifteen: On Verifying the Tomb Site**

**The Five Stars Verify the Tomb Site.**
For Metal’s form, bury at its hollow where movement stirs; for Wood’s form, bury at its sprouting bud; for Water’s form, bury at its meandering stillness; for Fire’s form, bury at its fading flame; for Earth’s form, bury at its parched thinness.

**The Nine Stars Verify the Tomb Site.**
The Greedy Wolf’s site is the nipple’s tip;
The Great Gate’s site is sought in the hollow’s core;
The Military Arts’ site hides in hairpin clamps;
The Fortune-Star’s teeth carve the helmet’s crown;
The Literary Star’s site lies flat within, or drops from the palm’s high center;
The Breaking Army’s site resembles spear and halberd, gathered at the west’s backhand;
The Auxiliary Star’s site mirrors a swallow’s nest, or a lantern hung on lofty peaks;
The Correcting Star’s site rests in low plains, its rounded head still concave.

**The Three Postures Verify the Tomb Site.**
Upright mountains soar, *qi* ascends;
Reclining mountains lie, *qi* descends;
Seated mountains harmonize, *qi* conceals within—these are Heaven, Earth, and Human postures.

**Facing Mountains Verify the Tomb Site.**
The *an* and *dui* seek resonance, like host and guest in greeting, like husband and wife in intimacy, their intent unbroken, their forms interlocked without retreat. A singular peak strikes true; paired ridges share the embrace. High peaks meet the brows, low peaks meet the heart. Thus it is said: "Only the facing mountain knows the heart’s fortune."

**The Bright Hall Verifies the Tomb Site.**
Where the site coalesces, a small *mingtang* must appear, gathering minor boundary waters within. If the hall is centered, bury slightly left; if the site leans left, bury right; if right, the hall’s aid lies there. The site resembles a tray holding cups, or bundled feet—never a lopsided hall.

**Water’s Flow Verifies the Tomb Site.**
Where the site forms, water must curve and pool, still as if unmoving. The text states: "Water bends without chaos—if it gathers left, the site leans left; if right, the site leans right." It also says: "Left water clear, lean left; right water clear, lean right. Facing water should be high; flowing water should be low." For water is the dragon’s blood and pulse: mountains move, water follows; waters merge, mountains rest. Thus the site is marked by water.

**The Joyous Mountain Verifies the Tomb Site.**
The dragon’s path winds, its site often misaligned. The peak behind the tomb star must birth a joyous mountain—whether from its own range or another—so long as it stands behind the burial point as a response. Straight arrivals favor rear seating; ancestral ranges favor lateral alignment. A joyous star misaligned with the site is not true.

**The Ghost and Official Verify the Tomb Site.**
A lateral arrival demands a ghost; a straight descent meets an official. Behind the site, trailing peaks are ghosts, also serving as support; ahead, trailing peaks are officials, also serving as *an*. With these two mountains, the site must sit upon the ghost and face the official—without this, it is not a true site.

**The Dragon and Tiger Verify the Tomb Site.**
The dragon opposes water, the site clings to the dragon;
The tiger opposes water, the site clings to the tiger;
A leftward lift leans on the dragon, a rightward lift on the tiger.
High dragon, high site; low dragon, low site; mid-level dragon, mid-level site.

If the site is low while the dragon and tiger are high, avoid compressing the tomb; if the site is high while the dragon and tiger are low, avoid exposing the burial chamber. Thus, examining the dragon and tiger can also reveal the nature of the site.

**The Ten Paths Verify the Tomb Site.**
The Ten Paths derive from the Four Directions, requiring front and back to align, left and right to balance. The seat should not be skewed, the *an* should not slant, and the flanking "ears" should neither advance nor retreat. When establishing the site, first observe the intersecting "piercing gaze" of the cross, then ensure the rear is rounded and the front is pointed—when left and right align with the gaze, it is called: *"The Celestial Heart’s Ten Paths, Mouth Holding a Bit."*

**Convergence and Divergence Verify the Tomb Site.**
Above, divergence clarifies the arrival; below, convergence confirms the residence as true. This is the inner *mingtang* where waters converge, akin to the "shrimp-whisker" and "goldfish-boundary" juncture. A site with convergence but no divergence has an unclear origin; one with divergence but no convergence has an unstable residence—it is a false site.

**Radiant Qi Verifies the Tomb Site.**
When the dragon and tiger’s outer elbows brim with flourishing *qi*, it forms radiance, which can also verify the site. Radiance on the left places the site to the left; radiance on the right places it to the right; radiance on both sides centers the site between them. Long and clear radiance denotes nobility; short and clustered radiance is most potent. Only when the dragon’s pulse is exhausted do such rare configurations appear in early or middle stages.

**Direction and Corner Verify the Tomb Site.**
While each form can verify direction and corner individually, they may also verify collectively. If the site forms at the center where paths intersect, use the convergence of two waters to confirm it. If the site forms where water flows horizontally across level ground, use the dragon’s distinct *qi* pulse to confirm it. If the site forms where the pulse and water compete, use the intent of the facing mountains to confirm it. For sites forming beside the main or back pulse, use the balance of left and right to confirm. For sites forming where dragon and tiger offer partial protection, use the grandeur of the *tang qi* to confirm. For ambiguous sites on flat or convex terrain, use wide sands and embracing waters to confirm. Generally, verifying a site involves multiple methods, all hinging on its *qing* (affinity) and *shi* (configuration). If the affinity is genuine, even a single verification suffices; if affinity is lacking, even multiple verifications cannot guarantee completeness—this depends on the practitioner’s own insight.

---

**Chapter Sixteen: On Stellar Qi**
Master Tao states: The highest methods are rarely complete, making true dragons difficult to discern and true sites hard to clarify. Some flourishing *qi* follows mountain contours, and identifying its star reveals its *qi*—this is easily distinguished. When stars array behind the *qi*, they spread before the peaks; when *qi* leans left, stars spread right. In such cases, grasping the star means losing the *qi*, and grasping the *qi* means neglecting the star. Hence, it is said: recognizing *qi* is difficult. Generally, dragons gather *qi*, and stars assist in anchoring it. The adept recognize *qi* without losing sight of the stars; the inept grasp the stars but miss the *qi*—this is the natural principle. Yet at the "clam’s waist" or "crane’s knee," examining the transition reveals the true arrival; at plump, rounded, or level convergences, discerning the dimple clarifies the formation. Extending this logic ensures infallibility.

**Chapter Seventeen: On the Ultimate Halo**
In geomancy, the *ji* (ultimate) and *yun* (halo) are paramount. Every path has its *ji*, every star its *yun*; without *ji* or *yun*, wealth and nobility are unattainable. Earth is the *ji* of Metal; Water, Wood, and Fire are its *yun*. The *ji* is the primordial *qi*; the *yun* is the functional spirit. The *ji* neither advances nor retreats, shimmering like a parent’s tender care. The *yun* neither urges nor bows, clinging like a mutual gaze in a portrait.

When a Metal dragon crosses a gorge, the Earth star subtly reveals its signs, and the *zi* (child), *fu* (father), *cai* (wealth), and *guan* (official) stars array in orderly succession. As it crosses the gorge and ascends to its origin, the Earth star shines from afar, while Water, Metal, Earth, and Wood cluster closely behind. When *qi* is bound with Earth concealing Metal, the father’s primordial *qi* guards and nurtures the dragon. When Metal carries Earth, the *cai* (wealth) and *gui* (ghost) stars escort the emerging *guan* (official), or wealth hides while the *zi* (child) star assumes a dignified stance—this is the *zheng yun* (proper halo). The *zheng yun* bestows enduring rank and eminence, producing governors and ministers. When the *guan* (official) star bows and wealth surges while the *zi* (child) star dominates, it is the *bian yun* (transformative halo). The *bian yun* confers fierce authority, producing censors and generals.

A dragon without the *ji* is like a cinnamon tree without roots—even with one *yi* or two *jia*, it will not yield a top scholar’s son. A *tang qi* without the *ji* is like gold without seed—even with civil and military aides, ascending to the halls of ministers is difficult. When the *ji* appears with the *yun*, the dragon’s pattern and phoenix’s decree are dignified and serene; when the *yun* appears with the *ji*, the purple chambers and vermilion gates shrink in awe. At the burial site, if the *ji* resides in the supporting *tuo*, it may become a screen or barrier of honor. In the *mingtang*, if the *yun* arrays like officials, the birds must adopt a formation like a court assembly.

If the *ji* lies hidden among the sands, its edges remain concealed, while the *yun* flows subtly. If the *ji* is concealed in the water’s curves, its sources and branches intertwine, and the *yun* spreads abundantly. If the *ji* and *yun* of the dragon and site are incomplete, disaster may befall the noble clans. If the *ji* and *yun* of the sands and waters are deficient, misfortune may spread among the people. A *ji* with flaws cannot produce filial sons or loyal ministers; a *yun* with deviations cannot speak of governing the state or aiding the world.

Each *ji* has its place, and every direction should reveal its *ji*. If seized by wealth, it becomes a greedy *ji*; if seized by the child, a weak *ji*; if seized by the official, a contentious one. If I suppress it, it becomes especially glorious for the ancestors. Each *yun* has its position, and each position has its proper alignment. The *guan yun*’s position should be sparse; the *cai yun*’s position should be concealed; the *zi yun*’s position should be upright. Any misalignment becomes a source of calamity.

If all four *yun* are complete within the *mingtang*, the occupant will pass the imperial exams in youth. If all four *yun* are present at the water’s mouth, the occupant will achieve scholarly success in later years. For great wealth, the *cai yun* must remain upright and properly positioned—wealth does not arrive suddenly but accumulates like pearls or stacks of silk, eventually filling thousands of granaries. For great nobility, the *guan yun* must be sparse and occupy the proper direction—nobility does not come abruptly but requires refinement and perseverance before one’s name is renowned. Thus it is said: what I overcome is wealth; what overcomes me is the official. Those who fail to recognize this must proceed with caution.

**Chapter Eighteen: On the Four Spirit Stars**

The land’s hundred forms share a single principle—trace the lineage to its origin, and the shadow reveals the shape. The gorge position corresponds to the public position; the rear ghost responds to the front official. The incoming *qi* follows the residual *qi*; the parted lapels match the joined lapels. Behind lies support, ahead a protective lip; the arrival shows swaying, the dwelling presents a face. The dragon’s whiskers charm the tiger, and the tiger must be tamed by the dragon. Most detestable is a dragon jagged like a tiger; most beloved, a tiger supple as a dragon. A tiger supple as a dragon brings no punishment and fills one’s coffers with wealth. A dragon jagged like a tiger harms the strong and depletes the treasury. The Vermilion and Mysterious Stars, with their father the Azure-White, govern wealth and office—flanked by no hollows, they ensure both prosperity and nobility. Ghosts dislike ghosts, yet officials favor them; falls are ill-suited to falls, but radiance must appear in radiance. To treat a ghost as a ghost is to invite thieving *qi*, leaving the household desolate and descendants few. To treat an official as an official fears no sharp peaks, for it nurtures men of distinction, grand estates, and top scholars. Ghosts dislike ghosts and also dislike sons; only when the father and elder brother’s wealth star aligns is it properly placed. Officials suit officials and also suit wealth and father—Wood and Fire, these two stars, are most favored by Heaven. To fall into a fall is to weaken one’s position, rendering one’s presence as if absent, the household dwindling. To radiate in radiance is to possess a striking form; if it is a noble dragon, one attains high office. Of harmful ghosts, ten or fifteen out of sixteen exist—wherever officials and radiance appear, all become blessings. Great wealth and nobility require clear distinctions: one cherishes only wealth and office that possess *qi* and sentiment. A noble dragon without an official’s momentum is not noble; without wealth’s embrace, it is also not noble. A wealthy dragon without wealth’s momentum is not wealthy; without an official’s embrace, it is also not wealthy. For wealth and nobility are intertwined, and wealth and office guard each other. Wealth unseen by office does not flourish; office unseen by wealth does not protect. When the master speaks, the gates open and the doors close. If the gate opens without a screen, the eldest branch declines. If the door closes without submission, the youngest child turns rebellious. The hall calls for an unobstructed *mingtang*—level, upright, and full of sentiment, which is most auspicious. Whether encircling or unlocking, width is immeasurable: broad expanses leak *qi*, while narrow confines gather sentiment. The water’s mouth shaped like a gourd makes the master laugh, yet without examining the stars and peaks, one vainly praises the ornamental pillar. If one encounters descendants like Tao Zhu, all is desolate—only when the brush and seal are solemn, and the water departs, does nobility arise. Granaries born against the flow gather wealth as water leaves; without crane’s claws in the sand, literary scholars are few. Crane’s claws that do not grasp instead become sordid; without ox horns in the sand, one ends in desolation. Ox horns that do not open instead yield fine returns. Without cicada wings in the sand, the tomb’s sentiment is unclear; cicada wings swollen with congestion leave men bewildered. The summit loves a central perforation, and the perforation loves its mystery. Without this mystery, it becomes a dead eel; branches suffering stem clashes—what boast of a proper descent? To be afflicted at the tomb is to face certain ruin; even with a water city, there will be many breaches. Masters who fail to recognize this, coveting locks and seals, turn *qi*-restraining dragons and water into prisons. Without a pardon, young women and children are bound in fetters. This is a secret transmission—only through keen insight can one attain it.

**Chapter Nineteen: On Left and Right Court Alignments**

Within the gate, a gentleman naturally attracts fine guests; outside the gate, few fine guests suggest an unworthy host. A tiled gate harbors bandits, bringing a hundred misfortunes; if the lock and key are not secure, flying disasters arrive from all directions. A desk for swift reports differs little from one for exiles; a mountain of weeping children is no different from one of prodigies. The form of dancing sleeves resembles parting from one’s homeland; the shape of a prison is like a granary. Within a hundred paces north and south, no towering desk should rise—once every sixty years, it sweeps clean as an empty void. To lift one’s gaze and see a high peak is to endure a hundred thousand years of unending sorrow. Thus it is said: when the *mingtang* opens toward *wu* and *zi*, the elderly grow despondent; when the door closes toward *you* and *mao*, the young lack vigor. Passing the imperial exams and achieving top honors require the Three Harmonies; the Fire Star accumulates jade and piles gold, but only with water channels in all eight directions. A mounted noble may yet be a thief peeking from concealment; a companion in the tent may be a mountain announcing edicts. A swaying writing brush ages one into a scholar; a gaunt jade zither sends one wandering in all directions. Before the ancestral tomb, facing it brings fortune, turning away brings misfortune—yet a crude, inferior corpse tiger returns. Civil brings misfortune, martial brings fortune—further examine merit and achievement. When dragon and tomb are complete, the Water, Fire, and Earth Stars all yield fine *yuanchen* prospects. *Zi*, wealth, and office positions become calamitous dragons embracing tigers; tigers embracing dragons all follow water’s judgment. Right shooting left, left shooting right—all derive from the hall’s division.
