A mountain standing alone and bowing low subdues a thousand officials; water arriving from afar and settling clear multiplies wealth by ten thousand acres. Among chaotic mountains, only one position faces the tomb directly—naturally, ten thousand tributaries branch forth. Only the half-mirror facing the hall settles decisively: the household’s wealth and nobility are assured, yet kinship’s glory is difficult. When water ends and mountains exhaust, one must seek peaks that accompany and honor; among many scattered graves, only the kneeling, sleeping form is beloved.

**Chapter Twenty: On the Body’s Compass**

For a straight dragon, observe the transformations of its brain; for a horizontal dragon, seek the forms of its support. With brain *qi* and support in place, there naturally exists upper division and lower union—between division and union, Heaven and Earth create the design. A grandson who does not resemble his ancestor is called *su*; *su* does not produce men of letters. A son who does not support his mother is called *lou*; *lou* is difficult to cultivate as a root of wealth and nobility. Among brothers, whether weak or strong, what matters is no clash and no affliction. Between host and guest, whether near or far, what matters is harmony and mutual generation. Among servants, whether many or few, what matters is no star that rebels against the master. Among gates and doors, whether wide or narrow, what matters is a constellation that guards the entrance. Among mountains, whether front, back, left, or right, what matters is alignment with the stars, with no head seen flying and no feet seen fleeing. Among waters, whether coming or going, clear or flowing, what matters is alignment with the directions, with no incoming clashes and no departing cuts. Gathered *qi* should be round and striving; the two sides must not leak. Leakage weakens the traveling dragon; passing through a gorge should be warm and harmonious, with no dispersion on either side. Dispersion leaves the tomb without sentiment. Better to borrow a resting chariot’s host as a guest than to mistake a resting guest for the host.

Better to draw upon the primal spirit’s waters for wealth than to summon the Yellow Springs’ waters for nobility.

Though ancestors may be fine, after five or six generations their merit fades. Though retainers may be many, after five or six generations they scatter.

A dragon fleeing or tiger soaring matters little if the water city enfolds it; a broad hall and distant desk matter little if the dragon’s power is not lofty.

Land that hastens office requires devotion to the emolument star; a noble tomb that brings fortune must welcome *qi* from the dragon.

Spirit, palace, and killing stars in the same village—though but a hair’s breadth apart—may bring either calamity or blessing. A jade eave but a foot away—with a single touch—may bring either fortune or disaster.

Star peaks in orderly ranks, though marred by one or two minor flaws, remain auspicious land. Ghostly obstructions layered thick, though a single mountain or stream may seem promising, do not make a fine city.

Water loves curves, not straightness: straight departure is surely inauspicious, while straight approach must still guard against clashing arrows. Tributaries love arrival, not departure: long outflow is unpropitious, while short outflow is but a fragmented branch.

Water clear and mountains bright—reliable and absorbable—are full of power. Mountains howling and waters weeping—unseen and unheard—are no cause for concern.

A dragon that does not unfurl its screen or array its canopy boasts in vain of powdered beauties and floral charms. A tomb without rear ghosts or front officials, though adorned with towers and drums, is but an empty display.

Literary brilliance unmatched yet failing the imperial exams stems from blurred brush imprints. Descendants’ wealth and nobility, though not numerous, arise from shallow green-felt lips.

Where water departs, a single gleaming stone exerts a thousand *jun* of force. When arrows arrive, a mere ladle of *xun* water holds ten thousand *yi* of sentiment.

The main dragon’s fortune comes late but endures, thus generals arise from generals’ houses, and ministers from ministers’ houses. The side dragon’s fortune comes swiftly but fades, thus one moment thunders forth, the next crumbles to ash.

**Chapter Twenty-One: On True and False Manifestations**

Dragon caves are easy to recognize, but discerning true from false is difficult. The true is often unrefined, the false often wondrous.

The true resembles a virtuous woman behind her curtain, solemn in her procession, with no sidelong glances or mixed inkstones. The false resembles a wanton woman with exposed face and bared head, brimming with chaotic, darting gazes.

The true resembles an imperial procession—though adorned with towers, drums, and horns—hands clasped and head bowed. The false resembles a prodigal son—though surrounded by floral charms and powdered beauties—racing west and chasing east.

A mountain head large yet crooked and fleeing is a false mountain. Sand feet numerous yet embracing backward are false sands.

A tomb site isolated and desolate, with no ear-piercing peaks, no brain-shielding pillows, no yang-lip cushions, and no yin-beard wings, is a false tomb.

A water city winding yet misaligned with directions, clashing arrows subtly approaching, appearing meandering up close but crude from afar, is false water.

True dragons may be pierced through, but false dragons are also pierced through. True dragons may be shielded, but false dragons are also shielded.

Pierced without root is often a dry dragon’s stray guest. Shielded without enclosure is half a scattered remnant of banditry.

Great falsehoods bring great misfortune over time; small falsehoods bring minor prosperity over time.

Tangled and clinging, ten out of ten are rarely genuine. Concealed and elusive, ten thousand out of ten thousand bear false appearances.

The vulgar eye points to a gray line beside which father and son coexist as true. The contemporary master, enamored of silk traces, sees official fortune and wealth as false.

**Chapter Twenty-Two: On Restraint**

Land without restraint leaves the dragon spirit adrift; with restraint firmly in place, wealth and nobility are both complete.

A mountain of restraint bears a dignified and solemn aura: it is not a ghost behind, not an official ahead, not a dragon to the left, nor a tiger to the right.

The restraint of imperial and royal capitals lies a hundred or two hundred *li* away. The restraint of official families and scholarly clans lies between ten and twenty *li*.

A father as restraint—rooted deep—brings generations of official caps. A son as restraint—suddenly flourishing—spreads mighty renown far and wide. Wealth as restraint fills granaries and brings imperial grain tribute. Office as restraint ascends the imperial exams and produces generals and ministers. An elder brother as restraint brings balanced power and shared glory.

Restraint of the Metal Star is surely soaring like a cauldron or bell, while the Fire Star fades nearby. Restraint of the Wood Star is surely towering like a halberd or spear, while the Metal Star yields below. Restraint of the Water Star is surely swelling like a flood, unmoving and unshaken, while the Earth Star circles low. Restraint of the Fire Star is surely blazing like flames, neither flying nor fleeing, while the Water Star lurks hidden. Restraint of the Earth Star is surely level with the heavens, neither pointed nor emaciated, while the Water Star retreats low.

Restraint encountering office, though steeped in poetry and ritual, may still bring lawsuits and prisons. Restraint meeting a father fosters filial piety and brotherly love yet risks displacement and danger. Restraint meeting a son brings many heroes yet may see them wither in their prime. Restraint meeting wealth brings abundance yet may leave the wife’s palace desolate. Restraint meeting an elder brother divides households and sparks rivalry.

Only a mountain standing alone, with no peers beside it and all stars yielding below, brings a household of solemn dignity and ten thousand blessings.

If the dragon comes from the left, restraint lies to the right, as if tempered on the left. If the dragon comes from the right, restraint lies to the left, as if tempered on the right. If the dragon comes from behind, restraint lies ahead, as if guarding the rear.

Where the dragon arrives, restraint beyond several layers of dragon and tiger is marvelous. Where the dragon departs, restraint within the water’s mouth and gate is sublime.

Restraint arising from the land of birth and prosperity brings a century of spring; restraint arising from the land of burial and decay leaves seventy years rare.

Within the water’s mouth, the suburbs serve as attendants, manifesting the Dark Warrior’s arm—this is a fortress of governance. When the great form and configuration overlook not a single mountain or stream, the master’s discerning eye seeks restraint first.

**Chapter Twenty-Three: On Intersection**

To judge fortune like tallying tokens, one must know intersection. The secret says: *Fengshui* without intersection brings no wealth like Tao Zhu, no nobility like the Three Dukes, no longevity like Peng Zu, no harmonious couples, only orphaned and widowed households.

Thus, a dragon without intersection is called "jealous"—its jealousy brings no century of fortune. A tomb without intersection is called "chronic"—its chronicity brings no decade of blessing. Sands without intersection are called "squalid"—their squalor leaves descendants without emolument. Waters without intersection are called "fragmented"—their fragmentation scatters households.

**Chapter Twenty-Three (Continued): On Intersection**

From the pass to the lesser ancestor marks the *dragon’s intersection*—if a Metal Dragon fails to receive Water’s protection, it is called "child-jealousy," and how can one hope for three or five prosperous generations? If a Water Dragon fails to receive Metal’s regard, it is called "father-jealousy," and white-haired elders will surely not fill the household. If a Metal Dragon fails to receive Wood’s support, it is called "wealth-jealousy," and daily sustenance will ever be hard to secure. If a Wood Dragon fails to receive Metal’s aid, it is called "office-jealousy," and literary talent will not ascend the azure path. If a Metal Dragon fails to receive Metal’s protection, it is called "elder-brother-jealousy," and one will toil alone to uphold the family.

From the ghost’s rise to the lip’s mat marks the *tomb’s intersection*—if a Metal tomb fails to encounter Wood, it is called "wealth-chronicity," and how can one boast of granaries in every quarter? If a Metal tomb fails to encounter Metal, it is called "elder-brother-madness," and envy not the dragon spirits’ many flanking aids. If a Metal tomb fails to encounter Fire, it is called "office-madness," and how can one speak of manifold literary stars’ assistance? If a Metal tomb fails to encounter Earth, it is called "father-madness," and surely the hall will lack uncles and aunts. If a Metal tomb fails to encounter Water, it is called "child-madness," and orphans and widows will often appear.

From the following dragon to the gate marks the *water’s intersection*—if a Metal tomb has Water as its city, this is called "child," and fragmentation ensures ancestral wealth will never be abundant. If a Metal tomb has Wood as its city, this is called "wealth," and fragmentation means daily toil with pecks and bushels. If a Metal city has Water, this is called "office," and fragmentation leaves one white-haired yet still poring over texts. If a Metal tomb has a Metal city, this is called "elder-brother," and fragmentation brings constant strife, like tigers battling within the walls.

From the green left to the white right marks the *sand’s intersection*—if a Metal Dragon’s waters layer upon layer, this is called "child," and squalor means frequent births but slow growth. If a Water Dragon’s Metals surge, this is called "father," and squalor brings discord between stepfathers and stepmothers. If a Metal Dragon’s Woods flow abundantly, this is called "wealth," and squalor scatters gains as soon as they are gathered. If a Wood Dragon’s Metals stand in rows, this is called "office," and squalor keeps reputation and rank ever lowly. If a Metal Dragon’s Metals blaze, this is called "elder-brother," and squalor leaves siblings equal in talent but unequal in fortune.

Thus, the dragon and tomb’s intersection is judged by visible *forceful sands*, while the water’s intersection is judged by visible *taboos*. This secret lies in heartfelt realization—it cannot be transmitted in a single breath.

**Chapter Twenty-Four: On the Five Phases’ Generation and Conquest**

The essence of *fengshui* is governed by the Five Phases, and their handle of power lies in generation and conquest. Generation is surely beloved, yet there is generation one ought not to love. Conquest is surely detestable, yet there is conquest one ought not to detest.

The forms of generation are five: first, *flying and crouching*; second, *resonance and response*; third, *sequence and reversal*; fourth, *masculine and feminine*; fifth, *reclining, sitting, and standing*—these are the modes of generation.

Heaven-bestowed Metal generates Water as *flying generation*; Lesser Yin Metal generates Water as *crouching generation*. Flying generation fears haste, crouching generation fears strength—generation that is hasty or overbearing breeds households of discord. A Metal tomb with a Water desk is *resonant generation*; a Metal tomb with an Earth desk is *responsive generation*. Resonant generation favors proximity, responsive generation favors prominence—without proximity or prominence, wealth and nobility will not be attained. Metal peaks eroding Water is *sequential generation*; Metal peaks eroding Earth is *reversed generation*. Sequential generation loves refinement, reversed generation loves clustering—without refinement or clustering, outstanding talent will not flourish. Left Metal and right Water is *masculine generation*; right Metal and left Water is *feminine generation*. Masculine generation favors prostration, feminine generation favors elevation—without prostration, flesh and blood turn against one another; without elevation, the household’s path goes awry. Generation from the proper form is called *sitting*; from the brain, *reclining*; from the face, *standing*. Sitting generation favors uprightness, reclining generation favors attachment, standing generation favors levelness—without uprightness, attachment, or levelness, early deaths and late lamentations will follow.

The forms of conquest among the Five Phases are also five: first, *strength and weakness*; second, *presence and absence*; third, *affection and fear*; fourth, *manifest and hidden*; fifth, *self and other*—these are the modes of conquest.

Heaven-bestowed Metal, residing in the *geng-shen* and *xin-you* directions to conquer Wood stars, is *strong conquest*. Lesser Yin Metal, emerging from the *bing-ding*, *si-wu* directions to conquer Wood stars, is *weak conquest*. Strong conquering strong or weak conquering weak is *slaughter*; strong conquering weak or weak conquering strong is *subjugation*. If a Fire star directly strikes a Metal tomb while ghostly omens and avian constellations are all Water stars, and no Earth star restrains the Water, then conquest exists as if it did not. If a proper Metal Dragon has no Fire star’s encroachment and Wood stars cluster densely, yet solitary Metal cannot restrain them, then conquest does not exist as if it did. Conquest that exists as if it did not brings lofty rank and might; conquest that does not exist as if it did brings repeated injuries. If a Metal star enters the hand with true sharpness, jealousy is *manifest conquest*; if glaring Fire stars flank and escort the Metal Dragon to the tomb, this is *hidden conquest*. Manifest conquest brings overt calamity; hidden conquest brings covert harm. If a concealed Metal Dragon bears Fire stars pressing upon the master, this is *self-conquest*; if an external mountain towers with Fire stars as rivals, this is *other-conquest*. Other-conquest brings strife among the village; self-conquest brings discord among kin. Malevolent stars and ominous omens, like jealousy, *lian-zhen*, and true sharpness, rejoice at the sight of Water stars—this is *affectionate conquest*. Auspicious stars in their proper places, like Metal in the *dui* position, dread the sight of Fire stars—this is *fearful conquest*. Affectionate conquest that conquers brings no small fortune; fearful conquest that conquers brings unending misfortune.

**Chapter Twenty-Five: On the Three Harmonies**

If the dragon does not harmonize with the tomb, the dragon is not true; if the tomb does not harmonize with the water, the tomb is not proper. For instance, if a Wood Dragon arises in *hai*, the tomb in *mao*, and the water flows to *wei*, then myriad blessings will follow. For the water’s source should favor generation and flourishing, while its outlet should favor repose and extinction. Thus it is said: the gate and door must not be occupied by descendants.

Without the Three Harmonies, water’s fortune will not prosper; without the Three Harmonies, fire’s nobility will not shine; without the Three Harmonies, the dragon’s *qi* will not accumulate; without the Three Harmonies, the tomb’s affinity will not gather; without the Three Harmonies, the sands’ force will not be upright. The mark of a land of wealth and nobility lies herein.

Diviners have proposed a theory of "false Three Harmonies" to prognosticate fortune and misfortune—this is entirely mistaken. When the dragon’s arising aligns with its proper direction, this is the Celestial Harmony; when the tomb’s formation rides the phase of generation, this is the Human Harmony; when the water rides the phase of flourishing, this is the Earthly Harmony—these are the Three Harmonies of the dragon. For instance, when the *gui* sits in *xuan*, this is the Rear Harmony; when the office refines in *ming*, this is the Front Harmony; when the luminaries unfold to the side, this is the Side Harmony—these are the Three Harmonies of the tomb. First, not clashing with the dragon is the Coming Harmony; second, not opposing the tomb is the Halting Harmony; third, not following the water’s flow is the Departing Harmony—these are the Three Harmonies of the sands. When the source comes from generation and nurturance, this is the Source Harmony; when it pours into the office of flourishing, this is the Pouring Harmony; when it departs toward extinction and repose, this is the Flowing Harmony—these are the Three Harmonies of water. When dragon and tiger blend as one, this is the Ear Harmony; when the Eight Characters evenly pour, this is the Elbow Harmony; when the shrimp’s whiskers branch in parallel, this is the Fluid Harmony—these too are the Three Harmonies of water. For example, when the true case lies in *wu* and a graceful peak arises, this is the Harmony of Heaven and Earth; when a peak responds on *yin*, this is the Harmony of Sun and Moon; when a peak shines on *xu*, this is the Harmony of Wind and Cloud—these are the Three Harmonies of fire.

A single harmony ensures no dispersion, no extinction; a double harmony ensures no poverty, no lowliness; a complete harmony brings great wealth and high nobility. Yet this may be encountered but not sought. Generally, when land possesses complete harmony, the gorge will have angular regard; when the gorge has angular regard, the dragon will have ancestors; when the dragon has ancestors, the tomb will not stand alone and exposed—sometimes like a moored boat with oars stowed, sometimes like an enthroned lord with civil and martial officials in full attendance. As for the theories of Three Failures and Three Taboos, they are simply the opposite of this and may be deduced by analogy. Fire aids the guest of literary brilliance, hence fire alone is cited to illustrate the rest.

---

From this chapter through the thirtieth, all are the discourses of Xiong of Guangfeng. Others observe form, I observe stars; others observe structure, I observe *qing*. Star-peaks are but the dragon’s exterior, while *qing*-veins are the dragon’s essence. The Metal star favors which *gui* for its lodging? Earth and Wood intermingle in their *qing*. When blue and white approach without water or fire, unmelting and untransforming, they become crude and unrefined. Metal, Wood, Water, and Earth are the tomb’s stars; hollows, pincers, breasts, and protrusions are the tomb’s forms; convex, concave, high, and low are the tomb’s terrain. Examine the evidence closely and discuss it clearly: first observe the Bright Hall and the facing case, then the rear lodging and the *gui* support, and beside them the dragon, tiger, and gazing water. Beyond this, consider the *qing* of sands and water.

I have seen folding-screen and writing-brush sands, the White Tiger clinging close in a picture-perfect form. Yet because the gorge’s right side stands alone, the youngest chamber remains without esteem. I have seen jade-belt and sleeping-bow waters, curving and embracing the Azure Dragon’s maw. Yet because the gorge’s left side lacks an auspicious star, the eldest chamber meets no felicity. I have seen hat-like and zither-like cases, solemn and towering trees truly admirable. Why then do the descendants fail to achieve scholarly honors? It is because the *gui*’s lodging has disordered the stars. I have seen a Bright Hall teeming like ten thousand horses, with ranks assembled and voices raised in acclaim. Who would know that the lip-cushion is not resonant? Wealth has no accumulation, and officials receive no stipends. I have seen a water’s mouth like a gourd, *zhi* and *xuan* winding and turning to regard one another. How then does the household not prosper? It is only because the dragon and tomb lack granaries. I have seen a literary brush thrust upon the case, with "Summons to Court" and "Heavenly Steed" present. If the dragon and tomb lack proper arrangement, why endure a decade of cold windows? I have seen a brocade quilt covering a tooth-bed, layered mountains resembling the forms of descendants. Yet because the dragon and tomb fall into gloom and chill, the household’s sons and daughters-in-law remain lonely and struggle to thrive.

How do sands and waters become calamities? It is only because the dragon and tomb’s original spirit is defeated. It is like a weak lord and mediocre ruler—treacherous ministers and fierce generals wield great power. How do sands and waters possess strong fortune? It is only because the dragon and tomb’s original spirit flourishes. It is like a sagely lord enthroned in the court—all four directions are shielded by stalwart generals. I have heard that dragon and tomb must greet one another, that water and sands must accord with one another. Auspicious water can always dispel malevolent sands, and good sands can also regulate water’s afflictions. If a yin sand on the right crosses to the left, water departing on the left cannot become a calamity. Yet if the sand departs and the water also departs, the household’s feast will swiftly collapse.

---

The dragon and tomb must recognize their *yongshen*; disturbing the *yongshen* is no trivial matter. Be warned: it may be known but not spoken, lest the contemporary masters fail to recognize it truly. What *shen* does a Metal dragon and Metal tomb employ? It is born in *si* and reposes in *chou*. Either use these two directions as facing mountains or employ them as entry points. Do not fear the Yellow Springs or the Punishing Tomb; the tomb must align with the dragon’s approach. The Doomed Fate favors the palaces of Water and Fire, and the burial time must be at the *chou* hour’s throat. Within the *si-chou* palace, *you* is concealed; one must use *mao* to strike and awaken the *hun*. If descendants include *hai* or *wei* persons, combining to activate the striking star is truly infallible.

The Metal Horse originally dwells in *hai* and *you*; the Metal Fortune cycles to *shen* and *you*. When the Fortune is struck, officials arrive; when the Horse is struck, illness is hard to prevent. If the Horse resides in the Illness Palace, favor its generation and combination; if the Fortune dwells in the Office Position, it must be lofty and grand. The roots of wealth and nobility lie in the Womb, yet maintenance favors the Long Life position. Arrangements must reside in the Flourishing direction, while verification favors the Repose and Extinction positions. I urge you: strike the Life, not the Womb—striking the Womb may damage the root’s origin. Only by striking from the Life position can maintenance be flexible and the celestial mechanism swift. I urge you: strike the Tomb, not the Flourishing—striking the Flourishing may render arrangements vain. Only by striking from the Tomb position can verification be natural and unobstructed.

Metal’s nobility lies within *yin* and *wu*; the Noble Person may be combined but not struck. Combining to activate the Noble Person and striking the Fortune—why fear wealth and nobility will not follow? Pray, how does one combine the Noble Person? For Metal’s nobility in *yin* and *wu*, use *xu* to combine.

The *yin* and *wu* directions hold auspicious stars; those born in the *xu* year find this most wondrous. If the deceased’s fate aligns with *mao* or *you*, wealth and nobility will converge in abundance.

Water and Fire prize their Tomb and Extinction positions; Metal’s nobility lies in □□, Water’s in Earth’s waxing and waning, and Earth’s in the *wei* Tomb and Extinction.

**Chapter Twenty-Eight: On Transformed Burial Sites**
Of the nineteen noble tombs, all are transformed types—how could common masters with vulgar eyes ever recognize them?

1. The first transformation: Fire scorches Metal, its molten essence flowing in a single thread. Only upon encountering Earth does the burial settle, its breast like a hanging gallbladder, true Metal revealed.
2. The second transformation: Wood chases Earth, Earth’s boulders transmuting into Metal’s offspring. Seek the nest where Fire lingers, and you will find a headrest and table before you.
3. The third transformation: Metal carves Wood, Wood morphing into a phoenix’s form or a chicken’s comb. At the sight of Water’s star, insertion is fitting—its subtle protrusion like a bud marks true Wood.
4. The fourth transformation: Earth dams Water, its torrents surging like a sloughing serpent. A Metal peak looms ahead, and the tomb rests serenely, as if suspended in a hollow.
5. The fifth transformation: Water extinguishes Fire. Fire’s star snuffed out like a water chestnut, seek the branching Wood’s nodes, where Water’s star hangs over the tomb’s hour.

Transformations through conquest favor the sight of the Father—see the Father, and the burial brings nobility and wealth. Transformations through generation favor the sight of Wealth—see Wealth, and riches and honor descend from heaven.

The subtleties of this wondrous art cannot be fully expounded; conquest and generation, generation and conquest—both must be arranged. Generative transformations delight in Wealth and Office; conquest delights in Father and Son. Thus, both are harmonized.

**Chapter Twenty-Nine: On the Essentials of Dragon-Seeking**
Do not say seeking the Dragon is hard—it is harder than gazing at high mountains from flat ground. Do not say it is easy—it is easier when viewing flat ground from high mountains.

1. Observe how the Dragon rises: where the true Dragon ascends, spirits gather.
2. Observe how the Dragon spreads: where the true Dragon expands, support abounds.
3. Observe how the Dragon crouches: where the true Dragon lies low, clusters form.
4. Observe how the Dragon moves: where the true Dragon travels, sentiment flows.
5. Observe the direction of its momentum: the direction it faces often conceals its path.

Where is this concealment made clear? Behind, there are senders; ahead, there are greeters. Yet the senders and greeters lie in the margins—guard the Guardian Dragon, but do not mistake it for the true Dragon. The true Dragon flashes and emerges; here, you must trace its roots. Examine carefully the Five Phases—Metal, Wood, Water, Fire, Earth—and identify the stars to name the Lesser Ancestor. If the Lesser Ancestor and the Remote Ancestor mutually generate, you will know this place’s power is layered. When the time is right, Father and Son, Wealth and Office follow—thus you know the lineage bears a thousand years of fortune.

When the Dragon moves with *qi* bound, you will know a true hall lies concealed. Having observed the Lesser Ancestor, next observe the pursuit: where Wealth and Office pursue, it is truly rare. If the Parents pursue, let them come leisurely; if the Children pursue, let them arrive in fullness. If they are neither leisurely nor full, prosperity will be fleeting. If you seek great wealth and nobility, Master Yang treasures only the pursuit of Wealth and Office. If it is merely I pursuing I, even sudden fortune brings no misfortune.

Having observed the Dragon, next observe where the Ghost descends—the Ghost’s descent reveals the stars’ spread. If the Ghost descends to generate the Dragon or the tomb, hidden blessings are truly worth celebrating. But if the Dragon or tomb generates the Ghost’s descent, it instead breeds thieving *qi*—a curse. Neither I conquering nor being conquered by it is fitting; better to harmonize and bear the load together.

In the plains, the Ghost’s descent should be pillowable; on high mountains, it should be reliable. Regardless of left, right, or center, observe where its sentiment embraces. Having observed the Ghost’s descent, next observe the tomb—distinguish between high and low, convex and concave. Observe where the *qi* enters: remember, burial must ride the generative *qi*. *Qi* entering through the ears is first; through the crown, second; through the waist, third. Beneath the transforming brain, recognize the eaves; the tomb’s sentiment should resemble a sprouting shoot. Above, a dimple; within, a trace; below, a plump lip—do not let it waste away. Shrimp whiskers and cicada wings press on both sides; chicken hearts and fish bubbles rise in mutual reflection.

Dragons of Earth and Wood bear many breasts and protrusions; Dragons of Metal and Water, bells and nests. Having observed the tomb, next observe its embrace. The embrace must harmonize with the Dragon’s spirit. If it embraces me with Wealth on both sides, descendants will wear golden belts at their waists. If it embraces me with Office on both sides, gold and jade will pile high, bringing a thousand blessings. If Parents embrace, descendants will be rare; if Children embrace, Parents will decline. Better that I embrace only myself—then living in ease brings no hardship.

Having observed the embrace, next observe the audience. What I generate and what generates me both align. If Wealth and Office also gather, the household’s wealth and nobility will know no end. Having observed the audience and the table, next observe the gates and doors—do not let descendants occupy them. First, Parents; second, Wealth; third, also welcome the Office star.

The auspicious person’s form is shaped by human hands—only arrange the head to face inward. Having examined Vermilion, Mysterious, Azure, and White, the sand and water, the Bright Hall, will naturally be fine. Water’s coming and going need not be constrained—seek clarity only in the directions. Its arrival should be leisurely, not hasty; its departure should be reluctant, as if loath to leave. If you seek great wealth and nobility, *xun*-water should flow ceaselessly to the mouth.

Land on high mountains is easy to manipulate; land on flat ground is often treacherous. When high mountains descend into the plains, always look for fur and bone. How to recognize fur? Where no crops grow, only grasses and reeds. How to recognize bone? Where earth and stone lie buried yet ever jut forth. Where there is fur and bone, there is protection; where there is protection, stars and spirits assist.

Where there is no *fengshui*, there is *fengshui*; where there is *fengshui*, there is no *fengshui*. Where there is no *fengshui*, eight or nine out of ten may be buried. Where there is *fengshui*, after searching the world, you will find scarcely one. This is the golden palm of Dragon-seeking—only those with keen eyes and clever minds can expound it.

**Chapter Thirty: On the Essentials of Residential and Burial Sites**

How can a family attain wealth and nobility? It must possess both a *yang* dwelling and a *yin* burial ground.
The *yang* is like a boat, the *yin* like its oar—when assessing the *yang*, first determine its orientation.
*Tai* and *Yang* aligned bring the root of wealth and nobility; *Sheng* and *Yu* open and expansive bring wealth and nobility to fruition.
*Guan* and *Dai* level and smooth bring pure wealth and nobility; *Guanwang* lofty and grand bring righteous wealth and nobility.
*Shuai* and *Bing* shielded bring secure wealth and nobility; *Si* open and receptive bring effortless wealth and nobility.
Burial sites that gather and store bring enduring wealth and nobility—auspicious spirits must align with their directions.
Yet one must also examine the connections between them, for the art of connection abounds with prohibitions.
*Tai*, *Yang*, and *Changsheng* must be separated—without separation, clarity is lost.
*Yu* and *Guan* despise hollows and protrusions—where hollows and protrusions exist, discord arises.
*Linguan* and *Diwang* should interlock—without interlocking, no scholarly or official success.
Between *Shuai* and *Wang*, avoid unevenness—one high, one low invites constant alarm.
*Si* and *Bing* require protection—without protection, jealousy and resentment arise.

The principles for *yin* and *yang* sites are the same: tombs and houses must achieve harmony with the stars.
If the *yin* site generates or restrains *yang*, descendants will enjoy wealth and nobility with grandeur.
If the *yin* site generates or restrains *yin*, descendants will attain wealth and nobility yet remain subdued.
But if *yin* and *yang* share the same *qi*, descendants’ wealth and nobility will endure without decline.

For *yang* dwellings, wealth and nobility require a root source, as well as maintenance and arrangement.
Every aspect must yield verification—only when the Five Auspices are complete does Heaven and Earth bestow favor.
Yet within these orientations, distinctions arise: *Sheng*, *Yang*, *Mu*, and *Wang* govern the four directions.
Even with a root source, without proper arrangement, fame will never reach the Phoenix Pool.
If Tiger Sand interlocks like a sash, official rank is revoked—no ten thousand strings of cash shall return.
With arrangement but no root source, why strive in vain for an official career?
If the Dragon’s head arches like a scholar’s cap, one may seek office through tribute or purchase.
With both root source and arrangement, one passes the imperial exams and returns in brocade robes.
If still not girded with a golden belt, know the burial site lacks support.
When support and arrangement are both present, and the root source aligns with its position, many will top the imperial exams.
Yet if one does not ascend to the Three Dukes, know the verification lacks convergence.

The *Hai* direction’s auspices favor descendants; the *Li* palace’s blessings fall upon those born in *Wu*.
Ascend the mountain and ponder these details—within your breast, the stars and immortals shall instruct.

*Confucian Principles and Harmonized Geomancy: A Record of Filial Piety, Scroll One, Concluded*

*Confucian Principles and Harmonized Geomancy: A Record of Filial Piety, Scroll Two*
**Chapter Thirty-One: On Selecting Auspicious Dates**

Those who practice geomancy concern themselves with but two matters: assessing terrain and selecting auspicious dates. Yet among the schools of this art, once they ascend the mountain, they abandon all discussion of terrain, seducing others instead with doctrines of celestial seals, astral configurations, and *qi* principles. The preceding scroll has already addressed this in full, and thus will not belabor the point, taking instead the proper alignment of form and *qi* as its guiding principle.

As for the selection of dates—here, the delusive theories that mislead are even more egregious. There are the so-called "various schools," the "thunder" methods, the "strange gates," each proliferating in wild and baseless profusion. All trace their origins to Lü Cai, whose *Comprehensive Almanac* disordered yin and yang, inverted the Five Phases, and so led practitioners to perpetuate errors, compounding distortions until the characters for *hai* and *shu* were confounded, their misreadings passed down without end.

During the Zhengde and Jiajing reigns, such bizarre doctrines spread even more widely. To this day, spurious texts defile the ox-boring pillars, deceiving not only petty men into leading others astray, but even gentlemen who, believing them, bring regret upon themselves. Blessings may not be secured, yet harm surely follows—what then becomes of the fulfillment of filial piety? Even when coincidental alignments occur, they arise from the hidden accumulations of virtuous households, not the power of delusive arts.

Master Yang once declared: *"Only when one comprehends the Five Phases in their inverted state does one become a Great Luo Immortal."* This holds true for Master Yang alone. Those who are not of his stature yet impose rigid interpretations upon these principles will invert mutual generation into mutual conquest, and mutual conquest into mutual generation, turning harmony into discord, utterly violating the fundamental principles of creation. How dare men employ such methods lightly?

Thus it is known: the hundred schools of miscellaneous doctrines are all illusions and falsehoods.

The profound principles of *Qimen*—who has truly grasped their subtle workings? Only the Seven Governors, transmitted since the time of Rong Cheng’s investiture, when Yao reverently observed the vast heavens to bestow the seasons upon humanity, and Shun aligned the Seven Governors with the jade pivot of the *xuanji*, have been the unvarying foundation by which successive dynasties regulated the calendar and illuminated the times. This principle, though subtle in its obscurity and manifest in its clarity, is none other than the true pattern of Heaven and Earth’s creative transformation. How then can men perversely abandon it in favor of strange and aberrant practices?

Little do they realize that the Five Movements and Six Qi are but the cyclical permutations of yin-yang and the Five Phases, while the Five Planets and Seven Luminaries are their radiant manifestations. The cyclical permutations flow ceaselessly through Heaven and Earth, bringing fortune or misfortune; the radiant manifestations shine perpetually within the cosmos, bestowing auspiciousness or inauspiciousness. Thus, in selection, one must first examine the qi movements to assess the dragon spirits, weighing their mutual generation, conquest, restraint, and transformation; then, by the positions of the stars, determine the orientation, discerning their stations of delay, concealment, or retrogression. These two considerations are the essentials of selection, grounded in the verifiable records of stellar and calendrical astronomy. Yet the flow of qi movements varies from dawn to dusk, and the distribution of stellar positions shifts from morning to evening. Only by consulting the Seven Governors’ almanac and mastering the methods of celestial reckoning can one attain the true secret of fate-creation and avoid regret. Other systems—such as the *Wenli*, *Wannianli*, or *Yizhangjing*—are unreliable. A deviation of a hair’s breadth leads to an error of a thousand miles; how can one not be cautious?

As for the mountain schools’ spirit-killings, one need only avoid the Three Killings and the Grand Year when they are in ascendance; when they are not, they pose no threat, and other killings may likewise be disregarded. Detailed explanations follow below.

**Chapter Thirty-Two: On Heavenly Timing**

The *Book of Changes* states: *"Supreme indeed is the generative power of Kun! All things owe their birth to it, as it follows the mandate of Heaven."* From this, we know that the vital qi within the Earth rides the celestial rhythms in due season. Thus, in spring and summer, the heavenly timing stirs the mechanism of emergence for the myriad things, and the Earth’s qi follows, unfolding its functions of growth and nurturance. In autumn and winter, the heavenly timing stirs the mechanism of withdrawal, and the Earth’s qi follows, gathering and storing. As the *Book of Changes* says: *"The Way of Earth achieves no completion, yet it ensures the fulfillment of all things."* This is the principle at work.

The dragon, cave, sand, and water are the movements of vital qi upon the Earth; the year, month, day, and hour are the revolutions of vital qi in Heaven. To secure the Earth’s favor without the Heaven’s is like desiring harvest in spring and summer or growth in autumn and winter—an impossibility. How can those who scrutinize the Earth below fail to gaze upon the Heavens above, thereby harmonizing yin and yang and aligning with the true pattern of their conjoined movements?

Yet one must not preconceive rigid configurations or predetermine transformations; rather, one should assess the flourishing or decline of the mountains, rivers, and qi movements in the moment, applying the methods of supplementation, assistance, conquest, or dispersion as needed. How could such dynamism be confined by the dead schematics of almanacs?

Someone may ask: Mencius said, *"Heavenly timing is not as valuable as terrestrial advantage, and terrestrial advantage is not as valuable as human harmony."* Thus, the insufficiency of heavenly timing is evident. Moreover, in ancient times, the Son of Heaven was buried after seven months, feudal lords after five, great officers after three, and scholars after a month. If one were bound by specific times and days, such customs could not be observed. Hence, some practitioners claim that once a site is secured, burial may proceed without regard to timing. Alas, this is mistaken.

Mencius spoke thus because the feudal lords of his time lacked human harmony, so he weighed their relative importance to enlighten them—not to advocate the complete abandonment of heavenly timing and terrestrial advantage. Otherwise, why would the sage kings have regulated the calendar and illuminated the seasons, or dug and constructed ponds?

In ancient times, the Son of Heaven was buried after seven months to await the arrival of feudal lords; feudal lords after five months to await their allies; great officers after three months to await their court peers; and scholars after a month to await their kinsfolk. Though these were royal decrees, the auspiciousness or inauspiciousness of burial was never neglected. Yet if one rigidly adheres to almanacs for selection, what is auspicious for one may be inauspicious for another, and what benefits the deceased may harm the living, leaving no burial opportunity for decades.

By using the Seven Governors for selection, one need only avoid the Three Killings and the Grand Year when they are in ascendance, allowing burials in every month of every year without conflict between heavenly timing and royal decree. Those practitioners who claim that once a site is secured, burial may proceed without regard to timing—are they not driven by a lack of principle, or perhaps an eagerness for profit? The wise must not fail to discern this.

**Chapter Thirty-Three: On Fate-Creation**

In the art of fate-creation, the day stem serves as the master, the month as the guiding thread, the year as the foundation, and the hour as the outcome. Once the Eight Characters are arranged, one examines which configuration they form, then applies the method accordingly. If the configuration is inauspicious, one should select another. This differs from astrology, for astrologers discuss fate as ordained by Heaven, immutable in its principle. In selection, however, fate is crafted by human agency, allowing one to choose the most favorable course.

I lament that those who practice fate-creation today overemphasize orientation, or the fate of the deceased, or the examination of the master, or insist on creating a new master—all of which are misguided. For the living, the moment of birth marks the origin of fate; if this origin is pure and unadulterated, lifelong prosperity is assured. For the dead, the moment of interment marks the origin of fate; if this origin is in ascendance, the ancestral lineage will endure. This principle cannot be gainsaid.

Yet if orientation is fixed at the dawn of creation, and life’s fate is determined at birth, then the fate of the deceased is already set, and the day of death lies ahead—all one can do is avoid direct clashes. To prioritize these factors in fate-creation is misguided. Therefore, the adept in fate-creation must select an auspicious Eight-Character configuration, scrutinize the qi, and reckon the stars, fulfilling both requirements to complete the entire scheme.

For the waxing and waning of qi form the foundation of fortune and misfortune. This principle underscores the paramount importance of stellar configurations, for they govern the manifestation or concealment of fortune’s handle—this, too, must not be taken lightly. Thus, when Wood is dominant, Wood must serve as the primary element; when Fire prevails, Fire must lead; when Earth holds sway, Earth must govern; when Metal is ascendant, Metal must preside; and when Water flows strong, Water must take precedence. The Four Pillars must harmonize to achieve balance, auspicious stars should occupy solid positions in their proper seasons, while inauspicious stars ought to fall into decline or void. Only then may the configuration be deemed viable. Should the Dragon Qi be amiss—either excessive or deficient—and the stellar alignments further deviate from harmony, even a configuration promising supreme wealth and nobility would be of no avail.

**Chapter Thirty-Four: On Configurations**
The method of fate-creation begins with determining the configuration, which may take the following forms: the Sun-Moon Opposition, the Sun-Moon Conjunction, the Trine Illumination, the Cardinal Quadrant Illumination, the Stem-Branch Enclosure, the Close Enclosure, the Imperial Star Enclosure, the Imperial Star Alignment, or the Triple Auspicious Illumination. Though configurations vary, the Sun and Moon must serve as the foundation—only then can it be considered a superior configuration.

The Sun-Moon Opposition typically occurs at the full moon; the Sun-Moon Conjunction, at the new moon; the Trine Illumination, around the tenth and twentieth days; the Cardinal Quadrant Illumination, also at the full moon; the Stem-Branch Enclosure, around the fifth, sixth, twenty-fifth, and twenty-sixth days; and the Close Enclosure, around the second, third, twenty-eighth, and twenty-ninth days. If the given day aligns with a configuration, every mountain may be utilized, though the timing will differ. For instance, at the fourth quarter of the *mao* hour, it may serve as the *zi* mountain; for a *wu* alignment, the lower fourth quarter may serve as the *gui* mountain. A *ding* alignment at the upper fourth quarter of the *chen* hour may serve as the *chou* mountain; a *wei* alignment at the lower first quarter of the *chen* hour may serve as the *gen* mountain. For a *kun* alignment, follow the sequence in due order—this principle applies to all twenty-four mountains. Only the mountains where the Three Killings ride in strength or where the Grand Year resides must be avoided. For configurations where the Imperial Star aligns with the Sun, only the *xu* hour is viable; all others are incompatible. Beware of solar eclipses on the new moon and lunar eclipses on the full moon—though before the eclipse or after its conclusion, no deep taboo applies.

Additional configurations—such as the Dual Virtues Illumination, the Official and Fortune Illumination, the Fields and Wealth Illumination, the Literary Eminence and Literary Star Illumination, and the Examination Success Illumination—are all noble, yet none surpass those where the Sun serves as the primary element. Nevertheless, these are but the carpenter’s square and the archer’s target—rules and standards for artisans. To wield the square to draw the circle, to grasp the bow to hit the mark, lies in the hands of the practitioner. Detailed diagrams follow.

**Chapter Thirty-Five: On Life and Destiny**
In the art of selection, fate-creation holds the greatest weight; and in fate-creation, the establishment of life and destiny is paramount. The Sun is as a father: one receives qi from the father, and thus life is born. Therefore, in establishing life, one must examine the degree where the Sun resides as the life degree. The Moon is as a mother: one takes form within the mother, and thus the body is born. Therefore, in establishing the body, one must examine the palace where the Moon resides as the body palace.

Modern astrologers who fix life at the *mao* hour by adding the Sun’s number to the birth time err. Those who take the Moon’s position on the given day as the body palace also err. For the Sun naturally moves leftward, traversing one palace per hour. Thus, around the winter solstice, when the Sun rises at the *chen* hour, one begins counting from *chen* and proceeds sequentially to the birth hour to determine the life palace. Around the summer solstice, when the Sun rises at the *yin* hour, one begins from *yin* and counts to the birth hour. At other times, when the Sun rises at the *mao* hour, one begins from *mao* and counts to the birth hour—this is the fixed method for establishing life.

The Moon also moves leftward, traversing slightly less than one palace per hour. Thus, one must first determine the hour and degree of the Moon’s position, then count sequentially from that degree, beginning with the birth hour, to locate the body palace. The same principle applies to other cases—this is the fixed method for establishing the body.

However, the Moon’s position varies between dawn and dusk, and even within these periods, gradual shifts occur. From the autumn equinox to the following spring equinox, the dawn position is said to be in *mao*, yet in truth, only the sixteenth, seventeenth, and eighteenth days lie in *mao*; the nineteenth and twentieth in *chen*; the twenty-first, twenty-second, and twenty-third in *si*; the twenty-fourth and twenty-fifth in *wu*; the twenty-sixth, twenty-seventh, and twenty-eighth in *wei*; and the twenty-ninth and thirtieth in *shen*. From the White Dew to the following Pure Brightness, the dusk position is said to be in *you*, yet in truth, only the first, second, and third days lie in *you*; the fourth and fifth in *xu*; the sixth, seventh, and eighth in *hai*; the ninth and tenth in *zi*; the eleventh, twelfth, and thirteenth in *chou*; and the fourteenth and fifteenth in *yin*. From the Grain Rain to the autumn equinox, the dusk position is said to be in *xu*—one must count sequentially from *xu*. From the Pure Brightness to the White Dew, the dusk position is said to be in *yin*—one must count from *yin*.

The Sun completes one revolution per day—how could it remain in *mao* at all hours? The Moon also completes one revolution per day, though it falls short by twelve degrees. How could it remain in the same palace at all times? This principle is self-evident, yet the wise fail to examine it.

This is why stellar calculations often fail to prove accurate, and those who craft destinies all lose the truth!

**Chapter Thirty-Six: On Discerning Qi**

In the methods of fengshui masters for discerning *qi*, one must first observe the grand trend of the dragon—from whence it comes and where the vein at its head halts. For instance, if the grand dragon comes from the south and, upon reaching its head, turns to face west, one should then supplement the vein at its head to form the grand wealth configuration. If one instead supplements the grand dragon’s body, the head will suffer restraint, and misfortune will swiftly follow. The rest follow this principle.

Or if the grand dragon comes from the east and, at its head, faces north, one should supplement the vein at its head to serve as the grand *shengqi* (generative *qi*). If one supplements the grand dragon’s body, it will drain the *qi* at the head, and the calamity of cold decline will soon follow. If the drainage is excessive, it will surely harm the inhabitants. The rest follow this principle.

Or if the grand dragon comes from the north and, at its head, faces east, one should only supplement the grand dragon’s body to serve as the *shengqi* at the head. If one supplements the vein at the head, it will drain the grand dragon’s *qi*. Though no immediate harm may appear, the seeds of decline and decay are sown therein. The rest follow this principle.

Nowadays, practitioners focus solely on the final segment at the head, treating its *qi* embryo as primary—this deceives people only for a time, but what of the later calamities?

In observing the grand trend, one must take the originating vein of the dragon as the foundation, disregarding its intermediate twists and turns. In examining the head, one must take the segment behind the site as the foundation, though what follows need not be rigidly observed.

Yet the Five *Qi* alternate in residence, having no fixed abode. If a dragon of balanced harmony dwells therein, the blessings it bestows will be great—even if the sitting mountain’s restraint or drainage poses no obstacle. But if a dragon of excess or deficiency dwells therein, the misfortune it brings will also be great, and even if the sitting mountain is prosperous, what benefit is there?

Thus, the ancients spoke of "occupying the substantial while riding the void," prioritizing the dragon’s spirit over rigid adherence to the sitting mountain’s yin-yang and Five Phases. One need only ensure that auspicious stars arrive at the sitting position, or at the facing direction, or at the three harmonies, or at the four cardinals, or at the palaces of mutual embrace—all are auspicious.

As for inauspicious stars, one need only avoid their periods of dominance. When such stars are weak or in decline, they pose no threat; discretion lies with the practitioner.

**Chapter Thirty-Seven: On Calculating Stars**

In the method of calculating stars, one must rely primarily on the *Seven Governors’ Almanac*, applying it to whichever direction the mountain occupies. The Sun, Moon, Emperor Star, and Grace Star are all supremely auspicious deities. The Commanding Star, Utilized Star, Fortune Star, and Auspicious Constellations are also propitious. The Yearly Virtue, Heavenly Virtue, Monthly Virtue, Monthly Union, Noble Persons, Grandchildren and Horses, Fields and Wealth Palaces, Literary Brilliance, Scholarly Honors, and Imperial Examination Stars—all are auxiliary auspicious deities. Whether they arrive at the mountain, the facing direction, the three harmonies, the four cardinals, a timely and useful palace, or a palace of mutual embrace, all are auspicious omens.

As for the Adversary Star, Calamity Star, Three Killings, Great Killing, and Grand Year Deities—all must be avoided. If they dominate and occupy favorable positions, their harm will be great; if they are weak, in decline, or void, they need not be feared.

For the essential method of selection is this: pursue the auspicious and avoid the inauspicious. Moreover, the heavens display omens of fortune and misfortune precisely through the Seven Governors. How can one neglect the method of calculating stars?

**Chapter Thirty-Eight: On the Two and Five**

I have heard that the Supreme Ultimate gives rise to yin and yang, yin and yang give rise to the Five Phases, and through the transformations of the Five Phases, the generation and completion of the myriad things are perfected. This is the unchanging principle of creation, constant across a hundred thousand ages.

In heaven, it manifests in the sun, moon, and stars, with the Five Phases arrayed in order. On earth, it appears in mountains, rivers, and living things, with the Five *Qi* flowing and circulating. In humans, it is expressed as vigor and compliance forming virtue, from which the Five Natures and Five Relationships emerge.

At the dawn of creation, this principle was revealed in the *River Chart* and *Luo Writing*, awakening the divine wisdom of the sages. Their purpose was to elucidate the essence of yin-yang and the Five Phases, guiding the people toward the path of seeking fortune and avoiding misfortune.

Observe the *Chart*: the number fifty resides at the center, while the *Writing* places five at the center—both represent the Supreme Ultimate. The *Chart* and *Writing* each contain twenty odd numbers and twenty even numbers—these are the numbers of yin and yang. In the *Chart*, one and six reside in the north, belonging to Water; two and seven in the south, belonging to Fire; three and eight in the east, belonging to Wood; four and nine in the west, belonging to Metal; five and ten in the center, belonging to Earth, with their prosperity delegated to the four corners.

Examining the *Luo Writing* after its transposition, the numbers of generation and restraint likewise interweave with the *River Chart*. Thus, from the Three Sovereigns to the hundred kings, all who regulated calendars and illuminated the seasons relied upon these principles.

Today, calendar makers depend on them to calculate celestial movements; physicians rely on them to investigate the *qi* and pulse; astrologers use them to predict human longevity, prosperity, and adversity. Moreover, fengshui serves to align earthly patterns with heavenly timing, forming the foundation of blessings for the people. How could one defy the correct principles of creation and follow absurd doctrines?

Alas, Lü Cai’s *Hongfan* Five Phases deviated from the true principle, and his theories of disputing yin and prioritizing yang were especially perverse. He failed to recognize that the Two and Five are rooted in the original state. Yet once one man set the precedent, later practitioners followed in his footsteps, ensnaring fleeting profit while sowing lasting harm. The people suffer the consequences, unaware, while the practitioners reap the gains without remorse—is this not deeply lamentable?

Our Taizu, the August High Emperor, soared like a dragon over the Huai region, reopening the cosmos in its renewal and awakening the benighted from their wandering paths. Fearing that the dozens of almanacs and divination manuals were all harming the people, in the ninth year of the Hongwu reign, he further prohibited their fraudulent fabrication, destroyed all counterfeit books, and commanded supervising officials to correct and select the calendar for promulgation throughout the realm, establishing a model for posterity—all to clarify the true transmission of yin and yang and reveal the profound mirror for seeking fortune and avoiding misfortune. Thus, a summary is set forth here.

**Chapter Thirty-Nine: On Mountain Orientations and the Two and Five**
Observing the odd and even numbers of the *Hetu*, one discerns the origin of yin-yang and the Five Phases. Their mutual generation and completion each attain the marvel of spontaneity, all being the essence of heaven and earth’s transformative nurturance, disclosed to humanity. For instance:
- Heaven’s One generates Water, Earth’s Six completes it, residing in the north at the *hai* and *zi* positions. Thus, from *hai* Palace’s *kui* 1° to *zi* Palace’s *wei* 7°, all pertain to yin Water. From *zi* Palace’s *wei* 6° to *chou*’s *dou* 22°, all pertain to yang Water.
- Earth’s Two generates Fire, Heaven’s Seven completes it, residing in the south at the *si* and *wu* positions. Thus, from *si* Palace’s *han* 9° to *wu*’s *zhang* 9°, all pertain to yin Fire. From *wu*’s *zhang* 8° to *wei*’s *jing* 27°, all pertain to yang Fire.
- Heaven’s Three generates Wood, Earth’s Eight completes it, residing in the east at the *yin* and *mao* positions. Thus, from *yin* Palace’s *dou* 3° to *mao*’s *xin* 3°, all pertain to yang Wood. From *mao*’s *xin* 2° to *chen*’s *jiao* 11°, all pertain to yin Wood.
- Earth’s Four generates Metal, Heaven’s Nine completes it, residing in the west at the *shen* and *you* positions. Thus, from *shen* Palace’s *jing* 8° to *you*’s *bi* 1°, all pertain to yang Metal. From *you*’s *bi* 0° to *xu*’s *lou* 4°, all pertain to yin Metal.
- Heaven’s Five generates Earth, Earth’s Ten completes it, though residing in the central palace, its *qi* flourishes in the four corners. Thus, the 18° of *chen* and *wei*, along with the final 18° of *xu*, all pertain to yang Earth. The final 18° of *chou* and *wei* all pertain to yin Earth.

The celestial circle comprises 365¼°, with each of the Five Phases allotted slightly over 72°, all being natural numbers, fixed and immutable. The twelve celestial palaces are demarcated between the earthly branches, yet though they occupy their positions, their affiliations remain unchanged. For example:
- *Qian* lies between *xu* and *hai*: the right 7½° in *xu* are reckoned as yang Earth, while the left 7½° in *hai* are reckoned as yin Water.
- *Ren* lies between *hai* and *zi*: the right 7½° in *hai* are reckoned as yin Water, while the left 7½° in *zi* are reckoned as yang Water. The rest follow this pattern.

Moreover, earlier scholars held that the Eight Trigrams merely elucidate the circulation of heavenly *qi* and never altered the yin-yang or Five Phases affiliations of the earthly branches. The ancient luopan compass featured only the twelve earthly branches, conforming to the fixed numbers of the *Hetu*. Later generations added the eighty-two celestial stems merely to complete the twenty-four positions, corresponding to the twenty-four solar terms of the year. To alter the yin-yang or Five Phases of the earthly branches based on the celestial stems is incorrect. The Eight Trigrams merely verify the movement of heavenly *qi*; to use them to alter the yin-yang or Five Phases of the earthly branches is likewise incorrect.

In later ages, failing to grasp this mechanism, some devised the fallacious "celestial star trigram examples," greatly deluding the world. Scholars, too, failed to examine these critically and instead followed them blindly, even contriving forced interpretations and conflating them with the sagely teachings to obscure the truth. They did not realize that the principles of yin-yang and the Five Phases are the wondrous mechanism of heaven and earth’s creation, not something human intellect or effort can augment or diminish.

**Chapter Forty: On the Seven Governors**
When the sun and moon conjoin, it is called *shuo* (new moon); when they oppose, it is called *wang* (full moon); when the moon’s light is evenly divided, it is called *xian* (quarter moon); when the moon’s light is entirely extinguished, it is called *hui* (dark moon). The five planets hide when they meet the sun, halt when in triple conjunction, move swiftly when near the sun, and slowly when far from it. When their slowness peaks, they halt; after halting long, they retreat; after retreating long, they proceed in direct motion.

The sun fears *Rahu*, the moon fears *Ketu*; Wood fears Metal in its flourishing, Fire fears Water in its flourishing, Earth fears Wood in its flourishing, Metal fears Fire in its flourishing, Water fears Earth in its flourishing. The sun favors daylight, the moon favors night; flourishing Wood favors Metal, flourishing Fire favors Water, flourishing Earth favors Wood, flourishing Metal favors Fire, flourishing Water favors Earth. Weak Water cherishes Metal, weak Metal cherishes Earth, weak Earth cherishes Fire, weak Fire cherishes Wood, weak Wood cherishes Water. Declining Water does not generate Wood, declining Wood does not generate Fire, declining Fire does not generate Earth, declining Earth does not generate Metal, declining Metal does not generate Water. In spring, Earth does not overcome Water; in summer, Metal does not overcome Wood; in the late seasons, Water does not overcome Fire; in autumn, Wood does not overcome Earth; in winter, Fire does not overcome Metal. These are all essential methods of the Seven Governors; scholars cannot be ignorant of them.

**Chapter Forty-One: On Crossing Palaces**
The constellations arrayed in the heavens number twenty-eight lodges, totaling 365¼°, though they rise and set, they do not truly shift. The heavens revolve leftward, completing one circuit daily and advancing one degree. The sun, moon, five planets, *qi*, and comets all proceed in direct motion. Only *Ketu* and *Rahu* revolve rightward—this is the Confucians’ unchanging doctrine, even the *Gexiang Xinshu* expounds it in detail. However, calendar makers, finding it inconvenient for computation, claim: "The heavens revolve leftward, while the sun, moon, and five planets all revolve rightward." The sequence of the twenty palaces’ degrees is counted from left to right for swift calculation—this is the method of calendar-making.

To select an auspicious date, one must adhere to the former doctrine to ensure precise timing. In all cases, the seven luminaries proceed in direct motion from right to left, with only *Rahu* and *Ketu* moving in reverse. For example:

- When *Rahu* is at 2° in *Nü* (女), it moves to 1° past *Chou* (丑);
- When *Dipper* (斗) is at 4° in *Chou*, it moves to 3° past *Yin* (寅);
- When *Tail* (尾) is at 2° in *Yin*, it moves to 1° past *Mao* (卯);
- When *Root* (氐) is at 2° in *Mao*, it moves to 1° past *Chen* (辰);
- When *Winnowing Basket* (輸) is at 10° in *Chen*, it moves to 9° past *Si* (巳);
- When *Spread* (張) is at 15° in *Si*, it moves to 14° past *Wu* (午);
- When *Willow* (柳) is at 4° in *Wu*, it moves to 3° past *Wei* (未);
- When *Well* (井) is at 9° in *Wei*, it moves to 8° past *Shen* (申);
- When *Net* (畢) is at 7° in *Shen*, it moves to 6° past *You* (酉);
- When *Stomach* (胃) is at 4° in *You*, it moves to 3° past *Xu* (戌);
- When *Straddler* (婁) is at 2° in *Xu*, it moves to 1° past *Hai* (亥);
- When *Rooftop* (危) is at 13° in *Hai*, it moves to 12° past *Zi* (子).

The boundaries of the celestial divisions are as follows:
- 5° in *Rooftop* marks the boundary of *Ren-Zi* (壬子);
- 9° in *Woman* (女) marks the boundary of *Zi-Gui* (子癸);
- 1° in *Ox* (牛) marks the boundary of *Gui-Chou* (癸丑);
- 11° in *Dipper* marks the boundary of *Chou-Gen* (丑艮);
- 6° in *Winnowing Basket* (箕) marks the boundary of *Gen-Yin* (艮寅);
- 11° in *Tail* marks the boundary of *Yin-Jia* (寅甲);
- 1° in *Heart* (心) marks the boundary of *Jia-Mao* (甲卯);
- 9° in *Root* marks the boundary of *Mao-Yi* (卯乙);
- 2° in *Neck* (亢) marks the boundary of *Yi-Chen* (乙辰);
- 17° in *Wings* (翰) marks the boundary of *Chen-Xun* (辰巽);
- 2° in *Dry* (干) marks the boundary of *Xun-Si* (巽巳);
- 6° in *Pivot* (斡) marks the boundary of *Si-Bing* (巳丙);
- 7° in *Spread* marks the boundary of *Bing-Wu* (丙午);
- 12° in *Willow* marks the boundary of *Wu-Ding* (午丁);
- The initial degree of *Ghost* (鬼) marks the boundary of *Ding-Wei* (丁未);
- The *Well*’s *Dou* (井斗) marks the boundary of *Wei-Kun* (未坤);
- 1° in *Well* marks the boundary of *Kun-Shen* (坤申);
- 14° in *Net* marks the boundary of *Shen-Geng* (申庚);
- 10° in *Pleiades* (昴) marks the boundary of *Geng-You* (庚酉);
- 11° in *Stomach* marks the boundary of *You-Xin* (酉辛);
- 8° in *Straddler* marks the boundary of *Xin-Xu* (辛戌);
- 9° in *Legs* (奎) marks the boundary of *Xu-Qian* (戌乾);
- 3° in *Wall* (壁) marks the boundary of *Qian-Hai* (乾亥);
- 5° in *House* (室) marks the boundary of *Hai-Ren* (亥壬).

Though the numbering appears reversed, the motion is in fact direct. The midpoint between 7° and 6° in *Void* (虛) is the center of *Zi* (子), while the midpoint between 6° and 5° in *Star* (星) is the center of *Wu* (午). The axis of transition between *Zi* and *Wu* is the pivot of palace traversal. While the sequence of degrees in each palace should ideally be corrected to proceed leftward in direct motion, this may startle beginners, so the old method may still be followed—provided that calculations are made with meticulous care.

**Chapter Forty-Two: On Determining Time**
Geomancy divides the land into forty-four mountains, while calendrical texts divide the day into twenty-four hours, each corresponding to a mountain. To determine time accurately, one must examine each hour and quarter-hour to ascertain the degrees of the constellations’ positions; otherwise, the timing will surely be amiss.

The twelve Earthly Branches denote fixed divisions, while the twelve Heavenly Stems occupy the intervals between them. For example:
- From the third quarter of *Hai* (亥) to the second quarter of *Zi* (子) belongs to *Ren* (壬);
- From the third quarter of *Zi* to the second quarter of *Zi* proper belongs to *Zi*;
- From the third quarter of *Zi* to the second quarter of *Chou* (丑) belongs to *Gui* (癸);
- From the third quarter of *Chou* to the second quarter of *Chou* proper belongs to *Chou*;
- From the third quarter of *Chou* to the second quarter of *Yin* (寅) belongs to *Gen* (艮);
- From the third quarter of *Yin* to the second quarter of *Yin* proper belongs to *Yin*;
- From the third quarter of *Yin* to the second quarter of *Mao* (卯) belongs to *Jia* (甲);
- From the third quarter of *Mao* to the second quarter of *Mao* proper belongs to *Mao*;
- From the third quarter of *Mao* to the second quarter of *Chen* (辰) belongs to *Yi* (乙);
- From the third quarter of *Chen* to the second quarter of *Chen* proper belongs to *Chen*;
- From the third quarter of *Chen* to the second quarter of *Si* (巳) belongs to *Xun* (巽);
- From the third quarter of *Si* to the second quarter of *Si* proper belongs to *Si*;
- From the third quarter of *Si* to the second quarter of *Wu* (午) belongs to *Bing* (丙);
- From the third quarter of *Wu* to the second quarter of *Wu* proper belongs to *Wu*;
- From the third quarter of *Wu* to the second quarter of *Wei* (未) belongs to *Ding* (丁);
- From the third quarter of *Wei* to the second quarter of *Wei* proper belongs to *Wei*;
- From the third quarter of *Wei* to the second quarter of *Shen* (申) belongs to *Kun* (坤);
- From the third quarter of *Shen* to the second quarter of *Shen* proper belongs to *Shen*;
- From the third quarter of *Shen* to the second quarter of *You* (酉) belongs to *Geng* (庚);
- From the third quarter of *You* to the second quarter of *You* proper belongs to *You*;
- From the third quarter of *You* to the second quarter of *Xu* (戌) belongs to *Xin* (辛);
- From the third quarter of *Xu* to the second quarter of *Xu* proper belongs to *Xu*;
- From the third quarter of *Xu* to the second quarter of *Hai* belongs to *Qian* (乾);
- From the third quarter of *Hai* to the second quarter of *Hai* proper belongs to *Hai*.

A day consists of twelve hours, totaling one hundred quarters. Each hour spans slightly more than eight quarters, hence the distinction between initial quarters and fractional quarters. When the twelve Heavenly Stems are included, each hour comprises just over four quarters.

For instance, in the first month, if the sun is at *Zi* (子) during the third quarter of *Mao* (卯), then *Yi* (乙) must correspond to *Gui* (癸), *Chen* (辰) to *Chou* (丑), and *Si* (巳) to *Gen* (艮), with the pattern continuing accordingly. Each day, the reckoning begins from the sun’s position at *Mao*, advancing one mountain per hour. However, around the Winter Solstice, the reckoning begins at *Chen* (辰), while around the Summer Solstice, it begins at *Yin* (寅).

The seven luminaries and comets all revolve leftward with time, except for *Ketu* and *Rahu*, which move rightward against the flow. This is the essential method for examining time to determine the positions of the stars—a principle scholars must not overlook.

**Chapter Forty-Three: On Astronomy**
Master Zhu said: The celestial Way, along with the sun, moon, and five planets, all revolve leftward. The celestial Way completes one circuit daily, always advancing by one degree.

The sun likewise completes one circuit of the heavens daily, beginning at the starting degree and ending at the starting degree. Thus, compared to the celestial Way, it always falls short by one degree. The moon’s motion falls short by thirteen and seven nineteenths of a degree. Modern scholars, however, claim that the moon moves swiftly while the sun moves slowly—this is a mistaken view. Astronomers, for the sake of convenience in observing the degrees of the sun and moon, adopt the theory of rightward rotation. Yet from Master Zhu’s words, we know that the seven luminaries all revolve leftward. Those who select auspicious times must not be ignorant of this. Today’s astrologers have strayed far from the truth.

Master Wu of Linchuan said: Since ancient times, astronomers have not been unaware that the seven luminaries also move leftward. However, their forward motion cannot be uniformly measured, so they calculate their retrograde motion to align with the celestial degrees, leading later generations to mistakenly believe that the sun, moon, and five planets move in reverse. Now, imagine an empty disk in the vastness of space, upon which the heavens and the seven luminaries move at varying speeds. The celestial Way moves fastest, advancing one degree beyond the empty disk each day. Saturn’s motion is slightly slower than the heavens; though it advances somewhat on the disk, over twenty-eight months, it falls thirty degrees short of the heavens. Jupiter’s motion is slower still; over twelve months, it lags thirty degrees behind the heavens. Mars moves even more slowly; over sixty days, it falls thirty degrees short. The sun’s motion is slower than Mars’s; on the celestial disk, it completes one full circuit daily without excess or deficit, yet compared to the heavens, it falls one degree short each day. Over one month, it falls thirty degrees short of the heavens. Venus moves slightly slower than the sun, though when its swift and slow phases balance, it matches the sun’s motion. Mercury moves slower than Venus, and when its swift and slow phases balance, it matches Venus’s motion. The moon moves slowest of all, sometimes falling twelve, thirteen, or fourteen degrees short of the heavens in a single day. Because its motion is so slow, its retrograde degree is greatest. Unaware of this, people mistake its motion for reverse movement and claim the moon is the fastest. Now, ranking their speeds: the heavens first, Saturn second, Jupiter third, Mars fourth, the sun fifth, Venus sixth, Mercury seventh, and the moon eighth. The heavens, Saturn, Jupiter, and Mars move faster than the sun, while Venus, Mercury, and the moon move slower. This is the general principle. Examining these two accounts, we see that the heavens and the seven luminaries undoubtedly revolve leftward. Those who wish to master the subtle applications of time selection must not fail to investigate this.

Master Zhou of Taihe said: The heavens, being of pure yang essence, revolve leftward, completing one circuit each day and night while advancing an additional degree. The point where it advances becomes one degree of the celestial sphere, which is how the three hundred sixty-five and one-quarter degrees of the heavens originate. Saturn’s motion is next, falling thirty degrees short of the heavens over twenty-eight months. Jupiter follows, falling thirty degrees short over twelve months. Mars follows, falling thirty degrees short over two months. The sun follows, completing one circuit of the heavens daily but falling one degree short of the heavens. Over three hundred sixty-five and one-quarter days, the heavens’ advance precisely matches its original degree, while the sun’s lag also completes its original count, reuniting with the heavens at the initial cycle to form one year. Venus follows the sun, and Mercury follows Venus, though at times their swift and slow phases balance, allowing them to match the sun’s motion. The moon is the slowest, falling thirteen and seven nineteenths of a degree short of the heavens each day. Over twenty-nine and four hundred ninety-nine nine hundred fortieths of a day, it reunites with the sun to form one month. Twelve such conjunctions yield three hundred fifty-four and three hundred forty-eight nine hundred fortieths of a day, making one year. The moon’s motion follows this pattern. The heavens, sun, moon, and five planets all revolve leftward around the earth. The ancients spoke only of the heavens revolving leftward because the forward motion of the seven luminaries follows the heavens to the left, while their retrograde motion opposes the heavens to the right. Astronomers, finding the forward motion’s varying speeds difficult to calculate, instead track the retrograde motion for its simplicity in prediction, calling it rightward movement. Cai’s *Nine Spheres Calendar* from the Western Regions follows the same principle, so they must have known that all revolve leftward.

In the *Zi* month, the sun is in *Dou* (Dipper); at dusk, *Bi* (Wall) culminates, and at dawn, *Zhen* (Wing) culminates, with the moon conjoining the sun in *Xingji* (Stellar Mark).
In the *Chou* month, the sun is in *Nü* (Woman); at dusk, *Lou* (Harvester) culminates, and at dawn, *Shi* (House) culminates, with the moon conjoining the sun in *Xuanxiao* (Mystic Void).
In the *Yin* month, the sun is in *Shi* (House); at dusk, *Shen* (Triaster) culminates, and at dawn, *Wei* (Tail) culminates, with the moon conjoining the sun in *Juzhi* (Horned Branch).
In the *Mao* month, the sun is in *Kui* (Straddler); at dusk, *Hu* (Bow) culminates, and at dawn, *Jian* (Establishment) culminates, with the moon conjoining the sun in *Jianglou* (Descending Tower).
In the *Chen* month, the sun is in *Wei* (Stomach); at dusk, *Xing* (Star) culminates, and at dawn, *Dou* (Dipper) culminates, with the moon conjoining the sun in *Daliang* (Great Bridge).
In the *Si* month, the sun is in *Bi* (Net); at dusk, *Yi* (Wing) culminates, and at dawn, *Nü* (Woman) culminates, with the moon conjoining the sun in *Richen* (Sun’s Sinking).
In the *Wu* month, the sun is in *Jing* (Well); at dusk, *Kang* (Neck) culminates, and at dawn, *Wei* (Rooftop) culminates, with the moon conjoining the sun in *Chunshou* (Quail’s Head).
In the *Wei* month, the sun is in *Liu* (Willow); at dusk, *Huo* (Fire) culminates, and at dawn, *Kui* (Straddler) culminates, with the moon conjoining the sun in *Chunhuo* (Quail’s Fire).
In the *Shen* month, the sun is in *Yi* (Wing); at dusk, *Jian* culminates, and at dawn, *Bi* (Net) culminates, with the moon conjoining the sun in *Chunwei* (Quail’s Tail).
In the *You* month, the sun is in *Jiao* (Horn); at dusk and dawn, *Zi* (Turtle Beak) culminates, with the moon conjoining the sun in *Shouxing* (Longevity Star).
In the *Xu* month, the sun is in *Fang* (Room); at dusk, *Xu* (Void) culminates, and at dawn, *Liu* (Willow) culminates, with the moon conjoining the sun in *Dahuo* (Great Fire).
In the *Hai* month, the sun is in *Wei* (Tail); at dusk and dawn, *Xing* (Star) culminates, with the moon conjoining the sun in *Ximu* (Divided Tree).

All the above are indispensable for the practice of time selection.
