**Chapter Forty-Four: On the Movements of Heaven**

The primordial *qi* of Heaven is formless, yet by observing the handle of the Dipper, one may know the celestial stems it points to. The Dipper consists of seven stars: the first is *Kui* (the Ladle), the fifth is *Heng* (the Balance), and the seventh is *Biao* (the Handle)—these three stars are called the "handle of the Dipper." For example, in the month of *Jianyin*, at dusk the handle points to *Yin*, at midnight the balance points to *Yin*, and at dawn the ladle points to *Yin*; other months follow this pattern.

When the Dipper points to the *Yin* and *Mao* months, it is auspicious to construct the Eastern Dragon. When it points to the *Si* and *Wu* months, it is auspicious to construct the Southern Dragon. When it points to the *Shen* and *You* months, it is auspicious to construct the Western Dragon. When it points to the *Hai* and *Zi* months, it is auspicious to construct the Northern Dragon. When it points to the *Chen*, *Xu*, *Chou*, and *Wei* months, it is auspicious to construct the Four-Corner Dragons.

The direction of the Dipper’s handle is where the primordial *qi* of Heaven resides. By aligning with this *qi* and acting accordingly, blessings come swiftly—like planting rice in spring and wheat in autumn, which invariably grow.

---

**Chapter Forty-Five: On the Constellations**

The *Xuanxiao* mansion corresponds to the *Zi* (Rat) stem. This is the *fenye* (territorial correlate) of Qi, belonging to Qingzhou. Today, this includes Dengzhou, Laizhou, Qingzhou, Jinan, and Dongchang in Shandong.

The *Xingji* mansion corresponds to the *Chou* (Ox) stem. This is the *fenye* of Wu-Yue, belonging to Yangzhou. Today, this includes the fourteen prefectures of Nanjing, the eleven prefectures of Zhejiang, the thirty prefectures of Jiangxi, the ten prefectures of Guangdong, Wuzhou in Guangxi, and all prefectures of Fujian.

The *Ximu* mansion corresponds to the *Yin* (Tiger) stem. This is the *fenye* of Yan, belonging to Youzhou. Today, this includes Hejian, Baoding, Yongping, Longqing, Xuanfu, and Bao’an in Northern Zhili, as well as Liaodong.

The *Dahuo* mansion corresponds to the *Mao* (Hare) stem. This is the *fenye* of Song, belonging to Yuzhou. Today, this includes the lands of Feng and Pei in Southern Zhili.

The *Shouxing* mansion corresponds to the *Chen* (Dragon) stem. This is the *fenye* of Zheng, belonging to Yanzhou. Today, this includes Kaifeng and Runing in Henan.

The *Chunwei* mansion corresponds to the *Si* (Snake) stem. This is the *fenye* of Chu, belonging to Jingzhou. Today, this includes Nanyang in Henan, Hanzhong in Shaanxi, the ten prefectures of Wuchang in Huguang, half of Kuizhou in Sichuan, and Guilin and Nanning in Guangxi.

The *Chunhuo* mansion corresponds to the *Wu* (Horse) stem. This is the *fenye* of Zhou, belonging to Yingzhou. Today, this includes one prefecture in Henan; Liu (part of *Liuzhen*) belongs to Tongren in Guizhou, and *Xingzhang* belongs to Nanyang in Henan.

The *Chunshou* mansion corresponds to the *Wei* (Sheep) stem. This is the *fenye* of Qin, belonging to Yongzhou. Today, this includes Luoyang in Henan, Xiping, Gongchang, Yan, Sui, and Ningxia in Shaanxi, as well as Yunnan and Hejunminfu.

The *Shichen* mansion corresponds to the *Shen* (Monkey) stem. This is the *fenye* of Jin-Wei, belonging to Liangzhou. Today, this includes Taiyuan, Datong, Pingyang, and Zelu in Shanxi; Shunqing, Yuwan, and Diexi in Sichuan; the fifteen prefectures of Yunnan; and the eight prefectures of Guizhou.

The *Daliang* mansion corresponds to the *You* (Rooster) stem. This is the *fenye* of Zhao, belonging to Jizhou. Today, this includes Zhenghding, Shunde, and Guangping in Northern Zhili.

The *Jianglou* mansion corresponds to the *Xu* (Dog) stem. This is the *fenye* of Lu, belonging to Xuzhou. Today, this includes Yanzhou in Shandong.

The *Juzī* mansion corresponds to the *Hai* (Pig) stem. This is the *fenye* of Wei, belonging to Bianzhou. Today, this includes Daming in Northern Zhili, and Zhangde, Huaiqing, and Weihui in Henan.

When constructing or burying, to determine a person’s scholarly rank or official post, one must not be ignorant of the following:

The four lodges *Jiao* (Horn), *Dou* (Dipper), *Kui* (Straddler), and *Jing* (Well) belong to Wood. Their *qi* aligns with winter, flourishes in spring. If Fire transits their degrees, it brings favor; if Metal transits, it brings enmity; if Water transits, it brings utility; if Earth transits, it brings difficulty. In summer and autumn, they are weak and in decline, and their fortunes are light.

The four lodges *Kang* (Neck), *Niu* (Ox), *Lou* (Harvester), and *Gui* (Ghost) belong to Metal. Their *qi* aligns with late summer, flourishes in autumn. If Water transits their degrees, it brings favor; if Fire transits, it brings enmity; if Earth transits, it brings utility; if Wood transits, it brings difficulty. In winter and spring, they are weak and in decline, and their fortunes are light.

The four lodges *Di* (Root), *Nü* (Girl), *Wei* (Stomach), and *Liu* (Willow) belong to Earth. Their *qi* aligns with summer, flourishes in late summer. If Metal transits their degrees, it brings favor; if Wood transits, it brings enmity; if Fire transits, it brings utility; if Water transits, it brings difficulty. In autumn and winter, they are weak and in decline, and their fortunes are shallow.

The four lodges *Fang* (Room), *Xu* (Void), *Xing* (Star), and *Mao* (Pleiades) belong to the Greater Yang. They are auspicious by day but inauspicious by night.

The four lodges *Xin* (Heart), *Wei* (Rooftop), *Bi* (Net), and *Zhang* (Spread) belong to the Greater Yin. They are auspicious by night but inauspicious by day.

The four lodges *Wei* (Tail), *Shi* (House), *Zi* (Turtle Beak), and *Yi* (Wing) belong to Fire. Their *qi* aligns with spring, flourishes in summer. If Earth transits their degrees, it brings favor; if Water transits, it brings enmity; if Wood transits, it brings utility; if Metal transits, it brings difficulty. In autumn and winter, they are weak and in decline, and their fortunes are also shallow.

The four lodges *Ji* (Winnowing Basket), *Bi* (Wall), *Shen* (Triaster), and *Wo* (Triaster) belong to Water. Their *qi* aligns with autumn, flourishes in winter. If Wood transits their degrees, it brings favor; if Earth transits, it brings enmity; if Metal transits, it brings utility; if Fire transits, it brings difficulty. In summer and autumn, they are weak and in decline, and their fortunes are also not heavy.

In all cases above, favor, utility, and flourishing bring auspiciousness, while weakness and decline bring misfortune. Enmity and difficulty, when flourishing, bring misfortune, but when weak and in decline, they bring auspiciousness.

---

**Chapter Forty-Six: On the Latitudinal Stars**

The sun is the essence of the Greater Yang and the symbol of the sovereign.

Its body spans one and a half degrees in diameter. Moving from east to west, it completes one celestial revolution each day, falling one degree short of the heavens’ motion. The path it traverses is called the *Yellow Way* (ecliptic), which intersects the *Red Way* (celestial equator). Half lies outside the Yellow Way, half within. On the winter solstice, the Yellow Way extends twenty-four degrees beyond the Red Way, reaching its farthest point from the North Pole. The sun rises at *chen* (7–9 a.m.) and sets at *shen* (3–5 p.m.), hence the cold season, short days, and long nights. On the summer solstice, the Yellow Way lies twenty-four degrees within the Red Way, closest to the North Pole. The sun rises at *yin* (3–5 a.m.) and sets at *xu* (7–9 p.m.), hence the hot season, long days, and short nights. At the spring and autumn equinoxes, the Yellow Way intersects the Red Way midway between the two poles. The sun rises at *mao* (5–7 a.m.) and sets at *you* (5–7 p.m.), hence the mild season and equal day and night.

The moon is the essence of the Greater Yin and the symbol of the empress and consorts. Its body also spans one and a half degrees in diameter. Moving from east to west, it completes one revolution daily, falling thirteen and thirty-seven hundredths degrees short of the heavens’ motion. In twenty-seven days, it retreats one full revolution. The path it traverses is called the *White Way* (lunar orbit), which intersects the Yellow Way, half lying within and half beyond, deviating no more than six degrees—just as the Yellow Way deviates twenty-four degrees from the Red Way.

The yang essence resembles fire, the yin essence resembles water. Fire emits light, while water reflects shadows. Thus, the moon’s light arises where the sun shines upon it, and its dark *po* (lunar essence) arises where the sun does not shine. When aligned with the sun, it is fully bright; when near the sun, its light is extinguished. When the sun and moon share the same degree, it is called *shuo* (new moon). Dividing the celestial sphere into four parts: on the eighth and twenty-third days, the moon’s motion brings it close to one part of the sun (*near-one*) and far from three parts (*far-three*), receiving varying degrees of sunlight. Thus, it is half-bright and half-dark, its *po* shaped like a drawn bow. At *shangxian* (first quarter), it appears at dusk, its light in the west. At *xiaxian* (last quarter), it appears at dawn, its light in the east. When it reaches the midpoint of the heavens, it is called *wang* (full moon). On the fifteenth day at dusk, the sun sets as the moon rises, facing each other from east and west, its light full and its *po* extinguished. When its light is fully spent, its body is restored—this is called *hui* (dark moon). On the thirtieth day, the moon nears the sun, and neither its light nor body is visible.

Whenever the sun and moon follow their proper degrees and their luminous influence aligns with the orientation of mountains, construction and burials are all auspicious. When the moon’s path along the White Way intersects the Yellow Way at a right angle, if this occurs at *shuo* (new moon), a solar eclipse results; if at *wang* (full moon), a lunar eclipse results. A solar eclipse occurs when the moon’s body obscures the sun’s light. A lunar eclipse occurs when the moon enters the dark void and does not receive the sun’s light. During solar or lunar eclipses, construction and burials must not be undertaken.

The five *wei* (wandering stars) are the essences of the Five Phases. Wood is called *Suixing* (Jupiter); in spring, it is the ruling star, suitable for invoking the Eastern Dragon. Fire is called *Yinghuo* (Mars); in summer, it is the ruling star, suitable for invoking the Southern Dragon. Earth is called *Zhenxing* (Saturn); in the four seasons, it is the ruling star, suitable for invoking the Dragons of the four corners. Metal is called *Taibai* (Venus); in autumn, it is the ruling star, suitable for invoking the Western Dragon. Water is called *Chenxing* (Mercury); in winter, it is the ruling star, suitable for invoking the Northern Dragon. Whenever solar or lunar eclipses or celestial anomalies occur, their auspices—whether fortunate or ominous—are divined based on the terrestrial region they traverse. Thus, those who practice selection must not be ignorant of this.

**Chapter Forty-Seven: On Star and Land Correlations**

Master *Bo* said: “The *fenye* (terrestrial correlations) of a given province or commandery can be determined—this is no idle claim. For the heavens have *fenxing* (stellar divisions), and the earth has *fenye* (terrestrial correlations), an unchanging correspondence of celestial influence and earthly response. For instance, if refined *sha* (sand) or *shui* (water) emerges in the *mao* (east) direction, it must signify that those born in *mao* will attain nobility. Or if in a *mao* or *yi* year one achieves official rank, this can be foretold. Alternatively, if refined *sha* or *shui* appears before a grave in the *you* (west) direction, one may predict that the occupant will hold office in the Zhao region. If refined *sha* or *shui* appears in the *xu* (northwest) direction, one may predict that the occupant will hold office in the Lu region. The rest can be inferred by analogy, and the responses are invariably accurate. However, this pertains only to the *sha* and *water* already present. One must also examine whether the *dragon* and *cavity* (geomantic features) are truly of noble configuration and whether the *qi* and fortune align with the prevailing direction—only then will predictions be infallible; otherwise, they will not hold.”

**Chapter Forty-Eight: On the Sovereign Star**

Master Zhu said: “The *Di* (Sovereign) is the ruler of heaven, and in selecting auspicious days, it must be given foremost consideration. Yet in common almanacs, discussions of the Sovereign Star are unverifiable. I have derived it from the *Yi* (Book of Changes) and the *Li* (Book of Rites). In the *Monthly Ordinances*, for the three spring months, it states: ‘Its sovereign is *Taihao*, its spirit *Gou Mang*—this is the lord of the green essence and the minister of the Wood Office, the so-called Green Sovereign.’ For the first and second summer months, it states: ‘Its sovereign is the *Yan Emperor*, its spirit *Zhu Rong*—this is the lord of the red essence and the minister of the Fire Office, the so-called Red Sovereign.’ For the last summer month, it states: ‘Its sovereign is the *Yellow Emperor*, its spirit *Hou Tu*—this is the lord of the yellow essence and the minister of the Earth Office, the so-called Yellow Sovereign.’ For the three autumn months, it states: ‘Its sovereign is *Shaohao*, its spirit *Rushou*—this is the lord of the white essence and the minister of the Metal Office, the so-called White Sovereign.’ For the three winter months, it states: ‘Its sovereign is *Zhuanxu*, its spirit *Xuan Ming*—this is the lord of the black essence and the minister of the Water Office, the so-called Black Sovereign.’

Furthermore, examining the *Xici* (Appended Judgments) of the *Yi*: ‘The Sovereign emerges from *Zhen* (Thunder), aligns with *Xun* (Wind), appears in *Li* (Fire), labors in *Kun* (Earth), rejoices in *Dui* (Lake), struggles in *Qian* (Heaven), toils in *Kan* (Water), and completes in *Gen* (Mountain).’ Viewed together, the ruler of heaven is one Sovereign. Divided, it becomes four; further divided, it becomes eight—and this is not the limit. Thus, from the three *hou* (five-day periods) of *Jingzhe* to *jia*, the three *hou* of the spring equinox to *mao*, and the three *hou* of *Qingming* to *yi*, this is what is meant by ‘the Sovereign emerges from *Zhen*.’ From the three *hou* of *Guyu* to *chen*, the three *hou* of *Lixia* to *xun*, and the three *hou* of *Xiaoman* to *si*, this is what is meant by ‘aligns with *Xun*.’”

The three *hou* of *Mangzhong* reach *bing*, the three *hou* of the summer solstice reach *wu*, and the three *hou* of *Xiaoshu* reach *ding*—this is what is meant by ‘appears in *Li*.’ The three *hou* of *Dashu* reach *wei*, the three *hou* of *Liqiu* reach *kun*, and the three *hou* of *Chushu* reach *shen*—this is what is meant by ‘labors in *Kun*.’ The three *hou* of *Bailu* reach *geng*, the three *hou* of the autumn equinox reach *you*, and the three *hou* of *Hanlu* reach *xin*—this is what is meant by ‘rejoices in *Dui*.’ The three *hou* of *Shuangjiang* reach *xu*, the three *hou* of *Lidong* reach *qian*, and the three *hou* of *Xiaoxue* reach *hai*—this is what is meant by ‘struggles in *Qian*.’ The three *hou* of *Daxue* reach *ren*, the three *hou* of the winter solstice reach *zi*, and the three *hou* of *Xiaohan* reach *gui*—this is what is meant by ‘toils in *Kan*.’ The three *hou* of *Dahan* reach *chou*, the three *hou* of *Lichun* reach *gen*, and the three *hou* of *Yushui* reach *yin*—this is what is meant by ‘completes in *Gen*.’ From the seventy-two *hou*, comprising 365¼ days, the pattern may be inferred.

Thus, the periods when the Sovereign arrives are auspicious times; the directions where the Sovereign descends are auspicious directions. Master Yang said: “In spring, acting in the east is auspicious; in summer, acting in the south is auspicious; in late summer, acting in the southwest is auspicious; in autumn, acting in the west is auspicious; in winter, acting in the north is auspicious”—this is the same principle. Today, people disregard the words of the classics and believe in baseless theories; their confusion is indeed profound.

**Chapter Forty-Nine: On the Five Stars and Resolving Doubts**
Some ask: “The east corresponds to wood, the south to fire, the west to metal, the north to water, and the four corners to earth—these are fixed positions. Yet the five stars also assign *zi* and *chou* to earth, *yin* and *hai* to wood, *mao* and *xu* to fire, *chen* and *you* to metal, *si* and *shen* to water, *wu* to the Greater Yang, and *wei* to the Greater Yin. Does this not mean that the five stars in heaven differ from the five phases on earth?”

It is said: The principles of the five stars and the five phases are one. *Zi* and *chou* are the root palaces of earth, not that *zi* and *chou* are earth in themselves. *Yin* and *hai* are the root palaces of wood, not that *yin* and *hai* are wood in themselves. *Mao* and *xu* are the root palaces of fire, not that *mao* and *xu* are fire in themselves. *Chen* and *you* are the root palaces of metal, not that *chen* and *you* are metal in themselves. *Si* and *shen* are the root palaces of water, not that *si* and *shen* are water in themselves. *Wu* is the palace of the Greater Yang, not that *wu* is the Greater Yang in itself. *Wei* is the palace of the Greater Yin, not that *wei* is the Greater Yin in itself. Just as metal is born in Lishui, one cannot directly equate Lishui with metal; jade emerges from Kunlun, yet one cannot directly equate Kunlun with jade.

Some ask: “Do the palaces where the seven luminaries reside also hold meaning?”
It is said: Among the celestial bodies, none are greater than the sun and moon. Thus, the sun resides in *wu*, and the moon in *wei*. Among earthly forms, none are greater than soil. Thus, soil resides in *zi* and *chou*. Among the movements between heaven and earth, none are greater than the four seasons. Thus, wood resides in *yin* and *hai*, corresponding to spring; fire resides in *mao* and *xu*, corresponding to summer; metal resides in *chen* and *you*, corresponding to autumn; water resides in *si* and *shen*, corresponding to winter. Within the span of a single star’s revolution, the great transformations of heaven and earth are contained. Thus, when Emperor Shun aligned the seven luminaries, was it without purpose? Clearly, the selection of the seven luminaries is of utmost importance.

Some further ask: “Among the twenty-eight constellations, there are stars named after days. *Xu* is the Rat Day, *Fang* is the Rabbit Day, and *Mao* is the Rooster Day. Does this mean there are four suns in heaven?”
It is said: No. These are all positions through which the Greater Yang flows. Just as the Son of Heaven in ancient times toured the four directions, each direction had its own Bright Hall—yet one would not directly equate the Bright Hall with the Son of Heaven. Moreover, the degrees of the twenty-eight constellations are all stages in the flow of the seven luminaries. This is akin to today’s provincial and prefectural officials, where each prefecture and county has its own traveling office. Those with keen insight will grasp this on their own.

**Chapter Fifty: On the Central Needle**
The ancients erected an eight-*chi* gnomon to determine the cardinal directions. The shadow of the sun in peripheral regions naturally differs from that in the central lands, for the mysterious needle moves yet remains constant. Yet this cannot be generalized; the *zi-wu* alignment for determining direction remains consistent everywhere. For instance, the ancients defined the “center” thus: in a hall, the center of the hall is the center; in a chamber, the center of the chamber is the center. Though the locations differ, each has its own center. Those who argue that the needle in peripheral regions differs from that in the central lands are mistaken. However, according to the theories of Lianzi, the *zi-wu* true needle slightly inclines toward *ren* and *bing*—this is a well-founded conclusion.

**Chapter Fifty-One: On the Two Luminaries**
Through observation, the Greater Yin and Greater Yang are the most auspicious deities in geomancy. Thus, in fate calculation and selection, they should be prioritized. All the foregoing discussions are based on the Greater Yin and Greater Yang. As for the five stars and four residues, they must be examined in real time and cannot be predetermined. Therefore, the following presents the fixed patterns of the two luminaries’ movements, so that even beginners without almanacs may seek auspiciousness and avoid misfortune.

The Greater Yang: Chen Tzu-hsien said: “The sun is the yang within yin, symbolizing the sovereign. Its virtue is utterly firm, its body utterly robust. Its movement through the heavens divides day and night, distinguishes cold and heat. It completes one revolution daily, falling short of the celestial sphere by one degree. Over a year, its cumulative motion precisely aligns with the heavens. Thus, the sun has three paths: the Yellow Path. When it reaches its northernmost point at Dongjing, it is closest to the pole; when it reaches its southernmost point at Qianniu, it is farthest from the pole. When it moves east to Jiao and west to Lou, it is at a median distance from the pole—these are the Central Path, Southern Path, and Northern Path, forming the three paths. When the southern pole extends to Qianniu, it marks the winter solstice, with forty *ke* of daylight and sixty *ke* of night.”

When the northern pole extends to Dongjing, it marks the summer solstice, with sixty *ke* of daylight and forty *ke* of night. Midway between the northern and southern poles, from Jiao in the east to Lou in the west, it marks the spring and autumn equinoxes, with fifty *ke* of daylight and fifty *ke* of night.

For the divisions of celestial motion: After the mid-*qi* of Rain Water in the first month, the sun enters the celestial lodge of Zī and Jūzǐ, corresponding to the stems *jiǎ*, *bǐng*, *gēng*, and *rén*. After the mid-*qi* of the Spring Equinox in the second month, the sun enters the lodge of Jiànglóu, corresponding to the trigrams *gèn*, *xùn*, *kūn*, and *qián*. After the mid-*qi* of Grain Rain in the third month, the sun enters the lodge of Dàliáng, corresponding to the stems *guǐ*, *yǐ*, *dīng*, and *xīn*. After the mid-*qi* of Lesser Fullness in the fourth month, the sun enters the lodge of Shíshěn, corresponding to *jiǎ*, *bǐng*, *gēng*, and *rén*. After the mid-*qi* of the Summer Solstice in the fifth month, the sun enters the lodge of Chúnshǒu, corresponding to *qián*, *kūn*, *gèn*, and *xùn*. After the mid-*qi* of Great Heat in the sixth month, the sun enters the lodge of Chúnhuǒ, corresponding to *guǐ*, *yǐ*, *dīng*, and *xīn*. After the mid-*qi* of Ending Heat in the seventh month, the sun enters the lodge of Chúnwěi, corresponding to *jiǎ*, *bǐng*, *gēng*, and *rén*. After the mid-*qi* of the Autumn Equinox in the eighth month, the sun enters the lodge of Shòuxīng, corresponding to *qián*, *kūn*, *gèn*, and *xùn*. After the mid-*qi* of Frost Descent in the ninth month, the sun enters the lodge of Dàhuǒ, corresponding to *guǐ*, *yǐ*, *dīng*, and *xīn*. After the mid-*qi* of Lesser Snow in the tenth month, the sun enters the lodge of Xīmù, corresponding to *bǐng*, *gēng*, *jiǎ*, and *rén*. After the mid-*qi* of the Winter Solstice in the eleventh month, the sun enters the lodge of Xīngjì, corresponding to *gèn*, *xùn*, *kūn*, and *qián*. After the mid-*qi* of Great Cold in the twelfth month, the sun enters the lodge of Xuánléng, corresponding to *yǐ*, *dīng*, *xīn*, and *guǐ*.

The regions it traverses are the directions illuminated by its light, where one may erect gnomons in the three cardinal and four orthogonal directions to harness its radiance. These are called the "mountain dwellers' imperial chariot stations," known as the *suìkōng*, where all celestial stars and terrestrial *sha* may be subdued. When the sun enters the lodges of Fáng, Xū, or Mǎo, it is termed the "sun ascending its palace," and its use is especially auspicious. If a solar eclipse or celestial anomaly occurs, it is most inauspicious and must be strictly avoided.

The moon, as stated by Chen Xiyi: The moon is the yin within yang, symbolizing the empress and consorts. Its virtue is utterly yielding, its body utterly compliant, and its movement through the heavens assists in regulating the sun, verified by the waxing and waning of nocturnal shadows. The moon itself possesses no light but borrows brightness from the sun. In terms of its unilluminated body, it is purely yin, resembling *kūn*. At the time of the new moon, after three days, it has not yet traversed thirty-seven degrees; as it begins to brighten, it first draws upon the sun’s light, hence called *shuò*, signifying the initial arising of yang. Visible at dusk in the *gēng* direction, after eight days, it has not yet traversed ninety-eight degrees; drawing upon half the sun’s brightness, it is called the "first quarter," signifying yang’s near-completion. Visible at dusk in the *dīng* direction, after fifteen days, it has not yet traversed 182.5 degrees; opposing the sun in full view, it draws upon the sun’s full brightness to become perfectly round, hence called *wàng*, signifying the full manifestation of the three yangs. Visible at dusk in the *jiǎ* direction, after another three days, it has not yet traversed 195 degrees; as the lunar essence begins to wane, it deviates from the full-moon opposition, and the yin essence begins to arise, hence called *pò*, signifying the return of yin. Visible at dawn in the *xīn* direction, after another five days, it has not yet traversed eighty-one degrees; half-obscured, it draws upon half the sun’s brightness, hence called the "last quarter," signifying yin’s growth to half. Visible at dawn in the *bǐng* direction, after another six days, it has fallen short of completing 365.25 degrees by 490 parts; reuniting with the sun, the moon’s brightness can no longer draw upon the sun’s, returning to obscurity, hence called *huì*, vanishing entirely at *yǐ*.

In its celestial motion, the moon traverses 352 degrees and 828.6 *lǐ* in a day, falling short of the sun by 12 degrees and 1/19 of a degree; in the heavens, it falls short by 13 degrees and 7/19 of a degree. Accumulating 29 and 499/940 days, it reunites with the sun at the celestial lodge, forming one month of twelve conjunctions. Accumulating 354 and 348/949 days, it reunites with the heavens, forming one year.

Thus, the moon has nine paths. The nine paths consist of two black paths emerging north of the Yellow Path, two red paths emerging south of the Yellow Path, two white paths emerging west of the Yellow Path, and two blue-green paths emerging east of the Yellow Path, together with the Yellow Path, totaling nine.

At the Beginning of Spring and the Spring Equinox, it follows the blue-green path, dividing degrees at *jiǎ*; at the Beginning of Autumn and the Autumn Equinox, it follows the white path, dividing degrees at *gēng*; at the Beginning of Winter and the Winter Solstice, it follows the black path, dividing degrees at *rén*; at the Beginning of Summer and the Summer Solstice, it follows the red path, dividing degrees at *bǐng*. The times when the sun and moon conjoin in degree, and the directions where the three conjunctions shine, are the stars of Celestial Virtue and Lunar Virtue.

Thus, in the third month, when the branch is *chén*, the sun and moon conjoin at *yǒu*, emerging at *gēng* and entering the palace at *rén*, hence Celestial and Lunar Virtue reside in *rén*. In the sixth month, when the branch is *wèi*, the sun and moon conjoin at *wǔ*, emerging at *bǐng* and entering the palace at *jiǎ*, hence the two virtues reside in *jiǎ*. In the ninth month, when the branch is *xū*, the sun and moon conjoin at *mǎo*, emerging at *jiǎ* and entering the palace at *bǐng*, hence the two virtues reside in *bǐng*. In the twelfth month, when the branch is *chǒu*, the sun and moon conjoin at *zǐ*, emerging at *rén* and entering the palace at *gēng*, hence the two virtues reside in *gēng*. As *zǐ* and *wǔ* mark the sun and moon’s beginning and end, and *mǎo* and *yǒu* their rising and setting, their divisions are many, giving rise to the Greater Yang and Greater Yin.

The other months when the moon enters its palace are times when the Greater Yin ascends its throne, where gnomons may be erected, and all celestial luminaries and terrestrial *sha* may be subdued.

If a lunar eclipse or celestial anomaly occurs, it portends great misfortune.

**Permanent Solar Position Chart**
Winter Solstice: Sun resides at *Jī* 4
Minor Cold: Sun resides at *Dǒu* 10
Major Cold: Sun resides at *Niú* 3
Start of Spring: Sun resides at *Xū* 1
Rain Water: Sun resides at *Wēi* 7
Awakening of Insects: Sun resides at *Shì* 7
Spring Equinox: Sun resides at *Bì* 3
Pure Brightness: Sun resides at *Kuí* 9
Grain Rain: Sun resides at *Lóu* 7
Start of Summer: Sun resides at *Wèi* 9
Lesser Fullness: Sun resides at *Mǎo* 8
Grain in Ear: Sun resides at *Bì* 11
Summer Solstice: Sun resides at *Jǐng* 1
Minor Heat: Sun resides at *Jǐng* 14
Major Heat: Sun resides at *Jǐng* 29
Start of Autumn: Sun resides at *Liǔ* 10
End of Heat: Sun resides at *Zhāng* 5
White Dew: Sun resides at *Yì* 3
Autumn Equinox: Sun resides at *Yì* 18
Cold Dew: Sun resides at *Gě* 13
Frost Descent: Sun resides at *Jiǎo* 9
Start of Winter: Sun resides at *Dī* 3
Minor Snow: Sun resides at *Fáng* 2
Major Snow: Sun resides at *Wěi* 2

For example, at the Winter Solstice, the sun resides at *Jī* 4; the following day, it moves to *Jī* 5, and the day after to *Jī* 6, progressing sequentially through each solar term in this manner.

**Permanent Lunar Position Chart**
First month: 1st–2nd day, moon at *Zǐ*
Second month: 1st–2nd day, moon at *Hài*
Third month: 1st–2nd day, moon at *Xū*
Fourth month: 1st–2nd day, moon at *Yǒu*
Fifth month: 1st–2nd day, moon at *Shēn*
Sixth month: 1st–2nd day, moon at *Wèi*
Seventh month: 1st–2nd day, moon at *Wǔ*
Eighth month: 1st–2nd day, moon at *Sì*
Ninth month: 1st–2nd day, moon at *Chén*
Tenth month: 1st day, moon at *Mǎo*
Eleventh month: 1st–2nd day, moon at *Yín*
Twelfth month: 1st–2nd day, moon at *Chǒu*

For example, in the first month, the moon resides at *Zǐ* on the 1st and 2nd days; on the 3rd, 4th, and 5th days, it enters *Hài*; on the 6th and 7th days, it enters *Xū*, progressing sequentially through each month in this manner.

The above are the fixed degrees of the sun and moon’s movements, verified in their time and unchanging.

Once the palaces of the sun and moon are determined, the life palace and body palace may be known, and the five planets and four residues may be deduced. This is the essential task of electional astrology; scholars must prioritize it above all.

---

**Chapter Fifty-Two: On the Celestial Mechanism** *(Chapters 52–60 are discourses by Master Lǐ of Ráo’ān)*

The Wood Virtue of the East is called the Year Star,
Its color ever green, its motion marked by stations and retrogradations.
Its regular path is recorded in the almanacs;
When stationary, it is called the Star of Prolonged Mourning,
Boding fortune or disaster.
When retrograde, it is named the Star of Calamity and Sorrow,
Presaging misfortune of no small weight.
When hidden, it is called the Heavenly Barrier,
Neutral in fortune or harm.

When this star follows its proper course unobstructed,
Fortune arrives swiftly.
If it meets a malefic star while in direct motion,
Its benefic power is halved.
It traverses one degree in six days—
This is no ordinary path.
Sometimes it moves one degree in three days,
Or completes a cycle in five.
Generally, it shifts one palace per year,
This is its general progression.
In twelve years, it completes a minor cycle;
Its precise degrees must be carefully examined.
After eighty-two years,
It completes a grand celestial circuit.

If the Eastern Dragon is encountered,
Great undertakings may be established,
And all celestial omens and terrestrial *sha* subdued.
When it enters *Jiǎo*, *Dǒu*, or *Kuí*, it is in its temple;
When in *Jī*, *Bì*, *Shēn*, or *Gě*, it gains power.
Direct motion is auspicious; hidden or retrograde, inauspicious.

---

**Chapter Fifty-Three: On Mars**

The Fire Spirit of the South is called *Yínghuò*,
Its celestial form distinctly arrayed.
Its motion swift or slow, direct or retrograde,
Its stations and occultations must be closely observed.
Slow, stationary, hidden, or retrograde—all portend disaster;
Direct motion brings fortune.

When stationary, it is renamed the Star of Heavenly Rainbow,
Most ominous, heralding fire.
When retrograde, it is called the Star of Heavenly Destruction,
Bringing pestilence and celestial punishment.
When hidden, it is named the Black Celestial Luminary,
Wreaking havoc with utmost cruelty.

When entering its palace, it brings joy and prosperity,
Its radiance shining with literary brilliance.
Its motion demands meticulous scrutiny—
Do not treat it as an ordinary star.
It traverses one degree in eighteen hours,
Following its direct path.
It shifts one palace in fifty days,
A time when all actions may be undertaken.
Fortune is bestowed during this period,
And all orientations are auspicious.
If it further enters a palace of prosperity,
It hastens spring’s arrival like the wind.

This star governs literary eminence and official rank,
Bestowing examination success and imperial favor.
It also heralds child prodigies entering the imperial court,
And ministers drawing near the throne.

If it moves slowly, stations, or retrogrades,
It traverses one degree in two days.
Two months pass before it crosses one palace—
This is an extremely inauspicious time.
When swift, it covers five degrees in seven days,
Or one palace in forty-five days.
When slow and retrograde, it may take three or four months
To traverse a single palace.
In two years, it completes a minor cycle—
A subtle art not lightly transmitted.
In seventy-nine years,
It completes a grand celestial circuit.

If the Southern Dragon is encountered,
Great undertakings may be established,
And the three harmonies and four cardinal directions are also auspicious.
When it enters *Wěi*, *Shì*, *Zī*, or *Yì*, it is in its temple;
When in *Dǒu*, *Jiǎo*, *Kuí*, or *Jǐng*, it gains power.
All celestial omens and terrestrial *sha* may be subdued.
Direct motion is auspicious; hidden or retrograde, inauspicious.

---

**Chapter Fifty-Four: On Saturn**

The Earth Virtue of the Center is called the Quelling Star,
The essence of *wù* and *jǐ* earth.
In the classics, it is also called the Earthly Net and Throat,
Its fortune and misfortune requiring careful examination.
Its motion marked by occultations, retrogradations, slowness, and directness,
Each aspect must be meticulously analyzed.

When hidden, it is called the Plague Star,
To be feared, for its calamities are grave.
In the Jin dynasty, it was also named the Dark Luminary,
Presaging disaster.
When retrograde, it is called the Star of Household Ruin,
Bringing sudden misfortune upon encounter.

When it enters its palace in direct motion,
Wealth and nobility flourish enduringly.
If it meets the Moon’s Comet in the same palace,
Fortune and misfortune are evenly matched.

It shifts one palace every three years,
Following both major and minor cycles.
Fifty-nine years mark its full period,
Its weighty nature making its motion slow.

If the Western Dragon is encountered,
Great undertakings may be established,
And all celestial omens and terrestrial *sha* subdued.
When it enters *Dī*, *Nǚ*, *Wèi*, or *Liǔ*, it is in its temple;
When in *Wěi*, *Shì*, *Zī*, *Zhāng*, or *Yì*, it gains power.
Direct motion is auspicious; hidden or retrograde, inauspicious.

---

**Chapter Fifty-Five: On Venus**

The Metal Star of the West is called *Tàibái*,
Also known as the Long Dusk.
Its motion marked by slowness, stations, directness, and retrogradation,
Its morning and evening apparitions distinguished.
*(To be continued...)*

**Chapter Fifty-Five: On Venus (continued)**

When it lies hidden yet follows the seasons, moving in direct motion,
Its blessings are truly unmatched.
If it strays from its proper path across the heavens, it is not fitting—
War and disaster follow in its wake.
When sharing a palace with Mars, it is constrained;
At such times, what merit does it hold?
When sharing a palace with Saturn, it is most fitting,
And its felicitous influence surpasses the norm.
When it is slow, retrograde, or merely slow in motion,
There is no misfortune, and even blessings are light.
Only fear its conjunction with *Bó* and *Luó-Jì*—
Their baleful power must not be encountered.
When the Sun, Moon, or Mercury join it in position,
One ascends to the imperial examinations in a single stroke.
Its motion is half a degree per day,
And it traverses one palace each month.
In a year, it completes a circuit of the heavens,
Ever aligned with the celestial degrees.
How long is its great annual cycle?
Mark it well: eight months.
This star, in general, brings no calamity—
Where it appears, fortune and grace arrive.
When it illuminates three directions, it brings great benefit,
And it delights especially in gathering with the Sun.
When it enters its temple and ascends the throne, joy fills the heart,
And high office reaches the Three Terraces.
If it meets Jupiter in the same palace,
The enemy state’s wealth may be spoken of.

The Western Dragon, when encountered,
Allows great undertakings to be established,
And all celestial omens and terrestrial *sha* subdued.
When in *Kàng*, *Niú*, *Lóu*, or *Guǐ*, it is in its temple;
When in *Shì*, *Nǚ*, *Wèi*, or *Liǔ*, it gains power.
Direct motion is auspicious; hidden or retrograde, inauspicious.

---

**Chapter Fifty-Six: On Mercury**

The Water Virtue of the North is called *Chénxīng*,
Its celestial image arrayed in the heavenly court.
Its motion marked by swiftness, slowness, concealment, and retrogradation,
Yet it is always direct, never retrograde.
This star, in general, governs auspiciousness—
When it meets the Sun, blessings are boundless.
If it is slow or retrograde, its power slightly wanes,
And when *Bó* or Mars join it, they move in opposition.
Its path ever near the Sun’s side,
It serves as the sovereign’s assistant.
It moves one and a half degrees each day,
And traverses one palace each month.
When slow, it takes sixty-nine days to shift;
When swift, twenty days suffice.
At its swiftest, it passes one palace in seventeen days,
Its monthly motion never the same.
In general, it completes a minor cycle in a year,
Its precise degrees requiring careful observation.
Sixty-five years mark its great celestial circuit—
Its subtle secrets cannot be fully spoken.

The Northern Dragon, when encountered,
Allows great undertakings to be established,
And all celestial omens and terrestrial *sha* subdued without question.
When in *Jī*, *Bì*, *Shēn*, or *Hàn*, it is in its temple;
When in *Kàng*, *Niú*, *Lóu*, or *Guǐ*, it gains power.
Direct motion is auspicious; hidden or retrograde, inauspicious.

---

**Chapter Fifty-Seven: On the Purple Qi**

The residue of Jupiter becomes the Purple Qi,
The greatest source of fortune.
Auspicious signs and joyous omens meet in its presence,
And all endeavors reach fulfillment.
This star, in general, brings abundant blessings,
Its nature benevolent, free of malice.
When it enters its temple and flourishes, its joy is extraordinary—
Its precise degrees may be carefully calculated.
Its motion is always direct, easy to seek;
It knows no slowness, retrogradation, concealment, or stations.
In general, it moves one degree each month,
And completes twelve degrees in a year.
Three years pass before it traverses one palace—
This is its fixed course.
After circling the heavens in a great cycle,
Twenty-eight springs have turned.

The Southeast Dragon is most fitting.

---

**Chapter Fifty-Eight: On the Moon’s *Bó***

The Moon’s *Bó* is mostly the essence of Water,
Named the Great Yin Spirit,
Also called the Broomstar, the Great General—
Its aid in misfortune and fortune runs deep.
When the Five Stars move in direct motion and meet it,
It may also assist in bringing blessings.
But if it meets retrograde stars, it is most baleful—
When it meets *Bó*, great misfortune arises.
When the Moon’s *Bó* enters a palace or its temple,
Observe the other stars to discern fortune or misfortune.
If the other stars are baleful, it augments their balefulness;
If they are auspicious, it follows their auspiciousness.
Its motion knows no slowness, stations, or retrogradation—
It traverses three degrees each month.
In nine months, it passes one palace,
Its motion following a constant path.
How many degrees does it traverse in twelve months?
Exactly forty-nine.
When does it complete a minor celestial circuit?
Its precise degrees are best carefully calculated.
Sixty-two years complete its celestial circuit—
The celestial degrees must be examined in detail.

The Northeast Dragon is most fitting.

---

**Chapter Fifty-Nine: On *Luó* and *Huì***

*Luó* and *Huì* are the residue of Water,
Their names at the Celestial Head unchanging.
When they do not meet inauspicious stars, they hold the greatest power—
Fortune and misfortune must be carefully calculated.
They know no concealment, retrogradation, slowness, or stations,
Ever moving in retrograde motion.
This star, in general, is baleful in nature,
Its power to bring blessings entirely weak.
At the new moon, when it shares a degree with the Sun,
A solar eclipse is certain, without doubt.
At the full moon, when it shares a degree with the Moon,
A lunar eclipse portends misfortune.
When it causes an eclipse, it is most ominous—
No affair can withstand its encounter.
Only when it enters its temple and meets Mars
Does its power to assist in blessings become significant.
It moves one degree every nineteen days,
And traverses half a degree each month.
Eighteen months pass before it shifts one palace—
All calendars agree on this.
Nineteen years mark its minor cycle,
Each step moving in retrograde motion.
Ninety-three years complete its great cycle—
The celestial mechanism must not be carelessly transmitted.

The Fire and Earth Dragons are most fitting.

---

**Chapter Sixty: On *Jìdū***

The residue of Earth is called the star *Jìdū*,
The Eclipse Spirit, known as the Leopard’s Tail.
This star’s nature resembles *Luó* and *Huì*—
Mostly baleful, with little good, meeting retrogradation.
Its constant motion has no form,
Nor does it require discussion of concealment or slowness.
When it shares a degree with the Sun or Moon, an eclipse occurs—
Its malevolence is most inauspicious.
When it enters a palace or its temple, observe the accompanying stars—
Fortune and misfortune yet follow one another.
When Mars and Saturn share its palace in direct motion,
Its blessings are beyond doubt.
But if Mars and Saturn are hidden or retrograde,
Calamity descends from the heavens.
Its motion is always opposite to Mars and *Luó*—
It knows no stations, directness, or retrogradation.
It moves one degree every nineteen days,
And traverses half a degree each month.
A year and a half passes before it shifts one palace,
Its minor cycle completed in nineteen years.
Ninety-three years mark its great cycle—
Its revolutions never cease.

The Earth and Metal Dragons are most fitting.

---

*The Record of Filial Piety in Geomancy and the Completion of the Confucian Path, Scroll Two, Concludes.*

**#1** *(Note: "Fire" was originally miswritten as "Great"; corrected according to context.)*
**#2** *(Note: After "nineteen," the character "fen" was omitted; corrected according to context.)*
**#3** *(Note: After "again," the character "next" was omitted; corrected according to context.)*
**#4** *(Note: "Auspicious" was originally miswritten as "announce"; corrected according to context.)*

*The Record of Filial Piety in Geomancy and the Completion of the Confucian Path, Scroll Three*

### **Introduction to Selection Principles**
This work on selection embodies principles timeless across past and present, serving to complement the application of dragon veins, burial sites, mountains, and waterways. Yet the fixed nature of yin and yang, the inherent qualities of the Five Phases, and the various auspicious and inauspicious stellar configurations must first be understood. Thus, these principles are outlined in advance for the benefit of beginners.

For burials, one need not rigidly adhere to major or minor burial days. For constructions, one need not strictly observe major or minor construction days. As long as the dragon spirit harmonizes with the balanced *qi*, the sitting direction aligns with auspicious stars, mutual support fortifies destiny, and the land meets the demands of the time, it suffices. However, the auspiciousness or inauspiciousness of stellar positions in the sitting direction must be determined primarily by the Seven Governors; one must not be misled by heterodox teachings.

### **Principles of Dragon Veins, Burial Sites, Yin-Yang, and the Five Phases**
From three degrees in *Dǒu* within the *Yín* palace to three degrees at the center of *Mǎo*, all belong to yang wood.
From four degrees in *Xīn* within the *Mǎo* palace to eleven degrees in *Jiǎo* at the center of *Chén*, all belong to yin wood.
From nine degrees in *Zhóu* within the *Sì* palace to nine degrees in *Zhāng* at the center of *Wǔ*, all belong to yin fire.
From eight degrees in *Zhāng* within the *Wǔ* palace to twenty-seven degrees in *Jǐng* at the center of *Wèi*, all belong to yang fire.
From eight degrees in *Jǐng* within the *Shēn* palace to one degree in *Bì* at the center of *Yǒu*, all belong to yang metal.
From two degrees in *Bì* within the *Yǒu* palace to four degrees in *Lóu* at the center of *Xū*, all belong to yin metal.
From one degree in *Kuí* within the *Hài* palace to seven degrees in *Wēi* at the center of *Zǐ*, all belong to yin water.
From six degrees in *Wēi* within the *Zǐ* palace to twenty-two degrees in *Dǒu* at the center of *Chǒu*, all belong to yang water.
The last eighteen degrees of *Chén* and the last eighteen degrees of *Xū* belong to yang earth.
The last eighteen degrees of *Chǒu* and the last eighteen degrees of *Wèi* belong to yin earth.

### **Principles of Yin-Yang in Years, Months, Days, and Hours**
*Jiǎ*, *Bǐng*, *Wù*, *Gēng*, and *Rén* are yang celestial stems;
*Yǐ*, *Xīn*, *Jǐ*, *Dīng*, and *Guǐ* are yin celestial stems.
*Zǐ*, *Yín*, *Chén*, *Wǔ*, *Shēn*, and *Xū* are yang earthly branches;
*Chǒu*, *Mǎo*, *Sì*, *Wèi*, *Yǒu*, and *Hài* are yin earthly branches.

### **Principles of the Five Phases in Years, Months, Days, and Hours**
*Jiǎ*, *Yǐ*, *Yín*, and *Mǎo* belong to wood;
*Bǐng*, *Dīng*, *Sì*, and *Wǔ* belong to fire;
*Wù*, *Jǐ*, *Chén*, *Xū*, *Chǒu*, and *Wèi* belong to earth;
*Gēng*, *Xīn*, *Shēn*, and *Yǒu* belong to metal;
*Rén*, *Guǐ*, *Hài*, and *Zǐ* belong to water.

### **Principles of Yin-Yang and the Five Phases in the Four Seasons**
The thirty-six days after *Lìchūn* belong to yang wood; the next thirty-six days belong to yin wood; the following eighteen days belong to yang earth.
The thirty-six days after *Lìxià* belong to yin fire; the next thirty-six days belong to yang fire; the following eighteen days belong to yin earth.
The thirty-six days after *Lìqiū* belong to yang metal; the next thirty-six days belong to yin metal; the following eighteen days belong to yang earth.
The thirty-six days after *Lìdōng* belong to yin water; the next thirty-six days belong to yang water; the following eighteen days belong to yin earth.

### **Principles of the Three Origins**
The ten celestial stems are the *Tiānyuán* (Heavenly Origin); the twelve earthly branches are the *Dìyuán* (Earthly Origin).
Within *Zǐ* lies *Guǐ* and water; within *Chǒu* lie *Guǐ*, *Sì*, and *Xīn*; within *Yín* lie *Jiǎ*, *Bǐng*, and *Wù*; within *Mǎo* lies *Yǐ* and wood; within *Chén* lie *Yǐ*, *Guǐ*, and *Wù*; within *Sì* lie *Bǐng*, *Wù*, and *Gēng*; within *Wǔ* lie *Dīng* and *Jǐ*; within *Wèi* lie *Yǐ*, *Jǐ*, and *Dīng*; within *Shēn* lie *Gēng*, *Rén*, and *Wù*; within *Yǒu* lie *Xīn* and metal; within *Xū* lie *Xīn*, *Dīng*, and *Wù*; within *Hài* lie *Rén* and *Jiǎ*—these are all the *Rényuán* (Human Origin).

### **Principles of the Life and Death of the Five Phases**
*Jiǎ* wood is born in *Hài* and dies in *Wǔ*;
*Yǐ* wood is born in *Wǔ* and dies in *Hài*;
*Bǐng* and *Wù* are born in *Yín* and die in *Yǒu*;
*Dīng* and *Jǐ* are born in *Yǒu* and die in *Yín*;
*Gēng* metal is born in *Sì* and dies in *Zǐ*;
*Xīn* metal is born in *Zǐ* and dies in *Sì*;
*Rén* water is born in *Shēn* and dies in *Mǎo*;
*Guǐ* water is born in *Mǎo* and dies in *Shēn*.
For rituals such as bathing and capping, begin from the *chángshēng* (birth) position. The sequence is: *chángshēng* (birth), *mùyù* (bathing), *guāndài* (capping), *línguān* (official position), *dìwàng* (prosperity), *shuāi* (decline), *bìng* (illness), *sǐ* (death), *mù* (tomb), *jué* (extinction), *tāi* (embryo), and *yǎng* (nurturing).

### **Principles of the Generation and Conquest of the Five Phases**
Wood generates fire; fire generates earth; earth generates metal; metal generates water; water generates wood—this is the mutual generation of the Five Phases.
Wood conquers earth; earth conquers water; water conquers fire; fire conquers metal; metal conquers wood—this is the mutual conquest of the Five Phases.

### **Principles of the Classic Stars**
*Wēi* 12 passes *Zǐ*; *Nǚ* 1 passes *Chǒu*; *Dǒu* 3 passes *Yín*; *Wěi* 2 passes *Mǎo*; *Shì* 1 passes *Chén*; *Hàn* 9 passes *Sì*; *Zhāng* 14 passes *Wǔ*; *Liǔ* 4 passes *Wèi*; *Jǐng* 8 passes *Shēn*; *Bì* 6 passes *Yǒu*; *Wèi* 3 passes *Xū*; *Kuí* 1 passes *Hài*.
*Xīng*, *Xū*, *Fáng*, and *Mǎo* are the positions of the sun;
*Zhāng*, *Wēi*, *Xīn*, and *Bì* are the positions of the moon;
*Jiǎo*, *Dǒu*, *Kuí*, and *Jǐng* are the positions of Jupiter;
*Wěi*, *Shì*, *Zī*, and *Yì* are the positions of Mars;
*Shì*, *Nǚ*, *Wèi*, and *Liǔ* are the positions of Saturn;
*Kàng*, *Niú*, *Lóu*, and *Guǐ* are the positions of Venus;
*Jī*, *Bì*, *Shēn*, and *Sōng* are the positions of Mercury.

### **Principles of the Weft Stars**
*Zǐ* and *Chǒu* are the positions of earth;
*Yín* and *Hài* are the positions of wood;
*Mǎo* and *Xū* are the positions of fire;
*Chén* and *Yǒu* are the positions of metal;
*Sì* and *Shēn* are the positions of water;
*Wǔ* is the position of the sun;
*Wèi* is the position of the moon.

The remaining stars are calculated as attached slaves: *Zǐ* (black) attaches to *Yín*, *Bèi* attaches to *Shēn*, and *Luó* attaches to *Xū*.

### **Principles of the Life Palace**
The palace where the sun arrives is the Life Palace; the degree where the sun resides is the Life Degree.
For example, on the fourteenth day of the first month in the *Guǐ-Wèi* year of the *Wàn-Lì* reign, at the *Mǎo* hour, the sun is at *Xū* 1° in the *Zǐ* Palace—thus, earth is the palace’s ruling star, and the sun is the degree’s ruling star.
At the *Chén* hour, the sun reaches *Jǐng* 3° in the *Chǒu* Palace—thus, earth remains the palace’s ruling star, while wood becomes the degree’s ruling star. Other cases follow this pattern.

### **Principles of the Body Palace**
The palace where the moon arrives is the Body Palace; the degree where the moon resides is the Body Degree.
For example, on the fourteenth day of the first month in the *Guǐ-Wèi* year of the *Wàn-Lì* reign, at the third quarter of the *Mǎo* hour, the moon reaches *Liǔ* 5° in the *Wǔ* Palace—thus, the moon is the palace’s ruling star, and earth is the degree’s ruling star.
At the *Chén* hour, the moon reaches *Jǐng* 10° in the *Wèi* Palace—thus, the moon remains the palace’s ruling star, while wood becomes the degree’s ruling star. Other cases follow this pattern.

### **Principles of the Annual Star**
Spring is wood, summer is fire, autumn is metal, winter is water; in the four seasonal months, it is earth.

### **Principles of the Active Star**
Wood meets fire, fire meets earth, earth meets metal, metal meets water, water meets wood.

### **Principles of Favor, Birth, Enmity, and Hardship**
That which gives me life is favor; that which restricts me is hardship; that which I restrict is enmity; that which I occupy is the host.

### **Principles for Determining Months from Years**
For *Jiǎ* and *Jǐ* years, *Bǐng* leads;
For *Yǐ* and *Gēng* years, *Wù* heads;
For *Bǐng* and *Xīn* years, begin with *Gēng*;
For *Dīng* and *Rén* years, follow *Rén* in sequence;
As for *Wù* and *Guǐ*, where do they start? Seek carefully from *Jiǎ-Yín*.
For example, in a *Jiǎ* or *Jǐ* year, the first month begins with *Bǐng-Yín*, the second month is *Dīng-Mǎo*, the third month is *Wù-Chén*, and so on in sequence. Other cases follow this pattern.

### **Principles for Determining Hours from Days**
For *Jiǎ* and *Jǐ* days, add *Jiǎ*;
For *Yǐ* and *Gēng* days, *Bǐng* begins;
For *Bǐng* and *Xīn* days, start with *Wù*;
For *Dīng* and *Rén* days, *Gēng-Zǐ* resides;
Where does *Wù* and *Guǐ* begin? *Rén-Zǐ* is the true path.
For example, on a *Jiǎ* or *Jǐ* day, the *Zǐ* hour begins with *Jiǎ-Zǐ*, the *Chǒu* hour is *Yǐ-Chǒu*, the *Yín* hour is *Bǐng-Yín*, and so on in sequence. Other cases follow this pattern.

### **Principles of the Great Fortune Cycle**
*Jiǎ*, *Bǐng*, *Wù*, *Gēng*, and *Rén* are yang years.
*Yǐ*, *Xīn*, *Jǐ*, *Dīng*, and *Guǐ* are yin years.
For house construction, in yang years proceed forward, in yin years proceed backward—this aligns with determining the fortune cycle for men in fate calculation.
For burials, in yang years proceed backward, in yin years proceed forward—this aligns with determining the fortune cycle for women in fate calculation.
In all cases, begin from the monthly command.
For example, in a *Jiǎ-Zǐ* year, the second month is *Dīng-Mǎo*; proceeding forward, the fortune cycle begins with *Wù-Chén*, then *Jǐ-Sì*, *Gēng-Wǔ*, and so on in sequence.
Proceeding backward, the fortune cycle begins with *Bǐng-Yín*, then *Yǐ-Chǒu*, *Jiǎ-Zǐ*, *Guǐ-Hài*, and so on in sequence. In all cases, consider the earthly branches, not the heavenly stems. Other cases follow this pattern.

### **Principles for Calculating Years in the Great Fortune Cycle**
Each month has a solar term preceding and a mid-term following, such as *Lì-Chūn* (Beginning of Spring) as the solar term and *Yǔ-Shuǐ* (Rain Water) as the mid-term—this applies to both days and months.
In the great fortune cycle, only the solar terms are considered, not the mid-terms.
In yang years, observe the preceding solar term; in yin years, observe the following solar term.
Every three days count as one year in the calculation.
For example, in a yang year, if an event occurs three days before *Lì-Chūn*, it is counted as the first year of the fortune cycle. If it occurs six days before, it is the second year; nine days before, the third year; twelve days before, the fourth year; fifteen days before, the fifth year. Other cases follow this pattern.
In a yin year, if an event occurs three days after *Lì-Chūn*, it is the first year of the fortune cycle. If it occurs six days after, it is the second year; nine days after, the third year; twelve days after, the fourth year; fifteen days after, the fifth year. Other cases follow this pattern.
If there is a remainder of one day, it is disregarded. If there is a remainder of two days, it is counted as three days. If there is a remainder of one and a half days, it is counted as half a year.

### **Principles of the Minor Fortune Cycle**
In determining the minor fortune cycle, the heavenly stem of the year’s ruling star is taken as the primary star.
Fate calculators also say: "The year uses the heavenly stem, the fortune cycle uses the earthly branch"—this is the same principle.
For example, in *Jiǎ* and *Yǐ* years, wood is the minor fortune cycle; in *Bǐng* and *Dīng* years, fire is the minor fortune cycle; in *Wù* and *Jǐ* years, earth is the minor fortune cycle; in *Gēng* and *Xīn* years, metal is the minor fortune cycle; in *Rén* and *Guǐ* years, water is the minor fortune cycle—this aligns with the yearly fortune cycle in fate calculation.
Additionally, the monthly command for determining the fortune cycle follows the same principles as the great fortune cycle. The daily spirit for determining the fortune cycle follows the same principles as the minor fortune cycle.

### **Principles of the Initial Limit**
This resembles the calculation of the juvenile limit in astrology:
One is life, two is wealth, three is illness and misfortune,
Four is wife, five is fortune—proceed forward in sequence.
At six years, return to the official salary palace,
And count backward until reaching the Life Palace.
For example, if the Life Palace is in *Zǐ*, then:
At one year old, the limit is in the same palace, with the host star as metal;
At two years old, it moves to the Wealth Palace, in *Sì*, with the host star as water;
At four years old, it moves to the Wife Palace, in *Wǔ*, with the host star as the sun;
At five years old, it moves to the Fortune Palace, in *Yín*, with the host star as wood;
At six years old, it moves to the Official Salary Palace, in *Mǎo*, with the host star as fire;
At seven years old, it returns to the Fortune Palace;
At eight years old, it reaches the Appearance Palace, in *Chǒu*, with the host star as earth;
At nine years old, it returns to the Life Palace.
After nine years, the great limit begins. For those whose Life Palace is in other palaces, the same principles apply.

### **Principles of the Great Limit**
In determining the great limit, first exclude the initial nine years, then begin the calculation from the degree’s ruling star in the Life Palace.
Each palace spans thirty degrees, with three degrees governing one year. After passing the degrees, exclude them, and begin the calculation from the remaining degrees, always proceeding forward from right to left—this is explained starting from the limit’s origin in the Life Palace.

The remaining palaces are traversed at a rate of one palace per ten years. For example, if the Life Palace is established at *Zǐ* in the eleventh degree of *Wēi*, the great limit begins at ten years old. If it is in the ninth degree of *Wēi*, the great limit begins at nine years old. If in the sixth degree, it begins at eight years old; if in the third degree, at seven years old. The same principle applies to other cases. If the Life Palace is established at *Zǐ* in the second degree of *Nǚ*, the entire palace is exhausted after the initial nine years, leaving no remaining degrees, so the great limit immediately enters *Chǒu* after the initial nine years.

### **Principles of the Minor Limit**
In determining the minor limit, calculations always begin from the current year’s *Tàisuì* position. Regardless of whether the year is yang or yin, the progression moves counterclockwise from left to right, with one palace traversed per year. The monthly limit is counted from the current year’s position, beginning in the first month; the daily limit is counted from the month’s position, beginning on the first day; the hourly limit is counted from the day’s position, beginning at *Zǐ* hour. For example, if *Tàisuì* is in *Wèi*, *Wèi* corresponds to one year old, *Wǔ* to two years old, *Sì* to three years old. Proceeding rightward through the palaces, at thirteen years old, it returns to *Wèi*, cycling endlessly with one palace per year. The monthly limit moves one palace per month from the year’s position; the daily limit moves one palace per day from the month’s position; the hourly limit moves one palace per hour from the day’s position.

### **Principles of the Three Directions**
*Shēn-Zǐ-Chén*, *Hài-Mǎo-Wèi*, *Sì-Yǒu*, *Chǒu-Yín-Wǔ-Xū*, *Gèn-Bǐng-Xīn*, *Qián-Jiǎ-Dīng*, *Xùn-Gēng-Guǐ*, and *Kūn-Yǐ-Rén*—these are all groupings of the Three Directions.

### **Principles of the Four Cardinal Points**
*Zǐ-Wǔ*, *Mǎo-Yǒu*, *Yín-Shēn*, *Sì-Hài*, *Chén-Xū*, *Chǒu-Wèi*, *Qián-Kūn*, *Gèn-Xùn*, *Jiǎ-Gēng*, *Bǐng-Rén*, *Yǐ-Xīn*, and *Dīng-Guǐ*—these are all groupings of the Four Cardinal Points.

### **Principles of Intercalary Palaces**
*Rén* and *Guǐ* flank *Zǐ*; *Zǐ* and *Chǒu* flank *Guǐ*; *Guǐ* and *Gèn* flank *Chǒu*; *Chǒu* and *Yín* flank *Gèn*; *Gèn* and *Jiǎ* flank *Yín*; *Yín* and *Mǎo* flank *Jiǎ*; *Jiǎ* and *Yǐ* flank *Mǎo*; *Mǎo* and *Chén* flank *Yǐ*; *Yǐ* and *Xùn* flank *Chén*; *Chén* and *Bǐng* flank *Xùn*; *Xùn* and *Wǔ* flank *Bǐng*; *Bǐng* and *Dīng* flank *Wǔ*; *Wǔ* and *Wèi* flank *Dīng*; *Dīng* and *Kūn* flank *Wèi*; *Wèi* and *Gēng* flank *Kūn*; *Kūn* and *Gēng* flank *Shēn*; *Shēn* and *Yǒu* flank *Gēng*; *Gēng* and *Xīn* flank *Yǒu*; *Yǒu* and *Xū* flank *Xīn*; *Xīn* and *Qián* flank *Xū*; *Xū* and *Hài* flank *Qián*; *Qián* and *Zǐ* flank *Rén*.

Additionally, *Hài* and *Chǒu* flank *Zǐ*; *Zǐ* and *Yín* flank *Chǒu*; *Chǒu* and *Mǎo* flank *Yín*; *Yín* and *Chén* flank *Mǎo*; *Mǎo* and *Sì* flank *Chén*; *Chén* and *Wǔ* flank *Sì*; *Sì* and *Wèi* flank *Wǔ*; *Wǔ* and *Shēn* flank *Wèi*; *Wèi* and *Yǒu* flank *Shēn*; *Shēn* and *Xū* flank *Yǒu*; *Yǒu* and *Hài* flank *Xū*; *Xū* and *Zǐ* flank *Hài*.

Also, *Rén* and *Gèn* flank *Guǐ*; *Guǐ* and *Jiǎ* flank *Gèn*; *Gèn* and *Yǐ* flank *Jiǎ*; *Jiǎ* and *Xùn* flank *Yǐ*; *Yǐ* and *Bǐng* flank *Xùn*; *Xùn* and *Dīng* flank *Bǐng*; *Dīng* and *Kūn* flank *Dīng*; *Kūn* and *Gēng* flank *Dīng*; *Kūn* and *Xīn* flank *Gēng*; *Gēng* and *Qián* flank *Xīn*; *Xīn* and *Rén* flank *Qián*.

### **Principles of the Talisman Head**
Each solar term is divided into three throats, with each throat spanning five days. *Jiǎ* and *Jǐ* serve as the talisman heads. When the talisman head aligns with *Zǐ-Wǔ* or *Mǎo-Yǒu*, it marks the upper throat; when it aligns with *Yín-Shēn* or *Sì-Hài*, it marks the middle throat; when it aligns with *Chén-Xū* or *Chǒu-Wèi*, it marks the lower throat. Whenever the talisman head of the upper throat arrives at a day or hour, it signifies the onset of the solar term. For instance, if the day of *Lìchūn* coincides with the upper throat talisman head, that day is regarded as the *qì* throat of *Lìchūn*. If the upper throat talisman head precedes *Lìchūn*, the preceding day is regarded as the *qì* throat. If the upper throat talisman head follows *Lìchūn*, one must wait until the talisman head arrives to mark the *qì* throat of spring—this is the method of "transcending the spirit and connecting the *qì*."

### **Principles of Day and Night Stars**
From *Mǎo* to *Yǒu* is daytime, during which the degree of the Life Palace serves as the primary reference, favoring the visibility of the Sun, Jupiter, and Saturn. From *Yǒu* to *Mǎo* is nighttime, during which the degree of the Body Palace serves as the primary reference, favoring the visibility of the Moon, Mars, and Venus. The Sun, Jupiter, and Saturn are all yang in nature, suitable for daytime but not for nighttime. The Moon, Mars, and Venus are all yin in nature, suitable for nighttime but not for daytime. Mercury, though yang in nature, is variable in its effects, sometimes benefiting daytime and sometimes nighttime.

### **Names of the Earthly Branches**
*Zǐ* is called *Xuányáng*, also known as the *Treasure Vessel*; *Hài* is called *Jūzī*, also known as the *Twin Fish*; *Xū* is called *Jiànglóu*, also known as the *White Sheep*; *Yǒu* is called *Dàliáng*, also known as the *Golden Ox*; *Shēn* is called *Shíchén*, also known as the *Yin-Yang*; *Wèi* is called *Chúnshǒu*, also known as the *Great Crab*; *Wǔ* is called *Chúnhuǒ*, also known as the *Lion*; *Sì* is called *Chúnwěi*, also known as the *Twin Maidens*; *Chén* is called *Shòuxīng*, also known as the *Celestial Balance*; *Mǎo* is called *Dàhuǒ*, also known as the *Celestial Scorpion*; *Yín* is called *Xīmù*, also known as the *Archer*; *Chǒu* is called *Xīngjì*, also known as the *Capricorn*.

### **Principles of the Heavenly Palaces**
Though the twelve Earthly Branches vary in width and length, the twelve Heavenly Stems are generally positioned between the Earthly Branches, with each side spanning seven and a half degrees, totaling fifteen degrees. For example, *Guǐ* lies between *Zǐ* and *Chǒu*, with seven and a half degrees allocated to *Chǒu* on the left and seven and a half degrees to *Zǐ* on the right, totaling fifteen degrees. *Rén* lies between *Hài* and *Zǐ*, with seven and a half degrees allocated to *Zǐ* on the left and seven and a half degrees to *Hài* on the right, also totaling fifteen degrees. *Zǐ* itself is divided equally on both sides, retaining only fifteen degrees. The same principle applies to the rest. Modern practitioners often incorrectly assign *Guǐ* to *Zǐ*, *Rén* to *Zǐ*, or *Rén* to *Hài*, or divide them into three and seven parts. Such methods are imbalanced and unreliable. Only the official timekeeping standards of today, which position the Heavenly Stems between the Earthly Branches, represent the correct and immutable method. The division of degrees in the Heavenly Palaces should follow this model.

### **Principles of the Sun and Moon’s Conjunctions**
In the first month, the conjunction occurs in *Hài*; in the second month, in *Xū*; in the third month, in *Yǒu*; in the fourth month, in *Shēn*; in the fifth month, in *Wèi*; in the sixth month, in *Wǔ*; in the seventh month, in *Wèi* again; in the eighth month, in *Chén*; in the ninth month, in *Mǎo*; in the tenth month, in *Yín*; in the eleventh month, in *Chǒu*; and in the twelfth month, in *Zǐ*.

To determine the moment of conjunction, one may infer it from the official calendar’s recorded time of the full moon; this is the method of establishing the *ding shuo* (exact new moon). **Principles of Day and Night Length**
At the Summer Solstice, the day is at its longest, comprising sixty *ke*; the night is at its shortest, comprising only forty *ke*. From the day following the Summer Solstice, gradual adjustments of addition and subtraction begin, roughly at a rate of one *ke* every nine days—daylight gradually decreasing by one *ke*, night gradually increasing by one *ke*. These adjustments continue until the day following the Winter Solstice, when the day is at its shortest, comprising only forty *ke*. For years governed by *Yín*, *Wǔ*, and *Xū*, observe the *Wǔ* (South) direction; for years governed by *Sì*, *Yǒu*, and *Chǒu*, observe the *Yǒu* (West) direction.

**Principles of Opposition**
This refers to the Heavenly Stems being overcome by the Earthly Branches in mutual opposition. For example:
- A person born in *Jiǎ-Zǐ* should avoid *Gēng-Wǔ*;
- A person born in *Gēng-Wǔ* should avoid *Bǐng-Zǐ*;
- A person born in *Yǐ-Chǒu* should avoid *Xīn-Wèi*;
- A person born in *Xīn-Wèi* should avoid *Dīng-Chǒu*;
- A person born in *Bǐng-Yín* should avoid *Rén-Shēn*;
- A person born in *Rén-Shēn* should avoid *Wù-Yín*, and so forth.

However, within these oppositions, there are varying degrees of severity. For instance, if a person born in *Zǐ* encounters *Wǔ*, the opposition is external, and the misfortune will be mild. If a person born in *Wǔ* encounters *Zǐ*, the opposition is internal, and the misfortune will be severe. If the Branch opposes but the Stem is not overcome, or if the Stem is overcome but the Branch does not oppose, these cases do not fall under this principle.

**Thirty-Four Essential Observations in Astrology**
1. In the method of observing the stars, first distinguish the Sun and the Moon. From the moment the Sun first appears, count to the time in question to determine the degree of the life palace. From the moment the Moon first appears, count to the time in question to determine the degree of the body palace. The degree ruler is of primary importance, while the palace ruler is secondary. The palace ruler is like a prefect, while the degree ruler is like a county magistrate—closer to the people and thus more immediate in influence. The palace ruler must encounter favorable conditions: it should be in a phase of growth, not subjected to overcoming or restraint, not obscured by the Sun, and free from stars in the three adjacent and four cardinal directions that would overcome it. Then, observe the degree ruler: it should occupy a strong position, be in the phase of *lù* (emolument), *mǎ* (horse), *chángshēng* (long life), or *suìjià* (annual chariot). It should also be in a phase of prosperity and timely, only then can it be considered excellent.

2. Once the life palace and body palace are established, further determine whether it is day or night. During the day, it is auspicious to observe the degree ruler and palace ruler of the life palace when they encounter the Sun, Jupiter, Saturn, or *jì* (calamity) as the *yòngshén* (useful spirit). Encountering the Moon, Mars, Venus, *bó* (comet), or *Luó* (Rāhu) is inauspicious. At night, it is auspicious to observe the degree ruler and palace ruler of the body palace when they encounter the Moon, Mars, Venus, *bó*, or *Luó* as the *yòngshén*. Encountering the Sun, Jupiter, Saturn, or *jì* is inauspicious.

3. In constructing a life chart, the Fortune and Virtue Palace must be examined first, for in human life, fortune and virtue are paramount. With good fortune, one may have a spouse and children, establish a reputation, and attain wealth and nobility. If the Fortune and Virtue Palace is deficient, the aforementioned benefits cannot be expected. The Fortune and Virtue Palace is the master of one’s life chart. If fortune resides in *lù*, *lù* resides in fortune, the body ruler commands life, and the life ruler enters fortune; if the Sun and Moon illuminate the Fortune and Virtue Palace, or if stars of power and nobility shine upon it, or if Venus and Mercury flank the ruler within the Fortune and Virtue Palace—such individuals are born with good fortune, their nature upright and magnanimous, blessed with both fortune and longevity. If Saturn or *jì* occupy the Fortune and Virtue Palace, one may still accumulate fortune later in life. However, if the Fortune and Virtue Palace is afflicted by stars of punishment, imprisonment, or depletion, one must not proceed. If mistakenly pursued, it will invite misfortune, and the individual will likely be harsh, treacherous, deceitful, quarrelsome, violent, disloyal, and unfilial. If the Fortune and Virtue Palace lacks favorable stars, observe the stars guarding or illuminating the three adjacent and opposite palaces. If no stars guard or illuminate these, one cannot immediately judge auspiciousness or inauspiciousness; instead, further examine the Wealth Palace.

4. The Wealth Palace is also a crucial aspect of human enjoyment. If the Fortune and Virtue Palace guards the Wealth Palace, or if the Sun and Moon illuminate the Wealth Palace, the Fields and Residences Palace, and the mutual enclosure, or if the body and life rulers reside in the Wealth Palace, and they encounter growth, prosperity, and timeliness, the individual will be born with emolument. Such a person, possessing wealth, can support a spouse and children, acquire servants, and attain both wealth and reputation. If the Wealth ruler is afflicted by punishment, imprisonment, or inauspicious stars, the individual will suffer depletion of wealth and grain, and will be foolish and useless. If the Wealth Palace is unfavorable, one cannot immediately judge auspiciousness or inauspiciousness; instead, further examine the Fields and Residences Palace.

5. The Fields and Residences Palace is also a source of human enjoyment. If the Fields and Residences ruler is not weak, or if the Fortune and Virtue Palace guards the Fields and Residences Palace, or if the Sun and Moon illuminate it, or if the body and life rulers reside in it, the individual will be born with wealth and reputation. With fields and residences, a spouse and children will naturally follow. If the Fortune and Virtue, Fields and Residences, and Wealth Palaces all lack auspicious stars, one must not proceed and should instead select another auspicious time to ensure good fortune.

6. Observe the progression of the life limit in the constructed chart. Even if a person’s life and body are auspicious, if the life limit is unfavorable, they will not prosper. A favorable life limit is more important than a favorable life chart. Only when both the life chart and life limit are auspicious can the time be considered truly auspicious. The life chart is like the soul, the body like the corporeal form, and the life limit like the *qi* and blood—all three must support one another. For example, in the human body, if the *qi* and blood are slightly obstructed, they will not flow smoothly, leading to alternating chills and fever, sores and toxins, and vulnerability to wind and pathogenic *qi*. If the *qi* and blood are harmonious and unobstructed, the body will be robust and free from illness.

7. The life limit must move toward brightness and not away from it to be auspicious. What is meant by "moving toward brightness"? At night, encountering Mars, Venus, or the Moon during the limit is considered moving toward brightness, and prosperity can be expected. During the day, encountering the Sun, Jupiter, or Saturn during the limit is also considered moving toward brightness. If the Sun is not visible, or if Jupiter and Saturn appear alone, or if Mars, the Moon, or Venus illuminate the limit, or if the limit traverses the *shā* (killing), *jié* (robbery), *yángrèn* (yang blade), *sān shā* (three killings), or *kōngwáng* (void), the individual will not prosper.

Every major and minor limit must be assessed based on the day following its inception: the *shén* (spirits), *shā* (killings), *lù* (emoluments), and *guì* (nobility) are examined. From the day after the Winter Solstice, the degrees of the life palace are traversed. Before the age of nine, the minor limit applies; at ten years old, the native’s original degree begins to progress.

In constructing a destiny, the seasonal *lìng* (commanding force) must be carefully observed. For instance, wood in spring, fire in summer, metal in autumn, and water in winter—earth, being lodged in the four seasons, also commands. The *lìng* is like a minister receiving the sovereign’s decree, proclaiming true teachings and transforming the people, upon whom their weal or woe depends. When the *lìng* presides, it must not oppose the decree; otherwise, its authority shines forth, and all gaze upon it in reverence.

When wood commands at the onset of spring, all things sprout and flourish in the season, and people likewise appear fresh and radiant. However, since wood governs the command, metal must not overcome it—metal, as the star of the season and month, should conceal its blade. Earth, then, nurtures wood. Yet if metal and earth intermingle chaotically, it is deemed rebellion and usurpation, inviting calamity. Such a configuration foretells misfortune.

In summer, when fire blazes across the sky, even if water overflows, it dare not encroach upon the fire’s monthly command. Metal, rendered impotent, cannot withstand the scorching heat—what use is it? It must be discarded. Thus, metal often traverses the southern direction in summer. If metal and fire dominate the day’s affairs, they give rise to dissolute, wine-and-women-loving individuals.

Generally, when a star that should be discarded governs the day, those born under its influence will harbor grand ambitions but lack sound strategies, their plans hollow and unrealized. Though they may appear content in life, they are easily set back. The greatest danger lies in metal and water uniting to contend with fire, forming a rebellious configuration. If such stars govern the day, misfortune and ruin are certain.

When metal commands in autumn, the great fire flows westward, abandoned by nature, and all trees wither and fall. Though it is a time when metal alone should govern, if it rebels, disaster follows. If wood and fire combine, they defy the command and grow unruly—how can this not bring misfortune?

In winter, when water is frozen and dark, it relies on fire to warm and assist its flow. Earth, as the star of embankments, poses no harm to the governing force. Yet water and wood share no principle of mutual generation; their union brings only freezing stagnation. If the water star appears at night and governs the day’s affairs, it gives rise to solitary, impoverished individuals.

Generally, when the commanding star rules in spring, it must rely on earth for cultivation. In winter, fire and earth naturally nurture one another, and even water may moisten them. Though a star may be discarded and out of season, it ultimately serves as an outer or inner counterpart. Only the fire star and the metal star of the month must never meet—if they do, harmony is impossible, and disaster strikes.

When the commanding star follows the yang, it is auspicious, serving as a supportive and harmonious star. If it rebels, it becomes inauspicious, a star of arrogance and recklessness. Thus, "following" means either drawing near the yang or aligning with it, even if ten degrees apart, without being overcome. "Rebelling" means encroaching upon the yang’s domain. The commanding star must act in accord with the Sun’s governance, occupying a position of vitality, and not opposing the life ruler. Only then does the command truly emanate from itself. If even slightly overcome, it becomes subject to another’s decree.

When the commanding star seizes control, death is certain; when it governs blessings, fortune follows. The five stars invading the palace or harming the lodges are those that overcome. Scholars must examine this carefully and not be negligent.

The Grand Year Star (*Tàisuì*) is of utmost importance. In constructing a destiny, auspicious stars must be positioned to oversee it, aligning with the Grand Year Star—only then can wealth and nobility be assured. If the Year Lord does not accept the configuration, it is of no use. One must also avoid inauspicious stars breaking the alignment.

In all matters, the body, life, and virtue are paramount. With emolument but no fortune, how can one enjoy blessings? With life but no body, to what can life be tethered?

Inauspicious stars (*shā*) should not align with their season but rather lose it—for example, metal in spring or water in winter. When inauspicious stars lose their season, the ruling star retains its vitality.

Fire as a killing star is unfavorable by day, earth by night. Wood as a killing star must be guarded against in spring, metal in autumn, water in winter, and fire in summer—these are the subtleties of seasonal alignment and misalignment.

If Venus resides in the Sun’s palace, it is called *yìngjìn* (reflected advancement). If the life ruler is positioned before the Sun’s path, it is called *tèjìn* (special advancement), also known as *jīnxīng yìngrì* (Venus reflecting the Sun). When Venus is in power, it signifies authority and nobility. Before the Sun’s path, only the body’s degree is favored. If the life ruler’s degree or Venus precedes it, both are considered noble. However, if before the Sun’s path lies punishment, imprisonment, or hidden depletion, it is called *wùjìn* (erroneous advancement), all inauspicious. If the life ruler or Venus follows the Sun’s path, it is called *hòujìn* (delayed advancement), and its fortune is halved.

When multiple stars move together, some lead and others follow. Generally, inauspicious stars should precede, while ruling stars should arrive later.
