"Eclipsing" occurs when the Sun and Moon align with *Rāhu* and *Ketu*. For those born at dawn on the new moon or at night on the full moon, an eclipse occurs if *Rāhu* or *Ketu* are present. It is inauspicious for the fate or body to reside in the degrees of the Sun or Moon.

**Clashing**
"Clashing" refers to opposition and mutual overcoming. For example, Fire in *Zi* and Water in *Wu*. Or Wood in *Chou* and Metal in *Wei*—opposing and clashing, this is inauspicious. The rest can be deduced accordingly.

**Restraining**
"Restraining" means a governing star is subdued. For example, if Wood is the governing star but is restrained by Metal. Or if Earth is the governing power but is restrained by Wood. Governing stars include those of the body, fate, official blessings, fields, wealth, spouses, and heirs. Being overcome by other stars is inauspicious.

**Facing**
"Facing" means mutual orientation. For example, if stars converge in the south and the fate resides there. Or if stars face north and the fate is in the north. If *Ketu* and *Rāhu* intercept stars in the east and the fate is in the east, or intercept stars in the west and the fate is in the west—all are called "facing."

**Encompassing**
"Encompassing" means a triple conjunction. For example, the Sun and Moon encompassing the body or fate, official blessings, fields, wealth, spouses, or children. Or emolument and fortune stars encompassing the body, fate, official blessings, fields, wealth, spouses, or children. Or fields and wealth encompassing the body, fate, official blessings, spouses, or children.

**Flanking**
"Flanking" means being on both sides. For example, the Sun and Moon flanking the body, fate, official blessings, fields, wealth, spouses, or children. Or emolument and fortune stars flanking the body, fate, official blessings, fields, wealth, spouses, or children. Or fields and wealth flanking the body, fate, official blessings, spouses, or children.

**Assisting**
"Assisting" means auxiliary support. For example, the body or fate star assisting the Sun and Moon before or after. Or the official blessing star assisting the Sun and Moon on the left or right. Or fields, wealth, spouses, and heirs being lifted by the Sun and Moon—all are considered proper configurations.

**Dividing**
"Dividing" means *Rāhu* and *Ketu* intercepting stars into two paths. They may separate civil and martial stars into two groups, or divide them into civil stars in the east and martial stars in the west. They may also separate the Sun and Moon into parallel brightness or isolate official blessings into clarity and vigor.

**Convergence**
"Convergence" means all stars gathering in a single palace or degree. For instance, the Eleven Luminaries converging upon the body or fate. Or the Eleven Luminaries converging upon official blessings, or upon fields and wealth. When all stars proceed in harmony, mutually generating without conflict or contention, it is most auspicious.

**Leading**
"Leading" means preceding. A leading star should be at a distant degree. For example, the Sun and Moon leading the way, or official blessings leading, or fields and wealth leading, or spouses and heirs leading. Or the Civil Chief leading, the First-in-Scholarship leading, or official seals leading—those that occupy favorable positions are excellent.

**Following**
"Following" means trailing. A following star should be at a near degree. For instance, Metal and Water leading and following: Water should precede, Metal should follow. Wood and Fire leading and following: Fire should precede, Wood should follow. Earth and Metal leading and following: Metal should precede, Earth should follow. This is because the latter can generate the former, while the former cannot generate the latter.

**Interception**
"Interception" means *Rāhu* and *Ketu* blocking the path. They may intercept all stars in the southeast, especially for those born during the day. Or they may intercept the multitude of luminaries in the northwest, particularly for those born at night. Or, when *Rāhu* and *Ketu* divide the center, they may intercept civil stars in the east and martial stars in the west.

**Leakage**
"Leakage" means intercepting and releasing auspicious stars. For those born during the day, leakage may release the Sun, Jupiter, Saturn, Mercury, *Ketu*, and *Rāhu* in yang palaces and yang degrees. For those born at night, leakage may release the Moon, Mars, *Rāhu*, and Venus in yin palaces and yin degrees.

**Residence**
"Residence" means the body or fate dwelling in a palace. The palace of residence, in conjunction with the converging stars, determines nobility or baseness.

**Divergence**
"Divergence" means two paths separated by a boundary. For example, the second degree of *Wei* in *Mao*, while the third degree of *Wei* passes into *Yin*—this is a divergence where the same degree spans two palaces. Or the ninth degree of *Xu* in *Zi* and the first degree of *Wei*—this is a divergence where the same palace spans two degrees. In all cases, the body, fate, official blessings, and other stars should occupy degrees deeply, avoiding entanglement in divergent boundaries.

The above eight methods of initiation and observation form the essential framework of astrological study, serving as the model for scholars. One should ponder them carefully to grasp their essence.

*Confucian School, Revering Principle and Balancing the Mean, "Kanyu Perfection of Filial Piety Record," Scroll Seven, concludes.*

*Scroll Eight of "Confucian School, Revering Principle and Balancing the Mean, Kanyu Perfection of Filial Piety Record":*
*Preface to the Simplified Rites of Burial*

It is often said that the noble person does not economize for the world at the expense of their parents—how then could one not bury them with utmost generosity? Yet the earth’s hidden veins are but a single thread, the marvelous mechanism of creation itself. In this age, officials and great families often indulge in lavish adornment to dazzle the eyes of the living, even going so far as to carve and damage the true *qi* of creation—how deplorable this is!

Here, I have examined and recorded several maxims on burial rites from the ancient worthies, that benevolent and filial descendants may find guidance in them.

**On the Coffin**
Master Zhu said: Oil cedar is best, followed by *xiang* wood, then earth cedar. Its construction should be square and straight, with a broad head and narrow foot, just sufficient to contain the body—avoid making it tall or large. Do not fashion hollow eaves or high legs. Both inside and out, apply a coating of ash lacquer. On the bottom, spread a layer of charcoal powder one or two *cun* thick, then place the Seven Stars board atop it. For the mixture of *lin* rice, oil, ash, and pitch (also called pine resin, pine rosin, or "cloud incense"), nails and metal fittings are unnecessary. What follows does not reflect Master Zhu’s intent.

Hu Yong of the Hu clan said: Peng Zhitang, in his *Instructions for the Young*, stated: "Sealing with pine resin is suitable for the north; in the south, it becomes a nest for ants." Moreover, nails and metal fittings may draw moisture, causing the wood to rot more easily. Peng must have further investigated this.

Liu Zhang of the Liu clan said: Among all rites for sending off the dead, only the coffin and the *ning* (inner coffin frame) are objects intimately connected to the body—what the filial son should devote his utmost care to. On the day of mourning, when selecting wood for the coffin, one may fear that in haste the right timber cannot be found, or that the ash lacquer may not yet be firm and complete. If it is a summer month, the body cannot be kept long. In ancient times, when a ruler ascended the throne, he would prepare his *bei* (outer coffin) and *guo* (outer casket), applying a fresh coat of lacquer each year. Today, some people prepare their own "longevity vessels" while still alive—this is still following the Way, not prematurely courting misfortune. The best woods are oil cedar and *xiang* wood; do not make the coffin tall or large merely for ostentatious beauty. The coffin need only encase the body, and the *ning* need only encase the coffin’s base. Both inside and out, wrap the coffin in cloth and apply lacquer to ensure firmness and solidity.

Note: The Seven Stars board inside the coffin is made from a single plank, its length and width sufficient to fit within the coffin, with seven holes carved into it.

**On Burial**
Master Zhu said: Burial should take place within three months; beforehand, select a suitable site. Master Cheng said: "Divining the grave site is to assess the quality of the land—its goodness or badness—not what the yin-yang practitioners call fortune or misfortune. Where the land is good, the spirits rest in peace, and their descendants thrive. This is like nourishing the roots so that the branches and leaves flourish—it is only natural. Where the land is bad, the opposite occurs. How then do we define a good site? The signs are the luster of the soil and the luxuriance of the grass and trees. Father and ancestors share the same *qi* with their descendants: if the former are at peace, so are the latter; if the former are in peril, so are the latter—this too is natural. Those who are bound by taboos, deluded by the selection of directional orientations or the determination of auspicious days, are indeed rigid. Worse still are those who do not consider honoring their ancestors but focus solely on benefiting their descendants—this is not the mindset of a filial son ensuring a proper resting place. Only the Five Hazards must be carefully avoided: ensure that the site will not later become a road, a city wall, a ditch or pond, be seized by the powerful, or be encroached upon by farmers."

Another version states: "The so-called Five Hazards are ditches, roads, avoiding villages, and keeping far from wells and cellars."

Qiu Jun, Duke Wenzhuang, said: "The theories of *fengshui* (wind and water), with their hopes for great wealth and nobility, cannot be trusted. However, aligning with the *qi* of life to secure the remains of ancestors does accord with Master Yichuan’s analogy of roots, branches, and leaves. Former scholars often adopted this view. Master Zhu and Cai Jitong once pre-selected a burial site, and when the time came, his disciples carried provisions and followed the funeral procession for six days before arriving—such was their careful selection."

Master Zhu, in discussing the selection of burial sites, said one must first assess the strength of the land’s dominant *qi*, the convergence or dispersion of its winds, the depth of its soil, the alignment of the burial path, and whether its vitality is complete. Only then can the quality of the land be properly judged. Those who choose burial sites today should follow Master Zhu’s principles, incorporating Master Yichuan’s signs of luster and luxuriance, and guarding against the Five Hazards—this should suffice.

**Selecting the Day to Open the Burial Ground**
According to the *Family Rituals*, the master leads the officiants to the chosen site to dig the grave pit. The soil from the four corners is placed outside, while the soil from the center and the south is placed to the south. Erect a marker at each of the four corners outside the pit, and two markers at the southern gate. "Placing the soil from the four corners outside" means removing the soil beyond the pit’s edges. "Placing the soil from the center and south to the south" means depositing that soil to the south.

**Sacrificing to the Earth Deity**
The *Family Rituals* states: Sacrificing to the Earth Deity is to prevent presumption. Select a distant relative or a guest, who wears auspicious clothing and a plain cap, to make the announcement to the Earth Deity. The invocator leads the officiants to set up an altar to the left of the central marker, facing south. A cup is placed there, filled with wine, and dried meats and fermented grains are arranged before it. A washing basin and two hand towels are set to the east and west—one for the announcer to wash, the other for the officiants.

**Ritual Procedures**
At the altar, the announcer stands facing north, with two officiants behind. They bow twice, rise, bow twice again, then stand upright. The announcer and officiants all rise. They wash their hands: the announcer and officiants all perform the ablution. The announcer approaches the incense table and kneels. One officiant, holding the wine vessel, kneels facing west; another, holding the cup, kneels facing east. The announcer takes the vessel, pours wine into the cup, returns the vessel, and takes the cup. He pours the wine onto the ground as a libation, then refills the cup and places it before the deity’s tablet. He bows deeply, rises, and steps back slightly. The invocator, holding the prayer tablet, kneels to the announcer’s left and reads the prayer. The prayer and paper money are burned together. They bow twice, rise, bow twice again, then stand upright. The ritual is complete.

**Prayer Text**
In the such-and-such year (cyclical signs), such-and-such month (cyclical signs), on the such-and-such day (cyclical sign), so-and-so, of such-and-such office and surname, respectfully announces to the Earth Deity of this mountain: Now, for my father (state the father’s official title and name) or mother (state the mother’s surname and title), I am establishing a grave site on this mountain, facing such-and-such direction. May the deity protect and bless us, that we encounter no future hardships. With pure wine and dried meats, I reverently offer this to the deity. May you deign to accept it.

Note: Ancient rituals did not include the Earth Deity as such; only the *Kaiyuan Rituals* of the Tang Dynasty did. The *Book of Rites* by Sima Guang and the *Family Rituals* by Master Zhu both follow the *Kaiyuan Rituals* in including sacrifices to the Earth Deity when opening the burial ground, interring the coffin, and offering tomb sacrifices. The title "Earth Deity" is paired with "August Heaven." Only the state may use this term; for common families, it may seem presumptuous. However, in the *Complete Works of Master Zhu*, there is a prayer text for the Earth Deity.

Here follows the proposed revision: henceforth, "Earth Deity" shall be styled "Spirit of the Local Earth."

The ritual implements and tables are then removed, and the grave pit is excavated. *Removing* refers to clearing away the sacrificial altar and its furnishings. *Excavating the grave pit*: Sima Wengong states that modern burials employ two methods—one digs straight down to form the pit and suspends the coffin for placement; the other carves a lateral tunnel to create a side chamber where the coffin is interred. According to ancient rites, only the Son of Heaven may construct a tunnel; all others must dig straight down to form the pit and suspend the coffin for placement. This method should be followed today. The excavation ought to be narrow and deep: narrow to prevent collapse, deep to deter grave robbers. *Note*: Deep burial to prevent theft is indeed a sound principle of the former worthies. However, geomancers also speak of shallow burial to harness natural *qi* and water veins, arguing that dragon veins rise and fall, and soil depth varies. As Master Zhu observed in Zhangzhou and Quanzhou, coffins buried only halfway, with earth mounded above, may encounter water if sunk too deep. Thus, burial depth cannot be uniformly prescribed. Moreover, before the Song dynasty, tombs often contained gold and treasures, inviting grave robbery. The present dynasty’s ritual codes forbid burial with gold or valuables, eliminating the threat of theft. This prohibition is most proper. In Wu region, even the deceased’s clothing is pre-cut to prevent theft. Yet true filial devotion lies in selecting an auspicious site to secure the departed, not in burying gold and jewels. Such practices do not honor the dead but invite grave robbery—a grievous folly.

**Constructing the Charcoal Barrier**
Once the pit is dug, first spread a layer of charcoal powder on the bottom, tamping it firmly to a thickness of two or three *cun*. Then lay a mixture of lime, fine sand, and yellow earth—three parts lime to one part each of the others—on top, tamping it firmly to a depth of two or three *chi*. Construct a thin wooden frame, shaped like a table, to serve as a charcoal barrier, leaving space in the center to accommodate the coffin’s walls, rising four *cun* above the coffin. Place this on the lime mixture, then fill the surrounding space with the four materials, separated by thin wooden partitions: charcoal powder on the outside, the other three on the inside. Once the base is tamped firm, gradually withdraw the partitions, adding more charcoal and lime mixture above, tamping until level with the coffin’s sides. Charcoal repels tree roots and deters moisture and termites; lime, mixed with sand, hardens, and with earth, binds tightly. Over time, it solidifies into stone, barring entry to insects, thieves, and burrowing creatures. Master Cheng said: "The ancients’ burials sought to preserve the transformed, ensuring earth did not touch the flesh. If we take such care to protect mere curios, how much more should we safeguard the remains of our kin? Yet the shallow-minded merely wish the dead to remain unseen. Others advocate hastening decomposition, ignorant of the principles of sincerity and trust. The goal is not to prevent decay but to ensure secure, airtight preservation."

**Mixing Lime and Sand**
Fill the outer coffin (*guo*) with lime and sand, lining the pit with charcoal powder. Master Zhu, in a letter to Liao Zihui, wrote: "Later refinements like wooden outer coffins sealed with pitch seem unnecessary. Instead, first lay charcoal powder on the pit’s bottom, tamping it to a thickness of two *cun*, connecting it to the previously laid layer. Once level, place the outer coffin, then fill its sides with the three materials as before." *Note*: The outer coffin is not strictly necessary. If used, a wooden or brick *guo* is best; stone *guo* should never be used. Stone generates moisture—observe how pillars grow damp before rain—and is excessively heavy. Over centuries, if it collapses, it may crush the remains, a grievous outcome. Thus, it is better omitted.

**Filling the Outer Coffin with Lime and Sand**
Fill the bottom of the *guo* and the space around the coffin (both inside and outside the *guo*) with sand and lime. Once full, add a lid, then spread more sand and lime, topped with a *cun*-thick layer of charcoal powder. Finally, fill the pit with earth. The lime and sand deter insects and burrowers; the thicker the layer, the better. *Question*: May sand and earth be mixed with lime around the coffin? Master Zhu replied: "Use pure charcoal powder outside the *guo*; inside, fill with a mixture of sand and lime." *Question*: May pure lime be used? *Answer*: "Pure lime may not harden properly; it must be mixed with sifted fine sand. Over time, the lime and sand will fuse into a solid mass." For stone *guo*, line all sides—top, bottom, and around—with charcoal powder, about seven or eight *cun* thick. This repels moisture, prevents water damage, and blocks tree roots, which turn away upon encountering charcoal. Thus, the efficacy of charcoal and lime is evident: charcoal, being inert, deters roots. *Baopuzi* states: "Charcoal buried underground remains unchanged for a thousand years." Ritual objects made of organic materials decay over time, attracting insects—hardly a consideration for the long-term welfare of the deceased. While ancient tombs contained many items, their ritual completeness often neglected practical safeguards. The essential concern is long-term preservation, ensuring earth does not touch the flesh. Other ritual details may be omitted. I once heard Master Wang of Jixi recount that a tomb using lime was later exhumed, revealing the lime had turned to stone. Charcoal powder blocks external tree roots, as observed in local reburials. Thus, the lime-sand mixture must be applied without gaps around all sides to ensure durability.

The above three passages are all from Master Zhu.

Take three parts lime, one part yellow earth, and one part fine sand, sift them evenly, then sprinkle with diluted wine using a spray pot. Tamp firmly.

**Note:** Common practice today mixes glutinous rice porridge into the three-part soil, which also bonds the materials securely—this may be used as well.

**Inscribed Tombstone:**
Use two stone slabs: one as a lid, inscribed with: *"Tomb of [Official Title] [Name]."* If the deceased held no office, inscribe: *"Tomb of Gentleman [Given Name] [Courtesy Name]."* The second slab serves as the base, inscribed with: *"[Official Title] [Taboo Name], styled [Courtesy Name], of [Prefecture] [County], son of [Father’s Taboo Name] and [Mother’s Surname] [Mother’s Title], born on [Year, Month, Day], held offices in succession [list], died on [Year, Month, Day], interred at [Village] [Hamlet] [Mountain] facing [Direction]. Married [Wife’s Surname], daughter of [Her Father’s Name]. Sons: [Names and Titles]. Daughters: married to [Husbands’ Names and Titles]. Grandsons: [Number and Names]. Granddaughters: [Number and Names]."*

For women: if the husband is alive, the lid reads: *"Tomb of [Husband’s Official Title and Name], [Her Title] née [Surname]."* If unenfeoffed, it reads: *"Tomb of [Husband’s Name]."* If widowed: *"Wife of Gentleman [Husband’s Given Name] [Courtesy Name], née [Surname]."* The base records her birth and death dates; if her title derives from her husband or children, include it—otherwise, omit.

On the day of burial, place the two stones face-to-face, bind them with iron chains, and bury them at the tomb’s front, three to four *chi* below ground level. This precaution accounts for future shifts in terrain or accidental disturbance; if the stone is uncovered first, someone may recognize the name and rebury it.

**Note:** The Fujian custom of placing this stone inside the tomb at the head of the coffin is inappropriate. The Zhejiang practice of placing it at the tomb’s front is better, though burying it three to four *chi* below ground is most proper.

**Lowering the Coffin:**
First, lay wooden beams atop the lime barrier. Use four ropes threaded through the coffin’s bottom rings (if present), lower the coffin onto the beams, then withdraw the ropes. Alternatively, fold fine cloth or raw silk to cradle the coffin’s base, lower it, and leave the cloth in place, trimming any excess. If the coffin lacks rings, loop ropes beneath it, lower it onto the beams, remove the ropes, and proceed with cloth as above.

When lowering the coffin, ensure steady force to avoid tilting or jarring. The chief mourner and brothers should wail and oversee the process. After lowering, adjust the coffin’s clothing, *ming* tablet, and funeral banner to ensure they lie flat.

**Question:** If the coffin lacks rings, removing the ropes may be difficult—hence this method. But if rings are present, why not lower the coffin directly with ropes, then switch to cloth at the beams?
**Answer:** Likely to avoid rope removal difficulties. Modern practice uses slipknots at both ends for stable lowering—this is also acceptable.

Place bricks at the tomb’s four corners to support the coffin, facilitating rope removal after lowering.

**Applying the Inner and Outer Lime Barriers:**
Measure the lime barrier’s dimensions and craft a thin board to fit snugly against the four walls. Place it atop the coffin, seal gaps with oil-lime, then add the outer barrier.

**Note:** The original method used molten pitch, poured to a thickness of three *cun* before adding the outer barrier. Today, stone slabs may replace pitch. Some modern practices layer rice porridge mixed with lime in ceramic bowls—this is also sturdy and acceptable.

**Filling with Lime:**
Spread the three-part lime mixture evenly on top, followed by a thicker layer of charcoal powder—twice the depth of the base and outer/inner coffin sides. Sprinkle with wine and tread firmly, avoiding tamping to prevent jarring the coffin. Use ample material to ensure compaction over time.

**Filling and Tamping Earth:**
Add earth in one-*chi* layers, tamping lightly to avoid disturbing the coffin. Afterward, offer sacrifices to the Earth God at the tomb’s left.

**Prayer Text:**
*"On the [Day] of the [Month], [Year], [Your Name and Title], humbly announce to the Earth God of this mountain: For [Official Title] [Name], [Posthumous Title], now laid to rest in this secluded abode, may the god protect and bless, sparing future hardship. With reverence, we offer pure libations, dried meats, and delicacies—may the god deign to accept."*

Return to your position. The announcer bows twice, then withdraws.

**Note:** Liu Zhang states: *"The deceased’s form rests here, so we honor the god to ensure peace."*

**Interring Grave Goods:**
When the earth reaches halfway, place *mingqi* (spirit objects), curtains, *bao* bundles, *shao* baskets, and jars in the side chamber.

**Note:** *Mingqi* include wooden carvings of carriages, horses, servants, and maidservants holding daily-use items—towels, bedding, etc.—miniaturized to resemble life. Officials of the fifth or sixth rank may include thirty items; seventh or eighth rank, twenty; non-court officials, fifteen; commoners, ten.

*Curtains* refer to bed canopies, mats, chairs, and tables, also miniaturized. *Bao* is a bamboo mat holding leftover sacrificial meats.

**Note:** *Yili* annotates: *"Bao* is grass; ancient *baoju* refers to this."* *Liji* annotates: *"Bao* wraps fish and meat; *ju* lines vessels for storage."*

*Shao* are five bamboo containers for the five grains.

Sima Wengong states: *"Today, small jars holding five *sheng* of each grain suffice."*

Liu Zhang states: *"The* Jili *records three* lü *vessels, like* gui*, holding millet, sorghum, and wheat—all steamed, as the gods do not consume food, but the Dao values reverence."*

**Note:** *Yili* annotates: *"Shao* are bamboo containers for food, like* gui*."*

The *Lunyu* annotates: *"Shao* are bamboo vessels holding one *dou* and two *sheng*." Three ceramic jars are used to hold wine, vinegar, and fermented sauces.

**Note:** These items are commonly called "granary and treasury." Today, they are no longer used, though in Fujian, two ceramic jars are employed—one holding the five grains, called the *granary*; the other holding copper coins or paper money, called the *treasury*. In my homeland, neither is used.

Zhang Yue states: "No jars or bottles are placed in the tomb, as they are akin to water. No feathers are placed, as they are akin to the corpse. No gold is placed, as it endures and becomes uncanny. No cinnabar, realgar, or alum are placed, as their fierce heat dries the earth, preventing it from nourishing growth. If ancient *mingqi* (spirit articles) are interred in the tomb, these objects will inevitably breed insects over time. If one insists on using them, it is better to dig a separate pit beside the burial chamber to contain them."

Sima Wengong states: "From *mingqi* downward, once the earth filling reaches halfway, a side chamber is excavated beside the burial pit to store them, its entrance sealed with a plank."

Liu Shi states: "Someone asked about the meaning of *mingqi*. The *Dangong* annotation says: 'It refers to sending off the dead with ritual, treating them with the rites due to the deceased.' To lack a heart that cherishes one’s kin is unfilial and cannot be done. To treat them with the rites of the living is to lack the clarity of principle, which is unwise and also cannot be done. Thus, objects are prepared but cannot be used. They are called *mingqi* because they are treated with the Way of the spiritually enlightened."

Someone asked: "Digging a side chamber may inconvenience the drainage of the empty burial pit. Would it be better to store them where the epitaph is buried?" The reply: "Though this reflects the ancient reluctance to treat one’s kin as truly dead, these objects are in fact useless. Moreover, dried meats decay and breed insects, attracting ants—this is especially unsuitable and should not be done. Only *mingqi* should be buried."

**Note:** Digging side chambers to bury *mingqi*—such as Fujian’s wooden carvings of golden roosters, jade dogs, cypress figures, and earth gods—may disrupt the earth’s veins and invite water, breeding insects and causing further decay. It is better to omit them entirely. If a filial descendant insists on following ritual, there is no need for intricate carvings; paper models may suffice. After the interment is complete and the earth is tamped, during the rite of thanking the earth and announcing the tomb’s completion, these paper models may be burned before the grave, following the principle of burning paper money. This is both convenient and appropriate. May this be noted by the benevolent and filial.

**Placing the Epitaph Stone**
If the tomb is on level ground, first lay a single layer of bricks in the southern part of the tomb path, place the stone atop them, surround it with bricks, and cover it. If the tomb is on a steep mountainside, dig a pit four to five *chi* deep several *chi* south of the burial chamber, bury the stone according to the method, then refill and tamp the earth firmly. The depth of the refilled earth should be about one *chi*, but it must be densely tamped and solidly packed.

**Note on Construction Methods**
Common practice employs brick vaults, which must be built tall and spacious, yet over time they inevitably collapse and sink—this is most improper. It is fitting instead to use ample *sanhetu* (three-part soil mixture) and tamp it firmly.

**Inscribing the Spirit Tablet**
The officiant arranges a table before the bier, facing left, with inkstone, brush, and ink placed to the right, and a washing basin with towel nearby.

**Ritual Procedure**
The master of ceremonies stands before the table. The invocator and the inscriber both perform ablutions.

**Presenting the Tablet**
The invocator opens the box, removes the wooden tablet, and lays it flat upon the table. After washing his hands, the inscriber stands to the right.

**Inscribing the Tablet**
The central characters of the tablet should first be written by another person, leaving only the blank surface for the final touch. For the character *zhu* (主) on the spirit tablet, omit the dot, leaving it as *wang* (王). The inscriber then adds the single dot above, completing the character *zhu*—this is called "inscribing the tablet."

**Placing the Tablet**
The invocator reverently installs the tablet upon the bier. The *hunpo* silk is then stored in the box behind the tablet.

**Offering Incense and Libations**
The invocator lights incense, pours wine, and kneels; the master of ceremonies and all others kneel as well.

**Reciting the Invocation**
The invocator reads the prayer but does not burn it.

**Concluding the Rite**
All rise and return to their positions. They bow, then perform four prostrations, rising after each. The master of ceremonies expresses gratitude to the inscriber with two bows; the inscriber responds with a single bow.

**Note on the Inscription Method**
Why are the central characters of the tablet written in advance by another, leaving only the final dot for the inscriber? It is because the one who performs the "dot inscription" is often a person of high rank or advanced age, for whom writing at the moment may be inconvenient. Thus, this is a matter of practical adaptation. The Confucian ritual classics do not address this practice.

**Form for Inscribing the Tablet**

**Inscription for the Tablet’s Central Characters**

For a father:
*"Ming gu* [Deceased with honor] So-and-so, [posthumous] official title So-and-so, personal name So-and-so, courtesy name So-and-so, seniority order [number]—spirit tablet."*

For a mother:
*"Ming gu* [Deceased with honor] Lady So-and-so of [her husband’s] noble rank So-and-so, personal name So-and-so, seniority order [number]—spirit tablet."*

For the tablet’s powdered face:
For a father:
*"Xian kao* [Illustrious deceased father] So-and-so, [posthumous] official title and honorific appellation—spirit tablet."*

For a mother:
*"Xian bi* [Illustrious deceased mother] Lady So-and-so of [her husband’s] noble rank—spirit tablet."*

On the lower left side of the tablet, inscribe:
*"Filial son So-and-so offers sacrifice."*

If the deceased held no official rank, use the title by which they were commonly known. For example, for a father:
*"Xian kao* [Illustrious deceased father] Gentleman of Leisure—spirit tablet."*

---

**Prayer Text**
*"In the year [X], cyclical year [X], month [X], first day [X], at the hour [X], orphaned son So-and-so dares to solemnly announce to [posthumous] official title and honorific appellation So-and-so: Your form has returned to the grave, your spirit to the ancestral hall. Now that the spirit tablet is complete, I humbly beseech your august soul to embrace the old and follow the new, to rely upon and abide by this tablet."*

For a mother, replace *"orphaned son"* with *"mourning son."*

*Notes on* **zhūnxī** *(窀穸):*
As recorded in *The Zuo Tradition* (*Zuo Zhuan*), Duke Xiang 13th year: When King Gong of Chu fell gravely ill, he gazed at his ministers and said, *"I shall preserve my head and limbs only to be buried in the earth."* This refers to the *Spring and Autumn* practice of interment. The commentary explains: *Zhūn* (窀), pronounced *zhānglún*, means "thick"; *xī* (穸), pronounced like *xī* (night), means "night." Thus, *zhūnxī* signifies the long night of burial.

---

**Procession with the Spirit Tablet**
The prayer is recited as the spirit tablet is placed in the carriage, with the soul banner box following behind. In modern custom, the master of the household personally cradles the tablet in his arms while riding in the sedan chair, ensuring the spirit remains close. This, too, has its rationale.

The attendants dismantle the spirit seat and proceed. The mourners follow, men on the left and women on the right, with those in heavier mourning leading and those in lighter mourning behind. Upon leaving the burial site, elders mount carriages or horses and depart, maintaining a distance of roughly a hundred paces from the grave. Younger mourners also ride carriages or horses, or boats if traveling by water. Only one junior descendant remains to oversee the completion of the grave’s earthworks.

*Query:* After the tablet is inscribed, why does the filial son carry it home, entrusting the sealing of the grave and mound to others?
*Response:* One serves the dead as one serves the living. The deceased’s remains are interred in the earth, while the spirit attaches to the tablet. Thus, the filial son prioritizes escorting the tablet to settle the spirit. The sealing of the grave and mound may be delegated to close relatives, disciples, or others. When Confucius attended a burial, he entrusted the sealing to his disciples. During a heavy rain that threatened to collapse the grave, Zigong returned late and informed Confucius, who wept bitterly and rebuked his disciples: *"In ancient times, graves were not repaired."* By this, he meant that graves were originally built sturdy and solid, requiring no later repairs—not that collapse should go unaddressed. The modern practice of returning to the grave three days later to complete the mound stems from this principle.

---

**Constructing the Grave Mound**
The mound should stand four *chi* high. As recorded in *Tan Gong* (*Book of Rites*), after Confucius was interred alongside his wife at Fang, he remarked: *"I have heard that in ancient times, graves had no mounds. But I am a wanderer from all directions—how could I not mark the site?"* Thus, he ordered a mound four *chi* high. This serves as the model.

A small stone stele is erected at the front, also four *chi* high, with a base (*fu*) approximately one *chi* tall.

Sima Wengong [Sima Guang] observed: *"According to modern regulations, the size and number of grave steles, stone beasts, and other elements vary by rank. Yet those who bury the dead should consider long-term consequences. How can future generations know whether these objects do not conceal gold or jade? They bring no benefit to the deceased and may even cause harm. The regulations further stipulate that the noble may emulate the humble, but the humble may not emulate the noble. For those with foresight, even within their rightful entitlements, it is best to reduce excess where possible."*

*Further note on Sima Wengong’s proposal:*
A separate small stele of white stone, at least one *chi* in diameter, should be erected. The rear two-thirds of the stele should be shaped like a *gui* tablet, with its face resembling the cover of a tomb inscription. On the left side, briefly record the deceased’s lineage, name, and deeds, continuing the inscription around to the right side. For women, the stele is erected only after their husband’s burial, with the face matching the cover of his tomb inscription.

*Ming dynasty regulations on burial grounds:*
- For first-rank officials: 90 *bu* (paces) of cemetery land, decreasing by 10 *bu* per rank; seventh-rank and below may not exceed 30 *bu*; commoners are limited to 9 *bu*.
- For first-rank graves: 1 *zhang* 8 *chi* high, decreasing by 2 *chi* per rank; seventh-rank and below may not exceed 6 *chi*.
- Stone steles: First-rank officials use *chī*-headed steles with tortoise bases; second-rank, *qílín*; third-rank, *tiānlù*; fourth- to seventh-rank, round-headed steles with square bases. The size of stone figures and beasts decreases proportionally by rank. The arrangement of stone figures, beasts, and pillar markers follows statutory regulations.
- The noble may emulate the humble, but the humble—regardless of wealth—may not emulate the noble. Those with foresight should, within their rightful entitlements, reduce excess where possible.

Sima Wengong further remarked: *"In ancient times, those of great merit and virtue had their names inscribed on bronze vessels and preserved in ancestral temples. Their burials included only a tall stele to lower the coffin. From the Qin and Han dynasties onward, scholars began composing eulogies to praise the deceased’s merits, carving them onto stone—hence the term 'stele.' By the Southern Dynasties, epitaphs were also buried in graves. If the deceased was truly virtuous, their fame would resound through the ages, their praises sung for eternity—how could a stele or epitaph add to their renown? If the deceased was unworthy, even the most artful words and lavish praise—claiming their merits rivaled Lü Wang’s or their virtue Confucius’s—would only invite ridicule. Who would believe it? Though a stele stands by the grave path and may be seen, an epitaph buried in the tomb remains unseen unless the grave is opened. When Emperor Wen of Sui’s four-year-old son died, his officials requested a stele. The emperor replied: 'If one seeks fame, a single volume in the historical records suffices. What need is there for a stele? It would merely serve as a weight for others.’"*

This is indeed a truthful statement. Now, since we cannot avoid following the wishes of the deceased, their epitaph should simply record their native place, family lineage, official positions, and life’s beginning and end—nothing more.

Before the tomb of Jizha stands a stone, which the world claims was inscribed by Confucius. Alas! For one such as Lord Yanling Jizi, does his virtue require many words before later generations recognize his worth? Today, it suffices to carve his name before the tomb, and people will naturally know him.

**Appendix: Epitaph for the Tomb of My Late Father**

My late father’s personal name was Song, his courtesy name Qiaonian, and his surname Zhu. He was a native of Ziyuan in Huizhou. His great-grandfather’s personal name was Zhen, his grandfather’s Xun, and both their wives were née Wang. His father’s personal name was Sen, and his mother was née Cheng. Three generations held no official posts. My father and mother were posthumously honored as *Chengshilang* and *Ruren* on account of my father.

My father was born on the *wushen* day of the intercalary second month in the fourth year of Shaosheng (1097). By nature, he was profoundly filial, possessed of lofty aspirations and great integrity, and his written words never failed to astonish. In the eighth year of Zhenghe (1118), he passed the *shangshe* examination and was appointed *Digonglang* and magistrate of Zhenghe County in Jianzhou.

When his father, *Chengshi* Gong, passed away, my father was too poor to return home, so he buried him in that county. Thereafter, he traveled for official duties in Fujian, where he first studied the teachings of *The Great Learning* and *The Doctrine of the Mean* under disciples of Yang Guishan. He was later transferred to serve as magistrate of Youxi County in Nan Jianzhou, then as tax supervisor of Shijing Town in Quanzhou, and was promoted to *Zuocongzhenglang*.

In the fourth year of Shaoxing (1134), he was summoned for an imperial examination and appointed *Zhengzi* of the Palace Library. After mourning the death of his mother, he was promoted to *Xuanjiaolang* upon audience with the emperor, then to *Zhuozuo Zuolang*, *Shangshu Du[...] Yuanwailang*, and concurrently *Jiaokan* of the Historiography Institute. He successively served in the Ministry of Personnel’s *Sikun* and *Libu* Bureaus, retaining his concurrent historiographical duties. For his service in compiling histories, he was promoted to *Zouyilang*, and for seniority, to *Chengyilang*.

Prime Ministers Zhao, the Loyal and Simple Duke, and Zhang, the Loyal and Solemn Duke, both esteemed and knew my father, but he was not employed before their departures. Qin Kuai grew resentful of him for this, and when my father led his colleagues in vehemently opposing the peace negotiations with the Rong, Qin’s anger deepened. My father was appointed prefect of Raozhou but did not assume the post. Instead, he requested to serve as *Zhuguan* of Chongdao Abbey in Taizhou. On the *xinhai* day of the third month in the thirteenth year (1143), he passed away in his lodging south of Jianzhou city at the age of forty-seven.

His writings include *The Weizhai Collection* in twelve volumes. He married the daughter of the *chushi* Que of the same prefecture, née Zhu, who was later enfeoffed as *Ruren*. She passed away twenty-seven years after him. His son, Xi, once served as *Zuodigonglang* and was assigned as compiler in the Bureau of Military Affairs. His daughter married *You Digonglang* and magistrate of Changting County, Liu Zixiang. His grandsons, Shu, Ye, and Zai, and granddaughters, Xun and Dui, were all young.

Initially, my father wished to be buried in Wufu of Chong’an. After his death, he was interred the following year beside Lingfan Abbey in his hometown. At the time, I was young and inexperienced in worldly affairs, and the divined burial site was deemed unsuitable. Fearing his remains would not rest in peace, I had him reinterred on the fifth day of the eighth month in the sixth year of Qiandao (1170) beneath Ezifeng of Baishui in our village.

I weep and lament, my grief piercing my heart and bones. Reflecting deeply, my father did not live to see his aspirations fulfilled, and I, his son, have failed to emulate his virtues—it seems I cannot honor him even to a fraction of his worth. I dare only record his surname, lineage, official positions, and the outline of his ambitions. Carving this and burying it in the dark, I shall also request an inscription to mark his tomb. The boundless grace of Heaven—alas, my sorrow!
